-65-
Annex 2
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
LESSONS LEARNED FROM TAMIL NADU WOMEN'S
DEVELOPMENT PROJECT
A. Project Background
1.          The Tamil Nadu Women's Development Project commenced its implementation
in 1989. The overall responsibility for project implementation lies with MAHAM (Tamil Nadu
Women's Development Corporation), which is a wholly owned Government of Tamil Nadu
undertaking. The principal objective of the project is the economic and social upliftment of
women to enhance the welfare of their families and to improve their status in the family and the
community. The objective of social advancement would be achieved through raising women's
level of awareness and fostering the confidence to strive for social change through the
development of strong, cohesive groups as a source of mutual support of the women and the
provision of an animator within the village to motivate and guide them. Improvement in their
economic position would be achieved through increasing their income eaming potential by
integrating them into the regular delivery system for credit and technical support services. In
order to overcome inadequacies in the selection of beneficiaries under existing government
programs, the project would involve NGOs in the initial screening process. The entry point and
central feature of the project was to be the fornation of savings and credit groups to meet
women's needs for crisis credit.
2.          The major innovative features introduced into the project approach included the
use of animators working at the village level to mobilize women; greater participation of women
in decision making; greater emphasis on promoting self-reliance through self-managed group
savings funds and group credit; improvement in the selection of suitable economic activities and
assistance to formal credit development.
3.          The project introduced three specific features to underpin the credit system. First,
it focused on developing the savings and loan activities of the groups to provide the small
amounts of credit for emergency, social or production purposes, which would not be available
from the banks and for which women would otherwise have recourse to money-lenders.
Secondly, it involved the participating banks in the vetting and selection of beneficiaries from the
outset thus building the confidence of the formal credit agencies in the system. Thirdly, it used
the animators and the groups themselves in deciding on the suitability of both beneficiaries and
activities thereby utilizing local knowledge of applicants and inculcating a sense of responsibility
for credit within the women's own organizations.



-66-
Annex 2
4.          A Mid-Term Evaluation of the project was carried out in January-February 1995
providing valuable provisional information on the impact of the project and on the lessons to be
learned from project implementation. These provide guidance for the design of the present
project.
B. Project Impact
5.          The project has shown considerable achievements. It has almost reached its
quantitative targets of loans disbursed and has overachieved by 40 percent the number of groups
to be formed. Qualitatively, there is evidence of considerable social impact of the project on
women, particularly in well functioning, homogenous groups of very poor women where women
report a greater degree of self-confidence, greater mobility and greater ease to visit banks and
converse with different officials. Landless women report a significant social change where they
were formerly laborers and are now small scale entrepreneurs as a result of obtaining loans under
the project. For other women, the most important social impact has been their ability to get out
of the house and attend group meetings, particularly where considerable opposition from their
husbands in the beginning had to be overcome.
6.          Group membership has allowed women to undertake many collective actions
ranging from petitions for street lighting to arranging for milk routes to stop in their village. The
social impact appears to have been greater in empowering women in the public rather than in the
private sphere. Women have reported feeling bolder now to go to agencies for inputs, to visit
banks and to talk with officials visiting the village. The project seems to have enabled women to
develop appropriate attitudes as well as skills and know how in dealing with external "modern"
institutions (NGOs, line agencies, banks, etc.).
7.          One of the positive features has been the kind of coordination achieved among
NGOs, banks, and line departments due to the proactive role played by the MAHAM and the
Project Implementation Units (PIUs). In addition, the bank has seen in the project a good
opportunity for developing poor rural clients and graduating them to regular bank borrowers.
8.          The strongest positive points of the project are: (a) women's groups have been
formed and the group members have been helped to develop the habit of systemic savings; (b)
the project has made it possible for unregistered self-help women's groups to open savings bank
accounts, an important achievement in itself; (c) in groups where women had the chance to take
and repay several, small, internal loans for a variety of purposes, they have been able to develop
a "repayment culture"; (d) the recovery rate of the bank loans is excellent due to the extensive
support system developed by the project. The project has demonstrated that banks can give loans
to women's self-help groups and to enjoy high repayment rates. This is important in the current
climate of market orientation and liberalization in which banking institutions are likely to shy
away from providing credit to the poor, especially poor women, because of the high transaction
costs involved and the perceived risk factor involved in repayments; and (e) the cooperation



-67-
Annex 2
between the Government, the Women's Development Corporation (WDC) and officers from the
line departments, the bank and the NGOs achieved in this project represents an important model
to be emulated.
C. Lessons Learned
Role of Intervenors
9.          Non-governmental Organizations (NGOs). The Tamil Nadu Mid-Term
Evaluation Report (MTER) highlights the key role that NGOs have played in the implementation
of the project; the quality of the group was mainly dependent on the type of orientation provided
by the NGO. It however stresses that even well qualified NGOs need to be monitored especially
with regard to the progress and quality of groups and the relevance and adequacy of training
provided. To shift the focus on "quality", the report suggests that NGOs should be paid not
exclusively on the basis of beneficiaries receiving loans as is the present practice, but also on the
number of groups trained in group dynamics and technical skills.
10.         The MTER draws attention to the variation in group quality and performance due
to the difference among NGOs in terms of philosophy, management style and experience with
credit programs. It also highlights the need to take a cautious approach to building on existing
groups which have been formed for a different purpose and to evaluate carefully how effectively
they can be modified to fit in with the project's objectives. When existing groups were
transformed into IFAD groups the members (and often the focus) did not change; as a result
several well to do women remained as members of the IFAD group, other groups were too large
to function as effective SHGs, yet others were formed into clusters without first consolidating the
SHGs at the base leading to top-down systems of management.
I1.         District Project Implementation Unit. It was originally envisaged that the
Project Coordinator would not only be a senior person but also one with experience in
participatory management, interaction with other institutions, especially with NGOs, and who is
committed to the project, open to new ideas and has confidence in other people's institutions as
part of a sustainable strategy. This has not been implemented. In addition, brief postings of one
year of officials from the main line departments, especially of those who are not happy with the
transfer, undermine the project.
12.         The MTER also stresses the need for a Project Implementation Unit (PIU) at
district level arguing that a Coordination Committee of district heads of technical departments
supported by a nodal officer cannot perform the same functions as a PIU. However, it is not
necessary for the PIU to have a large contingent of technical staff on deputation from each line
department.
13.         Technical Support. The MTER draws particular attention to the lack of support
provided by the line departments, particularly in the field of animal husbandry. This has had a



-68-
Annex 2
serious impact on a large number of people since over 45 percent of the loanees have opted for
milk animals. The report points out that several of these animals were not of good quality - one
of the reasons being that the market price of a productive animal was below the approved unit
cost (INR 10 000 versus INR 14 000); but it also highlights other reasons for which the
veterinary department involved needs to share responsibility, for example: inadequate provision
of fodder; high costs of purchasing fodder and concentrates; lack of professional advice and
training in management relating to health, feed needs and breeding; inadequate veterinary
services, usually difficult to access; the difficulty to maintain monthly repayments especially
when milk yield falls.
14.         Animators. The MTER highlights the crucial role that animators play; they have
had a strong impact on the groups. The tendency to dominate the group, however, was clearly
noticeable. The MTER emphasizes the need for training animators in participatory skills so that
they are equipped to support the emergence of other leaders within the group who in turn will be
mutually supportive. The report points out the need for the animators honorarium to be met by
the group and indicates from its study of several groups that this is feasible from the fifth year
onwards. The report indicates that animators in some areas tend to include women in the group
with whom they can relate resulting in a group where the literacy rate as well as the economic
status of members is relatively high.
15.         Banks. Only one commercial bank is involved in financing under the Tamil
Nadu project. As a result in certain areas, the presence of banks is inadequate in relation to the
number of groups resulting in an over-load on one branch and considerable inconvenience and
cost to the borrowers. It concludes that a branch finds it difficult to handle more than 50 groups
without computerizing its operations which it is estimated would cost INR 0.2 million per
branch. The MTER also highlights the elaborate procedures required for loan disbursement.
16.         The MTER recommends that the National Project should give consideration to
adopting the group lending approach using the pattern proposed under the Reserve Bank of India
circular and National Bank for Agriculture and Rural Development guidelines allowing banks to
finance SHGs directly as bankable institutions - no records of individual member loans need be
maintained by the bank.
Group Formation and Management
17.         The MTER states that "an important lesson learned from the Tamil Nadu project
is that one of the best indicators of the dynamism and cohesion of a group is the degree of
rotation to all members of small, internal loans based on their savings prior to applying for bank
loans. Groups which were not constrained from doing this due to local bank rules obliging them
to keep their savings as bank deposits and were able to postpone the application for bank loans
for at least a year, were able to develop a sense of self-reliance and solidarity as well as a
repayment culture. It would, therefore, be very important to ensure that in the National Project,
NGO and bank regulations are favorable in allowing groups to experience a high degree of small,



-69-
Annex 2
internal loans for a minimum of a year". The report suggests that no bank loans are to be given
until the groups have acquired the habit of regular savings credited to a common fund and
experience of revolving these savings through internal loans. It suggests that groups must have
the experience of saving and lending for at least one year before loans are provided. The practice
adopted by banks of holding the savings as a kind of collateral or risk coverage therefore must be
discontinued.
18.         The report also stresses the need for improvement in the quality of record keeping.
Most of the NGOs were not well equipped to maintain book of accounts and to audit the
common fund of the groups. They need to be provided with technical support to improve their
performance.
19.         Factors Enhancing Group Sustainability. The Review Mission found that the
following factors enhanced group solidarity and group sustainability: size - small groups less
than 25; homogeneity - similar socio-economic background; degree of rotation of the common
fund based on savings before project loans were availed of; training of animators in group
dynamics and management; quantum and rate of savings - high rate generated confidence in their
own efforts; broad spectrum of group activities to include provision of service which also
increased the common fund and supported group action.
20.         Federations. Attempts to create some kind of group federation should not be
undertaken until the groups have attained a mature level of functioning, appreciate the
importance of such federation, and are in a position to control it. The present experience
indicates that such federations should be limited to a social and information sharing role, and
refrain from playing any kind of financial role.
21.         Target Group. The MTER noted that even with well defined target group
criteria of poor women, the most disadvantaged women, namely the landless, women from
marginal farm families and female headed households, have not been adequately represented in
the groups. This has arisen partly from the tendency of the animator to select women with whom
she felt comfortable and to whom she could relate as they were literate. It has also arisen through
a misconception of the need to give emphasis to land based activities.
Training
22.         The MTER stresses that the content and quality of training differs from NGO to
NGO. It is much below the standard required in many cases and the NGOs need to be trained
particularly in: group dynamics; group management; gender and poverty issues; credit related
issues; and participatory planning skills. This service could be provided by larger experienced
NGOs and/or consultants.



-70-
Annex 2
23.   The MTER also noted the following points with regard to training:
The Animators in many areas are given the major responsibility for conducting
training modules but they are not adequately qualified and experienced to
undertake this role. In addition, they are not equipped with adequate
communication materials. In addition, the animators require training in
participation in order to enable them to modify their own often dominating
behavior and to create a participatory environment for all women members.
Group training is most crucial for the success of the project; this includes
primarily training in group dynamics and group management and participatory
skills required for group cohesion and sustainability. A large number of women
are still passive participants in meetings; group training with a focus on the
weaker members should be given priority. An important training aid to be
developed is a video of the behaviors and interactions between women members
in a well functioning group, illustrating successful group dynamics, active
participation and democratic decision making.
Entrepreneurship training is crucial for effective management of economic
activities; it should include the basic principles of planning, management and
accounting, including cost of their own labor and profit. In addition, inadequate
training had been provided to members in relation to their income generating
activity taken up. Technical training may be drawn from technical people attached
to NGOs, by the line departments or from technical trainers retained on a
contractual basis by the project. Training in poverty and gender issues should be
extended to all project officers, member of line departments on deputation to the
PIU and bank officers at district and branch levels as well as to NGOs.
Women find it difficult to leave the village to attend training. In order to
accommodate women's work overload and multiple responsibilities, their training
should be offered at the village level and should be practical, on-the-job training,
well adjusted to women's reality.
An exercise in the identification of training needs of the collaborating NGOs is
important to be followed up by the provision of training by specially trained
trainers. Animators and supervisors should be trained in recording the number of
trained beneficiaries, the type of training provided, and the number of visits by
technical staff to the group.
All training activities and particularly the quality of training offered to all target
groups and especially to beneficiaries needs to be carefully monitored by the
project management.



-71-
Annex 2
Economic Activities
24.         The MTER stressed the need to diversify the profitable productive activities for
which loans are available to women in order to increase women's awareness and empowerment.
This diversification, however, needs to be based on the results of good market research
undertaken in different localities of project concentration. Market research capability should be
accessed on a contractual basis.
25.         The MTER also noted that the direct subsidies provided to the beneficiaries as a
bonus after loan repayment do not constitute a significant motive for members of well
functioning groups. On the contrary, they may be creating expectations for future subsidies that
cannot be fulfilled. The MTER observed that many members were willing to borrow even
without subsidies, provided loans were given in time and investments were sounds. Economics
of some of these investments revealed that they would be viable even without subsidy. Since the
overall objective of the project is to make them regular clients of the bank over a period of time,
it is necessary to wean them away from subsidy as early as possible.
Savings and Contributions to Groups
26.         Some women are able to save more but are reluctant to save through the group
fund as there is no way of getting their savings back except as small loans - hence they deposit
their surplus savings outside of the group. If saved through the group, the members could have
earned higher returns, at the same time increasing the loanable funds of the group. In mature
groups, the system of individual deposits should be introduced, on a pilot basis and should be
thoroughly evaluated.
27.         Provision was made under the project for contributions to the groups from the
Capital Development Fund established under the project. This contribution should go as a grant
to well functioning, dynamic groups in order to increase the possibility of flexibility to its
members and safeguard sustainability by increasing women members' self-confidence.
Monitoring and Evaluation
28.         The MTER has broadened the list of indicators to be used in monitoring and
evaluation. It stresses the need to include data concerning the training of loanees in practical and
technical skills, in group dynamics and participatory skills, as well as data regarding the degree
of consolidation of groups (the indicators are listed below). It also stresses the need for
introducing "participatory evaluation" in the Dharmapuri District to begin with where the lead
NGO has experience in conducting this exercise and later in other districts.

Annex 3
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
STATUS OF WOMEN, TARGET GROUP AND PROJECT AREA
A. STATUS OF WOMEN
1.          Women in India have come a long way in the last quarter of a century. Improved
household food security, an expanding health care system, and maternal and child health
interventions have brought about impressive gains in women's health status. The life expectancy
for females at birth has risen from about 32 years in 1951 to about 60 years in 1991. The death rate
of females has declined significantly, and in 1991 was marginally lower for females than for males
(9.8 vs. 10.1). The infant mortality (IMR) differential between females and males has also been
bridged, with a male IMR of 81 and a female IMR of 80 per 1,000 live births. The maternal
mortality rate is about 40 per 10,000 live births. The girl child has become the focus of the
universal primary education campaign, and in 1993-94, 71.4 percent of the girls of primary school
age were enrolled in primary school.
2.           Yet women in India remain one of the most disadvantaged groups in society.
Viewed against almost all indicators of development, women continue to lag behind men. There
are only 927 women for every 1,000 men in India. Women's access to literacy and education is
more limited. Only 39.4 percent of women are literate against 63.8 percent for men. Of the 324
million illiterates enumerated in the 1991 Census, 61 percent were women and girls. School
enrolment exhibits similar gender disparities particularly at secondary and higher levels of
education. The employment record of women shows more women in the unorganised sector and in
unskilled and lesser paid jobs. Malnutrition is significantly higher amongst girls than boys, whilst
even as adults, women frequently do not have equal access to food within the farnily. Despite
women's higher morbidity, far fewer women than men use health services.
3.          The continued disadvantaged of women in large measure stems from entrenched
social attitudes. Whilst the Constitution guaranteed formal equality and radical social legislation
enacted in recent decades has introduced important innovations in the Indian social structure
affecting women's status and role, the enactment of laws does not change attitudes and the views of
society towards the position of women have changed little over the years. Devaluation of women
commences at birth with the preference for male offspring and continues through a childhood of
conditioning to the submissive, subservient role required of them in the marital home. A woman is
never viewed as a person in her own right but always as someone's daughter, wife or mother.
Within the family, a woman is a social dependent, her worth measured in terms of her ability to
produce male children. Widows represent a particularly vulnerable group of women, largely
debarred from remarriage and socially ostracised. Although forming a minority of women, in
absolute numbers widows represent a large group of women subjected to a great deal of suffering.



-76-
Annex 3
Where young brides have been married to older men, many widows can be quite young with young
children to support. The plight of many widows is hidden where they remain part of their
husband's family but are frequently neglected and ill-treated.
4.          The position of women in the social structure affects the way they are regarded in
their economic roles. Although women are major contributors to the survival of the family and are
vital and productive workers in India's national economy, this is largely unrecognised. The poorer
the family, the greater its dependence on women's economic productivity. Women contribute a
much larger share of their earnings to basic family maintenance than do men. Studies in Kerala and
Tamil Nadu found that despite women's lower wage rates and fewer days of paid employment,
(which meant that women's incomes were between one-third and one-half of their husband's
income), women's contribution to the household budget was greater. Whilst on average women's
earnings represented 53 percent of the earnings of their husbands, women's contribution to
household maintenance averaged 72 percent. Women on average contributed 98 percent of their
earnings towards family maintenance whilst men contributed only 78 percent. There is clear
evidence that women's income contributes markedly to family welfare through better health and
nutrition, particularly of children. In spite of this, women lack access to the resources which could
increase their productivity and earning potential. Social attitudes, which always view women as
only supplementary income earners even when they contribute 50-100 percent of the family
income, restrict them to low paid, unskilled occupations. More than 90 percent of rural women
workers are unskilled. Around 90 percent of women are engaged in the informal unorganised
sector and of these around 80 percent are in agriculture. Wage rates in agriculture are on average
30 to 50 percent less than for men. Female casual laborers in rural India show the highest incidence
of poverty of any occupational category, male or female. Women have little access to land and
other productive assets and this lack of collateral largely excludes them from institutional credit and
forces them to depend on high cost informal sources of credit to secure capital for self-employment
activities. Women also generally lack the bureaucratic know-how to make the system work for
them.
5.          Rural women living below the poverty line are mostly engaged in agriculture and
animal husbandry. Landless women engage in non-farm activities during the lean agriculture
periods, but the major part of their earnings come from agricultural labor. Women are responsible
for 70 to 80 percent of field work, whilst most post harvest and processing tasks are solely their
responsibility. However, stark gender divisions assign all activities involving direct manual labor
to women whilst all operations involving draught animals or machinery are generally performed by
men. Women are also heavily involved in animal husbandry, particularly small livestock, whilst
women account for 85 percent of the labor engaged in dairy production.
6.          In addition to their contribution to household income, women shoulder the entire
burden of household activities and child care responsibilities. Poor women generally work longer
hours than their menfolk - up to 14-16 hours per day. At the aggregate level 58 percent of women's
work time is spent in conventional activities such as cooking, cleaning and child care.



-77-
Annex 3
7.          There is a strong correlation between a woman's access to independent income and
her control over the family's resources. The ability to earn and control income is one of the most
powerful determinants of a woman's status in the family. Strategies for women's empowerment
therefore need to take note of the need to increase women's income. The Bank's research'
indicates that expanding women's economic opportunities and increasing the income they control
results in greater economic efficiency, improved child health and nutrition and reduced poverty.
8.          A disproportionate burden of poverty is suffered by female-headed households
(FHHs) which are noted to be much more poverty prone than male-headed households. Female
heads are usually widowed, separated or divorced, with much less access to and control over land,
greater dependency on wage labor for employment, a higher incidence of voluntary unemployment,
and lower levels of education and literacy. Female heads of households form a higher percentage
of the lower income deciles - often being amongst the poorest of the poor. At the same time, the
delivery structures of credit, technical advice, etc., do not reach them as institutions are slow to
recognise women as heads of households. The smaller size of FHHs also implies a lower
availability of household labor which affects their ability to undertake economic activities.
9.          The position of women varies across regions and according to the economic status
of the household and its place in the caste hierarchy. On the whole, the position of women is more
constrained in the north (covering Haryana, Bihar, and Uttar Pradesh amongst the project states)
than in the south (including Karnataka). Female labor force participation rates are lower, and female
land ownership is far less common in the north Health indicators, such as female infant mortality,
and overall sex ratios, are more heavily biased against females in the north. Female literacy is
lower in the north than in the south, whilst purdah, high dowry payments and violence against
women are all much more prevalent in the north. Regional differences are further modified by
caste and economic circumstances. Constraints on women are stronger among caste Hindus than
amongst Scheduled Castes (SC) and Scheduled Tribes (ST) - and stronger amongst land-owning
cultivators than among landless laborers or marginal farm families. Among the poor who cannot
afford female seclusion, women have always entered the labor force when they could find work.
Advancing Gender Equality - From Conception to Action, World Bank Publication, 1995



-78-
Annex 3
B. Target Group
10.         Definition of the Target Group. The target group for the project would be women
below the poverty line in rural areas as defined by the Government of India (GOI) normn, viz., INR
I 1 000 per household of 5.5 persons. Particular emphasis would be given to including both dejure
and the more numerous defacto female-headed households who constitute the most disadvantaged
groups of women (for the country as a whole, 30 percent of households are estimated to be female
headed). The project would also seek to include widows and deserted women who, although re-
integrated into extended farnilies, frequently live abject lives as a result of their lack of independent
means. Particular attention would also be given to ensuring that the most marginal women,
particularly the landless, are included. In order to ensure strong, cohesive and sustainable affinity
groups, no eligibility criteria would be set beyond the poverty criterion in order to allow women
genuine freedom of choice in group membership.
11.         Procedures for Identification of the Target Group. Beneficiary targeting
would be the responsibility of the NGOs. While the list of below poverty families (BPL) would
be used as a starting point, the NGOs would have the flexibility to assess the validity of potential
beneficiaries and, in particular, to include women who are not on the BPL list but are clearly
adjudged to be below the poverty line. The targeting process would follow a number of
sequential steps. First, keeping in mind the cluster approach, groups of villages with a high
percentage of poor households would be selected. Within the villages/hamlets, potential
beneficiary groups would be identified through regular visits to the village, informal talks when
people gather, and participatory processes involving the whole community in identifying the
social spectrum of the village, and the community's perceptions of who is poor. The NGO
would then interact with the identified potential beneficiaries to promote the idea of the
formation of an affinity group to seek to solve their problems of poverty and discrimination. Out
of this exercise would emerge self-selected groupings of poor women motivated to work together
for their self-enhancement.
12.         Profile of the Target Group. The target group would largely comprise women
from marginal and small farm households and landless families. The landless and near landless
are estimated to represent around 30 percent of rural households. While around 30 percent of
households in the country as a whole are estimated to be female headed, in some of the more
socially conservative states such as Bihar and Uttar Pradesh, the percentage is likely to be lower
due to social practices of absorbing widows into the husband's extended family.
13.         The percentage of marginal farm families in the project districts ranges from 18 to
76 percent. The majority of women are engaged in agriculture, with the percentage of the female
workforce involved in agriculture ranging from 75 percent in Karnataka to 90 percent in Bihar.
The percentage of the female workforce engaged as agricultural laborers ranges from 35 percent
in Uttar Pradesh to 59 percent in Bihar.



-79-
Annex 3
14.         The target group would comprise a high proportion of SC and ST. Overall the
presence of SC in the project districts ranges from 4 percent (Panchmahals, Gujarat) to 32
percent (Hardoi, UP). The largest concentrations of ST population in the project area are found
in Bihar (43% in Ranchi) and in Gujarat (47% in Panchmahals and 46% in Baruch).
15.         The position of women and the level of deprivation differs from state to state and
class to class. Within a region, differences exist between rural and urban women with rural poor
women generally being the most disadvantaged. Although there exists a broad correlation
between status of women and the general economic backwardness of the state, there are
exceptions such as in Haryana where the status of women continues to be much lower despite the
high rate of economic growth that the state has achieved.
16.         In addition to regional differences, there are also significant differences on caste
and class lines within the regions. Constraints on women appear to be stronger among caste
Hindus than among the SC and ST, and stronger among land-owning cultivators than among
landless laborers or marginal farm families. Thus the fact that SC/ST, the landless and marginal
farm families will predominate in the target group means that the degree of social oppression
faced by the target group is likely to be somewhat diminished. In addition, the poor are known
to be more open and receptive to change, having less at stake than the privileged and hence,
within the family, the scope for change would seem to be more propitious. However, by virtue
of society's views with regard to poverty, gaining recognition within the community may prove
more difficult. Paradoxically, the experience of the Tamil Nadu project has been that women
have made more advancement in public life than in their private lives.
C. Project Area
17.         The project area comprises the six states of Bihar, Gujarat, Haryana, Kamataka,
Madhya Pradesh and Uttar Pradesh. Within each state the project would be implemented in a
limited number of districts. The criteria for the selection of districts are as follows:
(a)   high incidence of poverty as reflected in the percentage of population below the
poverty line;
(b)   high incidence of disadvantaged groups as reflected in the percentage of Scheduled
Caste and Scheduled Tribe population in the district;
(c)   ad-7erse status of women as reflected in adverse sex ratio and female illiteracy;
(d)   representation of regional socio-economic variations within the state to ensure
testing of project interventions under all circumstances during the initial phase of
project implementation; and
(e)   implementation capacity as reflected in the availability of suitable NGOs, lending
institutions and other public and private sector agencies providing support services.



-80-
Annex 3
18.         Project activities would start in one or two districts in each state. A limited number
of additional districts would be brought under the project in a phased manner in accordance with
the increasing experience and implementation capacity. Uttar Pradesh is an exception as the project
would be building on the experience gained in the formation of women's SHGs by the UP Land
Development Corporation under the World Bank-assisted Sodic Lands Reclamation Project which
operates in 13 districts. In addition, two new districts (Sitapur and Bandha) would be taken up
within UP where the work could be coordinated by the UP Women's Welfare Corporation.
Feedback from the M&E system would indicate the readiness of states to expand the program into
new districts. The situation would be reviewed and any necessary changes made at the Mid-Term
Review. The 35 districts identified for project implementation are listed below.

INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
SELF-HELP GROUP CONCEPT
A. Role of Groups
1.    Group formation is seen as crucial to the empowerment process as women draw strength
from numbers. The group provides: (a) confidence and mutual support for women striving for social
change; (b) a forum in which women can critically analyze their situations and devise collective
strategies to overcome their difficulties; (c) a framework for awareness raising, confidence building,
dissemination of inforrnation and delivery of services, and for developing communal self-reliance and
collective action; and (d) a vehicle for the promotion of economic activities.
B. SHG Concept
2.    The project has adopted the self-help group (SHG) as the appropriate people's institution
which provides poor women with the space and support necessary to take effective steps towards
greater control of their lives in private and in society. The essence of an SHG is that it lays the
foundation for self-reliance through building up an institution which has the capacity to continue the
development and empowerment processes for women in the future. Self-help promotion attempts to
raise the choice level of the poor by building up an institutional framework that is in tune with their
capacities of self administration and better geared towards serving their needs. Until now this
potential of the rural poor of helping themselves has been underestimated and therefore
underexplored. Efforts were not made to stimulate the capacity of the rural poor to help themselves.
3.    Savings and credit is put forward as an important entry point for the activities of SHGs as
requires the active participation and commitment of all members. It provides them with an
opportunity to exercise control and participate in decision making. It also satisfies a short term
needs for crisis credit (for consumption and emergency purposes), leading later to the provision of
credit for productive purposes, and removes them from exploitation at the hands of the money-
lenders. The self-help group is seen as a dynamic institution, which builds on the resources and
management skills of its members and their increasing confidence to become involved in issues and
programs.
C. Essential Features of SHGs
4.    As the concept of SHGs and the strategy to form such groups is not widely known or
practised in most of the project areas, detailed information is provided on the essential features of
SHGs, their characteristics, benefits and contribution to the overall empowerment and development
objectives of the project. In addition, a detailed description is provided on the SHG formation and
development process. This is followed by a discussion on the role of the NGOs as intervenors and
ways in which other agencies can be used to supplement the available NGOs in the process of
forming SHGs.



-88-                                  Annex 4
5.    Some of the essential features of SHGs are:
(a)    Ownership of the group by the members. The group exists because the members see
some value in it in helping them to solve their problems through their collective
efforts. Ownership and control of the group belongs to the members and not to the
promoting institution, be it an NGO or government.
(b)   Affinity as the base for coming together. A sustainable, cohesive group needs a
common underlying bond on which trust can be built. This may be caste, sub-caste,
blood, community, place of origin, occupation, etc. The intervenors (whether from
the NGO, bank or government) must have the experience to be able to identify these
natural groupings or affinity groups. It is, therefore, essential that the groups are
formned naturally and by the will of the women themselves.
(c)    Mutual help as the foundation of the group's existence. The rationale for the
existence of the group is mutual help and progress towards self-reliance and not the
passive receipt of benefits.
(d)    Forumfor collective learning. The groups provide a forum for collective learning
which rural people find more "friendly", based on mutual respect/support, and which
is consequently more effective than the individual approach that is commonly
adopted.
(e)    Forum for inter-agency dialogue and cooperation. The groups provide a firn base
for dialogue and cooperation in programs with other institutions like government
departments, cooperatives, financial and panchayat institutions; if the groups are
functioning well, they have the credibility and the power to ensure their participation
in identifying, planning, budgeting and implementation of programs for the
empowerment of women with a special focus on the poor.
(f)    Cost-effective credit delivery mechanism. The group provides a cost effective credit
delivery system as the transactions costs of lending decrease sharply both to the
banks and the borrowers. At the same time, individual members maintain control
over the pace, timing, size and schedules of loans and programs. The group also
helps to build the individual member's management capacity to optimize returns.
D. Indicative SHG Development Process
Operations of SHGs
6.    Group size should be kept small, less than 20 members in order to ensure the necessary trust
and confidence essential for building a cohesive group. Groups of this size are also a prerequisite to
be treated as informal entities for the purposes of linkage with the banks'.
Prima facie, SHGs with more than 20 members would attract the provisions of the Companies Act, 1956
which requires all associations of more than 20 persons formed for the purposes of acquisition of gain by
the association or its individual members to be registered as a company under this Act.



-89-                                Annex 4
7.    It is essential that the groups are democratically managed and all decisions concerning the
group are made on the basis of group consensus. All SHG members would participate in the decision
making processes and no executive committee would be formed as suspicion may crop up when the
ownership and management of the SHG diverge which could prove fatal to the existence of the SHG.
8.    Each group would, with the assistance of the NGO, frame its own regulations for the
operation of the group savings or common fund, on the basis of group consensus, covering minimum
amount of monthly or weekly savings; maximum loan amount for the first and subsequent loans;
interest rates; repayment periods; penalties and action to be taken in the event of default; eligibility
criteria for loans; allocation of loans between consumption and production, etc.
9.    Regular meetings are important for cementing groups. Ideally groups should meet weekly
but the minimum would be monthly. At least one formal business meeting, at which all members
would be expected to attend, should be conducted each month. All the operations of the savings and
loans fund would be conducted at this meeting. Members would be required to bring their savings
and loan repayments to these meetings, applications for loans would be discussed by all the group
members and loans would be sanctioned and disbursed during the meeting (depending on the
availability of funds). The NGO field worker would initially attend these meetings to provide advice
if required and to check that the accounts are maintained correctly. If the group meets weekly or
fortnightly, savings and loans business can be transacted more frequently. However, the other
meetings should be used primarily for discussions linked to awareness raising; follow-up informal
interactive training sessions on group dynamics, leadership, etc., by the NGO field worker or
training on special topics, e.g., health, nutrition, agriculture, etc., at which outside resource persons
would be invited to attend. In addition, one meeting a month should be devoted to compiling the
letter for exchanging with other groups.
10.   Since most of the transactions would take place on this one day each month, liquidity
management should not prove to be much of a problem for the group as it is expected that the
majority of the savings would be loaned directly so that the problem of sizable surpluses would not
arise. A portion of any surplus funds would be kept in the cash box to be immediately available to
meet emergency needs of members. Most groups agree that the two representatives may decide on
emergency loans to group members up to a set amount to be formally sanctioned at the next group
meeting. The cash box would have two locks with one key held by each of the group
representatives. Additional surpluses would be deposited in the bank. Similarly for loans sanctioned
in excess of the cash collected at the meeting, additional cash would be drawn from the bank and
disbursed to the borrowers at the next meeting.
11.   The representatives would prepare a report and financial statement to the group every six
months. These half-yearly meetings would also be used for amending regulations and policies. As
the capital accumulations of the group increase over the years, the opportunity for the greater use of
loans for productive purposes would grow and groups would be guided towards granting larger loans
with longer repayment periods. Groups would generally recognize the need to charge lower interest
rates on longer term loans.



-90-                                Annex 4
12.   Loans would initially be provided mainly for consumption purposes but as the savings fund
grows and the group gains experience in managing the fund, more loans would be given for small
productive purposes. This would enable the group to gain experience of managing credit, learn
credit discipline and build up a track record of sound credit management making it an attractive
client for the bank.
13.   Experience shows that women's needs and ability to manage credit through their groups
rapidly outgrows the limitations of their own resources. Hence, after a minimum of six months and
more usually nine months, and based on the performance of the group in managing the funds, the
banks would be encouraged to provide a bulk loan to the group to be disbursed by the group to its
members as individual loans for both consumption and productive purposes - the group would be
responsible for repayment to the bank. Initially this linkage with the bank would take the form of a
line of credit (cash credit limit), initially equivalent to the group's savings (see Annex 7, para 12 for
further details). The cash credit limit is gradually raised in line with the bank's increasing
confidence in the group's credit management record. Over time, the group may, in consultation with
the bank, decide to progress to a group term loan or it may prefer to continue with the cash credit
arrangements. This process would constitute the beginning of a sustainable relationship with the
bank providing for a continual flow of credit to the group beyond the project period.
14.   At the same time, the SHGs would provide the platform for other aspects of empowerment
such as literacy, dissemination of information on family welfare, health and nutrition, financial
management, legal rights, etc., and the base for social action. Participatory processes involving the
husbands' participation would be encouraged on occasions to discuss relationships within the family,
participation in decision making and control of income and sharing of non-economic family
responsibilities in order to begin to break down stereotyped roles and to foster "togetherness" in
male/female relationships and a working towards the goal of a more equal partnership in the family.
15.   Once groups have became mature (after 3-4 years) the concept of individual savings
accounts with the group from which women would be able to withdraw their savings could be
introduced, initially on a pilot basis. This would permit women with a greater capacity to save to
have access to their savings while increasing the loanable funds of the group (see Annex 7, para 11).
Group Development Process
16.   Based on the present accumulated experience, the process of SHG formation and
development can be separated into three phases as indicated below. This also emphasizes the part to
be played by the NGO field worker at each stage and the features of the SHG at the end of each
phase. Groups would develop at their own pace and hence the timings given are purely indicative
and will vary from group to group.
Phase I - Identification and Formation of SHGs: 0 - 4 months
17.   This is a critical function on which depends all the further progress of the group. The NGO
as intervenor plays a key role here in identifying affinity groups to form the basis of the SHG. This
is where the experience of the NGO is crucial.



-91-                                  Annex 4
18.   The means used by the intervenors to identify these affinity groups are several; a few popular
ones are:
regular visits to the village, meeting with informal groups gathered around tea shops,
temples, water spots, markets, provision shops, milk collection centers; special care
should be given to visit and interact in areas where the scheduled castes and tribes
live and where women tend to gather;
involvement in a common action like desilting a pond, or building a road also helps
to identify these groups, provided people organize the action themselves; it is noticed
that in such cases they tend to work in affinity groups;
participatory appraisal methods are useful since they too involve all the community
in various exercises which help to identify the various groupings in the village, the
assets and resources of each family, people's perception of who is poor, their credit
needs, incomes and seasonal variations; the community is involved in collecting and
correcting data in a manner in which all can participate using instruments to which
they are accustomed such as sticks, stones, seeds, colored powders, etc.
during the participatory process background information which will be useful in
subsequent stages of the group's activities, e.g., when it begins to focus on economic
activities, is also collected viz., the availability of natural resources, activities
undertaken, skills, markets, perceptions on poverty and de, elopment constraints, etc.
These topics would be addressed later in a more expanded fashion at the appropriate
time.
19.   During this phase, since the SHGs are entirely composed of women, it has been found
necessary to meet with men and to explain what is going on and especially the benefits that will
accrue to the family through SHGs. Group formation is not complete with the first meeting. It takes
several meetings over a period of 2 to 4 months for the group to establish a definite membership.
During this period members may leave and others may join if the group agrees.
20.   The meetings held during this period are not only social gatherings; the members also raise
issues concerning the family and the village. Matters regarding savings and lending are also debated
thoroughly as this is the major common function of the group. Agreement to save and to lend
indicates a degree of trust in one another, the willingness to abide by group decisions without
breaking bonds and confidence in the group; these are the building blocks of effective SHGs.
21.   In this initial phase it is necessary that intervenors have experience in identifying and
forming SHGs and care should be taken to ensure that the NGOs place experienced staff, at least at
middle management level, to play a key role during this phase.
22.   Critical Features of the SHG at the End of Phase I. These include:
-     the membership of the group is identifiable and stable.



-92-                                 Annex 4
the group has given itself a name.
two members of the group have been elected/selected as the group's
representatives.
the date, time and place of meetings has been decided on by the members and
meetings are held regularly according to the schedule
the members have agreed to save regularly, to create a habit of thrift and to establish
a culture of self help. Some groups may have started savings. If savings have begun,
the group has opened an account in the nearest bank or Post Office. The account has
two signatories, either one from the SHG and the other from the NGO intervenor or
both from the SHG.
basic records like (i) attendance register; (ii) minutes book; and (iii) members
savings ledgers and individual savings pass books (if savings have started) are being
maintained.
Phase II - Group Stabilization: 4 months to 15 months
23.   The key activities which take place during this important phase in the development of the
SHG are:
Savings are regular and lending operations gather strength. This provides the
members of the SHG with an opportunity to acquire the skills to manage its
resources. The group decides on the rate of savings, on the timing of the loans, on
the schedule of payments and repayments; it assess whether the member is able to
manage the asset for which she has asked for a loan and decides on priorities.
Meetings are held regularly; preferably every week; attendance is maintained and
averages over 80 percent; repayments average over 90 percent; sanctions for default
on repayment and for deviant behavior (smoking, arriving late for meetings, etc.) are
imposed and accepted.
The minutes and accounts books are maintained either by a member or by the NGO
staff or preferably by someone from the village who is paid by the SHG for such
services.
Gender issues concerning women in private and public life are raised and discussed.
Literacy and numeracy classes are conducted and attended by all members.
24.   Group training is held regularly, with at least three modules each of two days during this
phase to enable all to participate effectively, especially the weaker members (in some cases special
modules may have to be conducted for them), to build group culture and to raise the level of



-93-                                Annex 4
commitment to mutual support and acceptance of group decisions and of self-confidence in private
and public life. Details of the indicative coverage of the training programs are given in Annex 12.
25.   Training of SHG Representatives. The two representatives who have been elected need to
be trained to conduct meetings, to help the group to establish a supportive culture, to establish a
priorities in lending and to impose and accept sanctions. They need to equip themselves with the
skills of numeracy and literacy to enable them to keep a check on what is recorded in the minutes
and pass books. They need to develop leadership qualities and the confidence to relate with
institutions like government departments and banks as well as to provide leadership in organizing
common action programs. It is important that the group selects/elects the representatives for the
following year at least six months in advance to enable them to build up these skills and confidence.
If the group representatives are not changed every year, there is a danger that power will accumulate
in the hands of a few which will in turn prevent the effective participation of all members. Rotation
of representatives provides all the members with an opportunity to acquire the skills of group
leadership. Since the first set of representatives are likely to be the most proficient women in the
group, once they have handed over responsibilities they could play the role of group animators, after
the groups have been in operation for 12-15 months, taking on some of the supportive roles played
hitherto by the NGO worker, provided they have had at least 3-4 modules of training and exposure to
operating SHGs. One or two small common action programs in the village are planned and
implemented preferably in collaboration with other SHGs if they exist in the village.
26.   Gender Issues. Gender issues concerning women and the girl child are raised at meetings
and form an important part of training. Men are invited to participate in group exchange and in
common action programs and occasionally in SHG meetings. Negative reactions from men to
women's absence from home when they attend meetings or are involved in common action or even
to the increasing presence and profile of women in public life is a common feature during this
period. NGOs have to cope with this problem even offering counseling service in the home when
the problem threatens to escalate.
27.   Support to the group fund of the SHG. After six to nine months, if the SHG is progressing
according to the indicators mentioned above, the group would be linked to a lending institution to
provide a line of credit matching the savings of the group (see para 25 above). This helps to build up
available capital, enabling members to access larger loans for business, trading and other small
income generating activities.
28.   Role of NGO and other intervenors. The NGO continues to play a key role in this phase
in the training of the groups and group representatives as well as in gender sensitization. As far as
the SHGs are concerned, the NGO moves away from direct intervention to support the process of
group growth by attending all the meetings of the SHGs and intervening where required. The line
departments and other private sector institutions play a direct role in training asset owners and
village resource persons.
29.   Features of the SHGs at the End of Phase II. These would include:
-     all the members are engaged in savings;



-94-                                 Annex 4
loaning is taking place for both consumption and, to a lesser extent, productive
purposes;
all transactions are supported with documentation; a summary of loans and
recoveries is posted in a public place and regularly updated;
the group fund is steadily increasing through interest, savings and fund raising by the
group;
the group's resources have been augmented through obtaining a line of credit to the
group from the bank or other lending institution;
the group fund is revolving; bank balance is low or nil;.
the recovery rate is consistently over 90 percent;
attendance is consistently over 80 percent;
the members have acquired the skills of literacy and numeracy to conduct meetings
and to resolve conflicts;
their confidence to act in public life has increased; they are able to relate with other
institutions and government as a group;
the group has the experience of organizing and being involved in one or two common
action programs; and
the members have begun to address gender issues and to take small though
significant actions both in public and at home.
Phase III Growth of self-reliance: 15 months to 36 months - Withdrawal of the NGO
30.   The key developments which take place for the SHG during this phase are:
-     SHG takes over responsibility for managing its own affairs including payment for
services required;
-     Promotion of inter-action between the SHGs within the cluster to facilitate cross-
learning and sharing between groups and to develop a more effective power base for
the women;
-     Greater emphasis on economic activities and on improving productivity through
technology transfer;
-     Credit available from bank increases in line with group's credit history and with
bank's increased confidence in group's credit management capacity;



-95-                                 Annex 4
Linkage with other technical agencies for technology transfer, demonstrations,
exposure to menus of options for more productive and remunerative income
generating activities;
31.   Role of intervenors. The intervenors, particularly the NGOs, need to integrate withdrawal
into their strategy from the beginning in order that interventions support the growth of self reliant
people's institutions and not increase their dependency. Withdrawal becomes more tangible and
acceptable after the major interventions required to build up the groups taper off.
32.   The process of withdrawal would be gradual and the duration of this phase variable
depending on the capacity of the SHGs. It is expected that the process of withdrawal could be
substantially completed by the end of the third year after the formation of the group, but in costing
the services of the NGOs allowance has been made for some support in the fourth year as a
precaution.
33.   During the later years of the project, efforts would be made to foster the emergence of inter-
group associations at the cluster level and inter-cluster associations, but the need for such bodies and
their functions should be decided by the group (see Annex 6 for further details). Experience of apex
bodies in Tamil Nadu has shown that they are vulnerable to political influences or are used as steps
towards political positions. Politicization of apex bodies weakens and eventually destroys the self
help groups. Care should be taken that the groups are strong enough to control the functioning of the
apex bodies before they are formed. In some parts of Tamil Nadu, groups have restricted the roles of
apex bodies to supervising the groups and assisting in problem solving; they have refused to give
apex bodies any funds or the power to control resources.
34.   During this period the supportive role of the NGO decreases. It needs, however, to ensure
that regular feedback and analysis of data is available in order to identify trends in financial
management of the SHGs. A few guidelines given in Attachment 1 relating to the management of
savings, the group fund, loans, book-keeping and audit will help the NGO to identify important
indicators of the SHGs' performance.
E. Strategies for Promotion of SHGs in Areas Without NGO Presence
35.   Since NGOs are recognised to be the most effective agents for the mobilization and provision
of on-going support to women's SHGs, project activities would start in districts/blocks with strong
NGOs who would be contracted by the WDCs to form and sustain the SHGs. However, suitable
NGOs, oriented to the concepts of self-reliance and self-help which underpin the SHGs, are not
omnipresent. Hence, it will be necessary to develop alternative mechanisms for forming SHGs in areas
without NGOs. In such areas, efforts would be made to harness the enthusiasm and commitment of
socially motivated people within the community. These are likely to be people who have been
campaign workers under other awareness raising programs such as the Total Literacy Campaign
(TLC). The WDC and the lead (experienced) NGOs would jointly identify suitable socially motivated
field workers 6-12 months in advance of commencement of work in an area devoid of NGOs. After a
period of initial training, they would work with the lead NGOs to gain practical field experience for
around six months before returning to their "home" areas to start forming SHGs. During the first year



-96-                                Annex 4
of their independent operation, the lead NGO would continue to supervise and support their work. In
time it is expected that these workers would be able to work independently like other smaller NGOs.
Funding required to implement this approach would be channelled through the lead NGOs until the
"emerging" NGO starts operating independently.
36.   Haryana WDC intends to experiment with a variant of this approach through fostering the
formation of a registered society to be managed by a selected number of field workers within the
community, particularly those previously actively involved in promoting the TLC. This society, after
training in the key elements of the project concept/approach , would take on the responsibility for the
formation of SHGs under the overall guidance of the District Project Coordinating Committee and the
WDC. It would engage field workers as required. The experience of other strategies involving various
community groups in the promotion of SHGs currently being piloted would also be closely watched
and evaluated. These would include WEMTOP's experiment with the promotion of SHGs by producer
cooperatives linked to the Khadi and Village Industries Commission.
F. Inclusion of Existing SHGs
37.   The growth path of SHGs outlined above indicates that it takes at least two years before SHG
members are likely to become actively involved in investing in income generating activities on a
significant scale. At the same time, the accumulation of savings by many SHGs under on-going SHG
programs indicates that groups are not readily able to identify potential viable investment opportunities.
Hence, this is an area to which the project needs to devote considerable attention. In order to provide
an early opportunity to promote income generating activities, as well as the development of inter-group
clusters/associations, the project would benefit from the inclusion of some existing SHGs formed by
NGOs. These groups would need to be consistent with the project concept. To this end, the following
criteria would be used to screen existing SHGs for inclusion in the project:
-     the group constitutes a democratically managed affinity group which conforms to the
general principles of SHGs outlined above;
-     the group is involved in the management of savings and credit;
-     the group is orientated towards self-reliance;
-     the group exhibits a high degree of group cohesion and stability and constitutes a good
group as measured by such indicators as high degree of attendance at meetings,
participation of all members in group discussions and decision making and regularity of
savings;
demonstration of sound credit management including high degree of internal loaning
and rotation of savings, and high repayment rate (above 90 percent);
linkage with bank providing resources for investment in income generating activities;
readiness to undertake income generating activities; and



-97-                                 Annex 4
group is sponsored by a project partner NGO which has been trained in the promotion
of business development.
38.   Other criteria to be taken into consideration relate to the sponsoring NGO and would include:
-     Existing SHGs are attached to NGOs which have been contracted to form new SHGs
and are located in or adjacent to areas of the district selected for implementation of the
project;
-     The NGOs which have promoted the SHGs should perceive SHGs as an important
means of women's empowerment and should be giving priority attention to SHG work
(in terms of staff time, training, and resources);
-     in order to facilitate formation of clusters of SHGs, the existing SHGs should be
located in close geographical proximity; and
-     the NGO should agree to work on the components which it may not have pursued to
date, particularly business support inputs, and promotion of clusters/associations.
39.   Existing SHGs would be evaluated for inclusion in the project by the NGOs and verified by the
WDC District Project Manager. "Adopted" existing SHGs would, however, not exceed 30 percent of
the anticipated number of SHGs to be managed in any state program.
40.   Groups formed under other programs such as Mahila Samakhya, DWCRA and TLC are
unlikely to meet these criteria. These groups tend to be large, lack homogeneity and affinity and are
not constituted as self-help groups. Thus, it may not be appropriate to incorporate them wholesale into
the project. The project may benefit from the awareness raising and mobilization efforts of these
programs but it is likely that potential SHGs would only emerge as sub-sets from within these groups.
G. Promotion of SHGs by Mature SHGs
41.        In order to promote the wider replication of SHGs in a more cost effective and
sustainable manner, the project would enable mature SHGs to promote the formation of additional
SHGs. Experience of other NGO programs indicates that stabilized mature SHGs have a powerful
demonstration effect within the locality and frequently women in neighbouring villages express a
desire to form an SHG. These women should first be encouraged to visit the mature group and learn of
their experiences. It is at this point that the members of the new group assess their own capability,
perceive problem areas and satisfy their queries. The potential new SHG would then request assistance
from a representative of the mature SHG in establishing the new SHG. Whilst the representatives from
the mature SHG can provide general guidance and support, training inputs for the new SHG from the
NGO are likely to be required, at least initially. Over time, however, the project would train members
from mature SHGs who show leadership qualities and communication skills as trainers. The project
would seek to form one training team of members from mature SHGs from each cluster of villages.
These training teams would be instrumental in spreading the SHG concept and forming SHGs on a
wider scale in their locality. The NGO's role would be to strengthen the capacity of the cluster and the
existing groups to facilitate this process through various leadership and capacity building programs.
