Annex S
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
NGO PARTICIPATION
A. Role of NGOs
Formation and Support to SHGs
1.          NGOs would play the key role in mobilizing the poor women in the communities
and forming them into SHGs and providing on-going support to the SHGs. It is essential that the
NGO staff adopt an approach in interacting with the SHGs which promotes self-reliance and not
dependency. Thus, the primary role of the NGO would be to motivate and organize groups and
to provide the SHG members with opportunities to acquire the skills to manage the group's
affairs. NGOs would follow participatory processes in order to promote self-reliance.
2.          WDCs would engage NGOs on contract to form and support SHGs. The full
scope of the support to be provided by the NGO staff would cover:
(a)   Identifying affinity groups.
(b)   Forming SHGs.
(c)   Guiding groups in the formulation of savings and credit procedures and liaising
with local lending institutions.
(d)   Attending all group meetings for the first 9-12 months after which, according to
the strength of the group, attendance can be reduced but should be at least once
per month for the first two years.
(e)   Providing initial training of group members and group representatives by the
NGO training team and the provision of follow-up training to group members at
regular group meetings.
(f)   Maintaining the group accounts and minutes for first 12 months (if no suitable
person from within the group or community can be found initially), and training
of a group member or local person to maintain the accounts thereafter.
(g)   Auditing group accounts half-yearly in the first year and thereafter annually.
(h)   Assisting women in the selection of appropriate income generating activities
through participatory processes.



-102-
Annex 5
(i)    Channeling the requirements of group members for training in skill development
and entrepreneurship; for other technical support services e.g. design and
technology and assistance in establishing market linkages to the WDC district
staff.
(i)    Assisting SHGs in community consultation to establish priorities for social
programs and submission of proposals to WDC district office.
(k)   Assisting groups with the organization of cluster level meetings as per the interest
of the groups and later with formation of associations of clusters;
(1)   upporting common action programs and lobbying activities of SHGs.
(m)   Collecting statistics on each group and preparing monthly monitoring tables as
equired as inputs to the M&E system; and quarterly progress reports.
(n)   Performing the necessary administrative functions including: (i) preparation of
uarterly progress reports on activities for submission to WDC District Project
anager; (ii) maintaining accounts of project expenditure in format agreed with
DC and submitting quarterly statements of expenditure, with documentation, to
DC district office; and (iii) attending meetings at district and state level as
equested by WDC.
3           Sharing of responsibilities with WDC staff . The NGO staff and WDC district
staff would work as a team. Through its close contacts with the group members, the NGO would
be responsible for nurturing the development of the groups and the members and for channeling
the requirements for support in other areas (particularly in relation to income generating
activities), which the NGO is generally not equipped to provide, to the WDC. The WDC district
staff would be responsible for organizing the delivery of the required support services to the
SHGs. In addition, the WDC staff would be responsible for monitoring the overall
implementation of the project activities.
4.          Each SHG would receive support from the NGO for a period of 3-4 years. The
nature of the specific activities to be supported by the NGO would change with the different
stages of the group maturation as follows:
(a)   Start-up Phase
The NGO would be actively involved in the group formation and development
process and assisting the SHG in the establishment of its operating procedures,
especially for its savings and credit operations. It is expected that after 12 months
the SHG would have taken over responsibility for the maintenance of its records
and accounts through training a competent group member (if available) or paying



-103-
Annex 5
for the services of a person in the community to perform these tasks. They would
also be involved in providing training for group members and group
representatives (see para 5(d)), including exposure visits and inter-group
exchanges. It is expected that within 3-6 months after commencing its operations,
the group would begin revolving its savings through internal loaning to meet the
consumption needs of its members.
(b)   Group Stabilization
The NGO would assist the SHGs to establish linkages with the banks or other
lending institutions to obtain a line of credit to augment the resources of the
groups. As the focus of the SHG members moves more towards the adoption of
various economic activities, the role of the NGO would switch to assisting the
SHG members in the selection of appropriate activities and ensuring access to the
necessary technical support services which they require. Thus, the NGO would
engage the members in the interactive processes of presentation and discussion of
the menu of options for for on-farm and non-farm activities, involving the
respective technical service providers engaged by the project management to
provide clear information on the technical parameters.
The NGO would then be involved in channeling the women's requirements for
training and technical and marketing support to the WDC. It would assist the SHG
in identifying women from within the group or community to be trained as the
resource persons for the village to provide the basic technical support for land
based and animal husbandry activities.
The NGO would also be involved in assisting the SHGs in drawing up their
requests for social programs in consultation with the community and in preparing
proposals for the development of the community asset selected for submission to
WDC.
Where SHGs express an interest in establishing inter-group linkages through
coming together at the cluster level to form a loose association, the NGO would
assist the process through identification and training of leaders/office bearers,
providing guidance on operational structure and procedures and assisting the
member SHGs to evaluate the pros and cons of the potential roles which the
cluster body could play (see Annex 6).
(c)   Growth of Self-Reliance and Withdrawal of the NGO
During this phase the NGO would continue to support the SHG members in
establishing their economic activities but increasingly responsibilities would be
taken over by the SHGs themselves. To facilitate this, the focus of the NGOs'
attention would begin to switch from close interaction with individual groups to



-104-
Annex 5
assisting the capacity building of clusters of SHGs and, at a later stage,
associations of SHG clusters.
(d)   Training of SHG members and group representatives
Training of the SHG members and group representatives would be treated as a
separate function of the NGOs. The initial training programs to be provided to
each group would cover group development, participatory processes, group
management, maintenance of records and accounts, gender sensitization, and
social issues. It is estimated that one trainer would be able to provide the initial
training to 20 SHGs per year. This initial training in the fundamental principles of
SHGs is crucial to the development and future sustainability of the groups. Hence,
it is essential that a consistent philosophy is presented by all intervenors at this
formative stage. To achieve this, the overall content of training programs would
be agreed on between the WDC and all partner NGOs and, where necessary,
appropriate orientation and training would be provided by a Resource NGO for
the staff of the participating NGOs. Given the critical nature of this input,
implementation of training provided to the SHGs would be closely monitored by
the WDC.
B. NGO Staff
5.    NGO staff inputs. The changing nature of the role of the NGO will also require inputs
from different types of staff. In the first stage the input would mainly be from the field worker
with support from specialist trainers within the NGO. In stages 2 and 3, the frequency of
involvement from the field worker in general support to the SHGs would reduce but the SHGs
would require inputs from more professional, technical personnel on the NGO staff. Thus
although the intensity of inputs to the SHG would reduce, the involvement of higher cost staff
may mean that there is no significant change in the overall cost of servicing the SHGs. Given the
broad-ranging support to be provided by the NGO staff it is expected that participating NGOs
would recruit incremental staff with appropriate expertise and provide necessary additional
training before taking charge of groups at various stages. Adequate financial support has been
provided under the project to ensure recruitment of good quality staff and maintenance of
appropriate skills (see para 1O). It is estimated that in all the participating states (with the
exception of UP and Haryana which have larger villages and no tribal areas under the project), a
field worker would be able to service up to 10 groups of mixed levels of development with 1-2
groups formed per village. In UP and Haryana where the number of groups per village is
expected to average 3, a field worker would be able to service 15-20 groups. The NGOs would
also provide the necessary supervisory staff to oversee implementation at the field level. It is
estimated that one supervisor would be able to supervise 5 field workers but in UP and Haryana,
due to the greater concentration of groups, the number would increase to 7-8 field workers. In
addition the services of the NGO accountant would be required to train the persons selected by
the SHGs to maintain their accounts and to audit the SHG accounts.



-105-
Annex 5
6.          Training of NGO staff. The project would fund the training of NGO staff. This
would include initial and annual refresher training for the field workers and supervisors; training
of the specialist trainers imparting the initial training in group dynamics and management to the
SHGs; training of the NGO accountants and on-going training in entrepreneurship and business
development, gender sensitization and communications skills and use of communications
materials for all NGO project staff. This training may be organized internally where the NGO
has the relevant experience or it would be organized by the WDC Training Coordinator.
Training of field workers would largely take place within the states with the exception of MP and
Haryana which do not have NGOs with experience in SHGs as resource agencies. Staff from
participating NGOs in these states would therefore be trained outside of the state by one of the
experienced NGOs in the country. This training would be supplemented by exposure visits and
study tours within country with provision for two overseas study tours per year for NGO staff.
C. Selection Criteria for NGOs
7.          The experience of the IFAD supported Tamil Nadu Women's Development
Project has highlighted the crucial importance of the selection of NGOs with the right orientation
as project partners. Under the Tamil Nadu project a very clear relationship could be
distinguished between the strength and sustainability of the groups and the philosophy of the
promoting NGO. This emphasizes the need to evaluate not only the performance but also the
outlook and attitudes of the NGOs which wish to participate. Negative factors are welfarist and
paternalistic approaches to the beneficiaries which create dependency not self-reliance.
8.          The selection of NGOs would be guided by the following criteria:
(a)   As part of the legal requirements, the NGO should be a registered body under the
relevant State Law. It should maintain its accounting records and have properly
audited annual statements of income and expenditure;
(b)   The orientation and philosophy of the NGO should conform to the essential
features of the project design viz. adherence to participatory processes; belief in
the objective of self-reliance and empowerment of women; experience in the
formation of affinity groups, preferably groups managing credit and involved in
income generating activities and experience of capacity building of grassroots
institutions including the basic systems required - namely, records, accounts,
formal meetings and feedback systems. If an NGO has the right orientation and a
good track record but lacks specific experience in SHG formation. it can be
provided with adequate exposure and training;
(c)   The NGO is open to learn and absorb new strategies and ideas;
(d)   The NGO is based in the project area, has field experience in the area and has
adequate numbers of field level staff with a clear understanding of the socio-



-106-
Annex 5
economic, cultural and political situation of the area. They should also have
excellent communications skills and good rapport with the community;
(e)    As NGOs participating in the project would be part of a larger effort involving
other NGOs, government officials, private sector institutions and others, the NGO
should be able to interact smoothly with and be part of a team;
(f)    The NGO should have internal stability so as to ensure long-tern support to the
project;
(g)    The NGO should be engaged in secular operations (i.e., development activities of
religious NGOs should not be associated with proselytizing) and should not be
affiliated with any political organization.
(h)    The NGO should be willing to sign a memorandum of understanding (MOU) with
the government committing itself: (i) to pursuing the objectives of the project; (ii)
to making changes within its strategy and systems if there are obstacles in
achieving the objectives of the project; (iii) to attend meetings and to contribute to
the feedback systems and procedures required by Government including regular
reporting. It is only after the MOU is signed that the agreement to contract the
NGO for the project outlining clearly its role and function can be entered into.
D. Contractual Arrangements for NGOs
9.            The payment to the NGOs would be made on the basis of an annual fee per SHG
serviced in accordance with certain performance criteria. The basic fee would be related to the
inputs of field workers and other staff required to fulfill the tasks outlined above. Costs incurred
in the provision of training would be funded in addition to the basic fee for forming and
sustaining the SHGs. NGOs costs in promoting SHGs will vary depending on a number of
factors, including density of target population; number of SHGs which can be forned per
village; distance between the groups promoted; nature of terrain and transport services available;
characteristics of members, e.g., level of education and leadership potential; frequency and
timing of meetings; and educational qualification, and experience of the facilitator. Participating
NGOs would submit costed proposals for undertaking SHG development. For budgeting
purposes a fee of Rs. 5,500 per SHG per year has been used for the formation of new groups by
NGOs' and Rs. 3,000 where groups are promoted by mature SHGs. This would include the
overhead costs of the NGO. The distribution of expenditure between staff, operating expenses,
provision of capital equipment such as cycles or mopeds for the field workers, etc. would be at
the discretion of the NGO. In practice, the appropriate fee should be determined in relation to
the specific circumstances of each NGO and the area in which it is operating.
With the exception of UP where the existence of large villages enabling the formation of 3-5 groups/village and the
demonstration effect from the on-going program will allow for substantial savings in costs - an allowance of Rs, 1,300 per
group for LDC managed districts and Rs. 3,200 for WWC managed districts has been made.



-107-
Annex 5
10.         The suggested performance criteria against which payment of fees would be made
to the NGOs are as follows:
(a)   After 6 months - group is meeting regularly with an average of 80% attendance,
group is saving regularly, basic records are being maintained, group
representatives have been identified, one of the group members' training modules
has been completed;
(b)   After 12 months - all members are engaged in savings; loaning has commenced;
transactions are documented satisfactorily; recovery is consistently over 90%;
attendance is consistently over 80%; members participate in meetings on a wide
scale; group representatives have been trained; group members have received the
second training module;
(c)   After 18 months - indicators as above plus group is in contact with a lending
institution for the provision of a group loan; group has identified a local person to
maintain the accounts and has begun to make contribution towards the payment of
this person;
(d)   After 24 months - indicators as in (b) plus group is able to substantially manage
the regular operations of the group and the need for regular attendance by the
NGO field worker has ceased; members taking up economic activities have
received skill development training related to the economic activity adopted.
(e)   Subsequent payments would be based on similar indicators.
11.         While these indicators represent the desired path of progress of the SHGs, care
needs to be exercised that the NGOs do not prejudice the long term sustainability of the groups
through pushing them to attain these targets before they are ready just to ensure that they receive
the fee. A case by case assessment would be made by the WDC district staff of SHGs which
have not attained these performance criteria and, where there are legitimate reasons for the SHG
proceeding at a slower pace and the group is judged to be basically on course to become a viable
group, payment would be sanctioned.






-109-
Annex 6
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
SHG CLUSTERS AND ASSOCIATIONS
Rationale
1.          The formation of clusters of SHGs (CGs) and associations of clusters (ACs) at
appropriate levels can play an important role in the sustainability of the SHGs and the wider
replication of the SHG concept. Such representative bodies can provide the on-going support and
linkages which the SHGs require, whilst also being responsible for promoting the formation and
nurturing of further SHGs. This would contribute towards significantly reducing the social
intermediation costs involved in group development and make the whole process of expanding the
SHG concept more cost effective and replicable. Over time, these bodies can take over most of the
ftunctions performed by the NGOs and hence they provide a sound basis for withdrawal of NGO
support. CGs/ACs also represent a useful feature of the empowerment process broadening the
power base of women in addressing issues that affect them in a democratic manner.
Lessons Learnt from Past Experience
2.          Limited experience has been gained to date by a number of NGOs in the forrnation
of CGs/ACs both in the project states, (with the exception of Haryana) and elsewhere in the
country. This has highlighted a number of important lessons which are likely to be important
factors in the development of CGs/ACs. The key factors in the formation of CGs/ACs are
summarized below:
(a)  it is essential that CGs/ACs emerge naturally based on demand generated from the
SHGs as they come to appreciate the utility and importance of these bodies and are
in a position to control them. If the foundations of the SHGs are laid well through
providing opportunities for inter-action between groups e.g. through joint trainings
of group members and representatives and member conferences, this process is
likely to occur;
(b)   identifying and training the leaders who are ready to motivate is important for
sustaining the momentum;
(c)   the closer the geographic proximity of the groups, (involving little of no transport
costs), the greater the chances of their coming together;
(d)   SHGs should be located in the service area of a bank branch which helps to
strengthen further linkages with the lending institution;



-110-
Annex 6
(e)   CGs/ACs must fix their own criteria for membership and admission procedures - in
general, only a group that is stable and working well can join;
(f)    member groups should pay an admission fee and annual regular contribution to
cover operating costs;
(g)   the size of CGs consisting of 15-20 SHGs (200-500 members) and ACs of 4-8
clusters (60-160 SHGs) has been found effective;
(h)   the process of forming an AC needs to be approached with caution as there are
dangers in these larger bodies becoming politicized, particularly if they become
financial institutions; and
(i)   the formation of clusters can be expected to start within the first 18-30 months after
the formation of the SHGs. The ACs would come up at a later stage - after 3-4
years.
Roles of CGs/ACs
4.          CGs/ACs can play a variety of functions depending on demand and capacity to
provide effective support in areas ranging from providing a forum for collective action and
promoting awareness building and collective learning to supplying services or acting as a financial
intermediary. Some of the important functions which these bodies can perform can be broadly
categorized as follows:
A. Sharing of Experiences and Collective Action
(i)   collective knowledge building and leadership
(ii)   supporting weak groups
(iii)  solving of inter-group and intra-group disputes/problems
(iv)   promoting new groups
(v)   training of SHG members/leaders in running of SHGs;
(vi)   monitoring SHGs' progress towards empowerment of its members
(vii)  lobbying for policy changes and resource mobilisation (government funds, schemes,
bank funds)
(viii) promoting representation of SHGs in appropriate fora
B. Management of Business Activities and Provision of Technical and Marketing Services
(i)   business activities such as purchase of inputs/raw materials and marketing to
benefit from economies of scale, either directly or through promoting business
associations or producer groups among members in the CG/AC engaged in the same
on-farm or non-farm activity;
(ii)  training in business promotion activities (e.g. market surveys, costing, designing);



-1 11 -
Annex 6
C. Provision of Financial Services1
(i)   audit facilities;
(ii)  inter-group lending of surplus funds generated by some groups to member groups
wishing to borrow additional funds;
(iii)  financial intermediary on behalf of banks, receiving larger loans to be passed on as
smaller loans to the member SHGs; and
(iv)  provision of specific purpose saving and credit services.
D. Provision of Social Services
(i)    management of certain community services (creche, maintenance of drinking water
supply); and
(ii)   provision of social security schemes e.g. life insurance, cattle insurance, health care,
economic security schemes for women.
5.           The roles mentioned in sections B, C and D demand a higher level of expertise than
the roles mentioned in section A. These different roles can be played at either the Cluster or
Association of Cluster level. The role which each CG/AC will perform will be deternined by
demand and the capacity of the human resources available and would depend on:
(i)   strength of the member groups
(ii)   leadership available to the cluster/association of clusters
(iii)  socio-economic conditions in which the SHGs are working
(iv)   vision of the groups and of the NGOs promoting them
(v)   expertise and resources available with the NGOs or the capacity to access resources
Financing of CGs/ACs
6.           The level of costs incurred to maintain a CG/AC depends on the roles performed
which in turn determines the complement of staff which a CG/AC requires. For sustainability,
mechanisms for covering the costs through annual contributions, service fees, or comrnission need
to be established from the outset. However, these charges have to be set at a level which enables
individual SHGs/members to derive a benefit. The roles described in section A can largely be
handled by the CG/AC representatives on a voluntary basis. CGs/ACs acting as financial
intermnediaries, although requiring a higher and more professional level of staffing (2-3 staff
including 1 professional and 1 accountant), have a ready mechanism for cost recovery through the
interest margin. With a volume of lending of Rs. 5 million and an interest margin of 6-9%, such a
CG/AC can be self-sufficient. Careful analysis of the cost of funds and potential lending terms
would be required to be undertaken by any CG/AC intending to act as a financial intermediary to
The project would insist on strict external audit procedures for any CGs/ACs functioning as financial
intermediaries



-112-
Annex 6
ensure that an adequate interest spread would be available to cover costs. CGs/ACs providing
commercial services, such as inputs supply would need to charge commission to cover the costs of
making the arrangements. Covering the costs of technical and social services through charges may
initially be more difficult but there is increasing evidence that SHG members are willing to pay for
services which they find useful.
Role of NGOs in Promoting CGs/ACs
7.          The role of the NGO is very crucial in facilitating the emergence of CGs/ACs.
Although the initial motivation must come from the SHG members, thereafter the SHGs need
support and guidance from the NGOs to operationalize the concept. The orientation of the NGOs is
also important as it is essential that the NGOs have confidence in the SHG members' ability to
promote and maintain cluster and association structures. They must be ready to see that their
functions reduce year by year and the SHGs have freedom to take intiative so that CGs/ACs
become an effective part of a gradual withdrawal strategy.
Project Interventions
8.          Development of Guidelines for the Formation of CGs/ACs The project would
promote the emergence of CGs/ACs on a pilot scale, commencing in those states where existing
mature SHGs are to be inducted into the project, principally UP and Kamataka. This work is
expected to commence during the second year of project implementation. As a prelude to this
process, the CPSU would commission a study by experienced management consultants with
financial expertise to analyse the experience of on-going experiments in the formation of CGs/ACs
in order to establish a set of guidelines for the use of NGOs in assisting the process amongst SHGs
whom they are supporting. The study would focus on the pros and cons of the different roles which
the CGs/ACs can play; the institutional framework including staffing and organizational procedures
required in relation to the different roles; possible financing arrangements to ensure cost recovery
and sustainability; and the inputs required for capacity building of the CGs/ACs including training,
expert guidance and back-up support. Terms of reference for the study are given in Attachment 1.
The guidelines are not intended to be prescriptive but to establish a framework of the essential
organizational features required for the operation of CGs/ACs performing different functions within
which the modus operandi can evolve to meet the specific needs of different groups of SHGs.
9.          Support to Facilitate the Formation of CGs/ACs The Project would provide the
following support to facilitate the formnation of CGs/ACs: - (i) provision of expert guidance through
consultancy inputs from experienced NGOs and/or other agencies in the development of
institutional structures for CGs/ACs and in support of the specific roles which CGs/ACs may wish
to play e.g. undertaking market surveys, designing specific purpose saving scheme; (ii) provision of
orientation and training to the NGOs in the functioning of CGs/ACs; (iii) provision of leadership
training and exposure visits for CGs/ACs leaders; and (iv) support for networking amongst the
CGs/ACs both within and outside of the project. The support provided by the NGOs in the
promotion of CGs/ACs would form part of the overall provision under the project for NGO
support to sustaining SHGs. (As the SHGs mature, the focus of the NGO would switch from the



-113-
Annex 6
individual groups to fostering the management of the CGs/ACs). At the outset, a workshop would
be organized by CPSU for the WDCGs and participating NGOs in the states which will be
launching the experiment (with other WDCGs as observers), drawing on the resources of NGOs'
nationwide with experience of CGs/ACs.
10.         Learning from Implementation Experience As CGs/ACs are a recent
phenomenon emerging in the last 5-8 years, little documentation is available on the process. As the
project would generate valuable experience, emphasis would be placed on documenting the
processes as part of the overall M&E system. A data base on all CGs/ACs promoted under the
project would be maintained. Possible areas of documentation would include:
(i)   background of the SHGs in terms of age of groups, membership composition,
motivation for SHG formation, and systems built up within the groups;
(ii)  the process of cluster formation and association formation, difficulties faced and
how they were tackled;
(iii)  roles performed by the CGs/ACs;
(iv)   organizational structures of the CGs/ACs - division of labor, accountability and
decision making;
(v)   role of facilitating organization in training/exchanges; other human resource
development, support in kind, linkages, finance; and
(vi)  costs of promotion and building of sustainable CGs/ACs.
I1.         The experiences of participating NGOs and other NGOs nationwide involved in
sponsoring CGs/ACs would be drawn upon and the experiences would be shared in inter-state
workshops. An advisory committee would be set up to coordinate the program with membership
from the CPSU; lead M&E agency; each WDC participating in this experiment; 3 representatives
(in rotation) from NGOs sponsoring CGs/ACs under the project, 3 representatives (in rotation)
from other NGOs nationwide with experience of CGs/ACs e.g. Leads (Trichey, Kerala), Chaitanya
(Pune, Maharashtra), Self-Employed Women's Association (SEWA, Ahmedabad), Mysore
Rehabilitation and Development Agency (MYRADA, Tamil Nadu), Professional Assistance for
Development Action (PRADAN - Madurai, Tamil Nadu); and representatives of CGs/ACs. An
initial workshop would be held to launch the documentation process. This workshop should
conclude with an action plan for each state. Organizations which may be interested in documenting
these experiences would include Friends of Women's World Banking (FWWB), Banker's Institute
for Rural Development (BIRD) and SPARC.

Attachment
STUDY TO DEVELOP GUIDELINES FOR THE FORMATION OF CLUSTERS OF SHGs
AND ASSOCIATIONS OF SHG CLUSTERS
TERMS OF REFERENCE
1.          The study would be based on a thorough analysis of the experiences gained to
date by the NGOs who are involved in the formation of Clusters of SHGs (CGs) and
Associations of Clusters of SHGs (ACs). It would involve in-depth discussions with the
sponsoring NGOs and the members/representatives of the CGs and ACs and analysis of their
records and accounts. The objective of the study would be to develop a set of guidelines on the
key institutional factors which need to be taken into account in forming CGs and ACs in order to
ensure their organizational and financial sustainability.
2.          The key issues to be addressed would include:
(a)    a clear analysis of the lessons to be learned from the on-going experiments,
highlighting in particular the mistakes which have been made to ensure that
repetition is avoided;
(b)   analysis and detailed recommendations describing the organizational structures
required by CGs/ACs performing different functions including drawing up of
draft constitutions to fit different scenarios; eligibility criteria for SHGs for
membership; staff required and their expertise and likely levels/methods of
remuneration; organizational procedures required including election/selection of
representatives/committee members, length of tenure, procedures for appointment
of paid staff, and operational management procedures;
(c)   analysis and recommendations on financing arrangements for CGs/ACs
performing different functions to ensure that the organizations can be self-
financing;
(d)   details of the inputs required to assist the formation and growth of CGs/ACs; and
(e)   evaluation of the need for and options for registration of such associations,
highlighting the pros and cons of the different Acts under which such bodies
could be registered.



-115-
Annex 7
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
MOBILIZATION OF FUNDS BY SHGs
A. Credit and the Rural Poor
1.          Despite the vast expansion of the formal credit structure in India, the dependence
of the rural poor on moneylenders continues,1 especially for meeting emergency credit
requirements. Some of the major causes lie in the difficulties formal institutions face in dealing
effectively and economically with a large number of small borrowers who require credit
frequently and in small quantities; limitations imposed by the legal framework on their
operations and bankers' perceptions of the risks and creditworthiness of these borrowers. Such
dependence is even more pronounced in the case of women in view of the additional constraints
imposed by gender discrimination. The major social factors which hinder women's access to
formal credit include their low level of literacy and numeracy, their general lack of exposure and
contact with the world outside their immediate environs, unfamiliarity with bureaucratic
procedures and the unsympathetic attitudes of bank functionaries. Women's access is also
limited by high borrower transaction costs and lack of collateral. Women's demand for credit
requires a system which allows them to engage in risk-averse, multi-production strategies,
provides for small loans with the minimum of procedures and allows repayment of loans in
frequent, small installments.
2.           To overcome these hurdles, thrift and credit Self-Help Groups (SHGs) have been
promoted, particularly among women.2 These groups initially draw on their own accumulated
savings to provide loans principally to meet consumption and emergency needs, thereby
removing women from exploitation by moneylenders. As a credit delivery system, SHGs bring
the benefits of flexibility and responsiveness to the needs of the borrower. They act as both
moneylender, advancing small loans quickly, and development banker, providing small amounts
of production credit, whilst following the principles of participation and mutual cooperation.
SHGs enable women to gain experience of managing credit and to leam credit discipline.
However, the ability of SHGs to meet the financial needs of their members rapidly outgrows the
limitations of their own resources. In recognition of this, the National Bank for Agriculture and
Rural Development (NABARD) has spearheaded systems to link the SHGs with the formal
credit system.
Around 63% of households depend on informal sources of credit - these are predominantly poor, non-
cultivator households - All India Debt and Investment Survey (1981-82)
2  Details on the functioning of SHGs are given in Annex 4



-116-
Annex 7
B. Role of SHGs in Accessing Institutional Credit
3.           After initial hesitancy, the formal financial sector is gradually recognizing the
importance of linking SHGs with their own operations. Linking SHGs with banks has been
shown to be a cost-effective, transparent and flexible approach to improve the accessibility of
credit from the formal banking system to the unreached rural poor. Lending to SHGs offers the
banks a much needed solution to the twin problems being faced by banks, viz., recovery of loans
in rural areas and the high transaction costs of dealing with small borrowers at frequent intervals.
The provision of credit through SHGs reduces the direct transaction costs of the banks by 40-60
percent as a major task of appraisal, supervision and recovery of the loan is taken over by the
group itself. However, the major contributory factor is the improvement in the recovery rate.
Repayment rates of loans to SHGs are around 95 percent compared with recovery rates on
individual loans to the target group under the poverty alleviation programs such as the Integrated
Rural Development Program (IRDP) of 30-50 percent. As shown in Attachment 1, the total
transaction costs of the banks (including default risk) are reduced from 34 percent when lending
to individuals to 11 percent when lending to SHGs.3
4.           The transaction costs for the borrowers are also substantially reduced through
being able to access credit in the village without having to make several time consuming
journeys to the bank branch. Studies have indicated the overall reduction in the borrower's
transaction costs to be 85 percent.
C. NABARD Initiative
5.           Pilot program. To foster this approach, NABARD launched a pilot program in
1992 for linking the SHGs with the banks. This followed the issue of instructions by the
Reserve Bank of India (RBI) in July 1991 which allow banks to finance informal and
unregistered groups without collateral whilst giving SHGs the liberty to decide their own terms
and conditions for financial transactions. Banks are increasingly getting involved in financing
SHGs. The pilot program achieved considerable success and has covered around 5,000 groups as
against the original target of 500 SHGs The repayment performance of the loans taken by the
groups has been more than 95 percent. Eighty percent of the groups were formed by women.
6.           Future directions. Encouraged by the success of the pilot program, RBI has
made financing of SHGs by the banks a part of their general priority sector lending. Banks are
being encouraged by RBI to treat the linkage program as a business opportunity for reaching the
rural poor and to make it part of their corporate strategy. The guidelines issued in April 1996
formalise some of the innovations which have been developed during the pilot phase to simplify
procedures. In particular, the guidelines make provision for banks to provide cash credit limits4
3 'Transaction Costs of Lending to the Rural Poor; Non-governmental organizations and Self-help Groups of the
poor as intermediaries for banks in India': V. Puhazhendhi, The Foundation for Development Cooperation in
collaboration with NABARD, 1994
Cash credit arrangements are a standard banking practice



-117-
Annex 7
to the SHGs - a system which has been pioneered by the UP Land Development Corporation
under the Sodic Lands Reclamation Project. The guidelines also remove many of the doubts in
the minds of bankers and set proper procedures and documentation for SHG financing.
Adherence to the service area is not mandatory and SHGs/NGOs may deal with a branch other
than the service area branch. The instructions issued by RBI clarify the situation regarding
defaulters, assuring the banks that the defaults by a few members of SHGs and/or their family
members to the financing banks should not ordinarily come in the way of financing the SHG,
provided the SHG itself is not in default to the bank. However, the bank loan may not be utilized
by the SHG for financing a defaulter member to the bank. NABARD's support for training of
banks' staff as well as 100 percent refinancing has been assured. The RBI instructions encourage
the banks (commercial banks, regional rural banks and cooperative banks5) to step up their credit
to the unreached rural poor by extensively using the SHG route.
D. Lessons Learnt and Continuing Constraints
7.           The experience to date indicates that SHGs provide an effective mechanism for
demonstrating that the poor are bankable. The repayment rate on bank loans of over 90 percent
indicates that SHGs have the ability to manage credit and to impose credit discipline. The credit
and recovery performance of SHGs are better, as loans availed by members are self-chosen and
small-size manageable loans; credit is available in a timely manner; and loans availed by
members are need-based. At the same time, the close-knit nature of the group ensures proper
utilization of loans and peer pressure for repayment.
8.           However, despite the clear indications of success, a number of constraints still
remain. Despite the issue of guidelines and circulars, information does not always filter down to
the bank branches and many branch managers are still unaware of the scheme or do not fully
understand the flexibility it provides in lending to SHGs. Hence, continued effort is required to
orient both senior level bank management, particularly Regional Managers, and branch managers
and to train them in the provisions and procedures of the scheme. Even where bankers are aware
of the scheme, there is a need to instill confidence in the mechanism through facilitating contact
with existing SHGs which have been successfully linked with banks.
E. Project Interventions
9.           Mobilization of Funds. The project would assist SHG members to mobilize
funds for consumption and investment through inculcating the habit of savings and helping the
groups to access external sources of funds to augment their own resources in order to be able to
meet the needs of their members. To this end, the SHGs would be linked with banks to access
credit at market rates of interest preferably using the simplified flexible procedures offered by the
cash credit limit arrangements.
5  The participation of cooperative banks depends on the provision of the state cooperative laws/rules in regard to
financing informal groups and at present only Bihar amongst the participating states has this provision.



-1 18-
Annex 7
10.         Capacity Building. (i) SHGs. Since the ability of the SHGs to access credit
from lending institutions rests on demonstrating their creditworthiness, the project would assist
the groups to establish a track record for sound financial management of their savings and credit
operations through provision of training in credit management and maintenance of accounts and
ensuring regular audits of the SHGs' accounts. (ii) NGOs. The project would strengthen the
capacity of the NGOs to support the SHGs through training in credit management and the
operating procedures of SHGs; and in appropriate simplified accounting systems to be
transmitted to the SHGs and through exposure visits to successful SHGs. (iii) Banks. Since
proper motivation and orientation of regional managers and branch managers is essential to
bank-SHG linkage, the project would fund one workshop per year for senior bank management
in each state to provide orientation to the SHG concept. For branch managers participating in the
project, orientation and review workshops would be held each year on a district basis. In
addition, both regional and branch managers would participate in exposure visits to successful
SHGs in order to instill confidence in the SHG mechanism. Provision is also made for two
international study tours per year per state for the best performing staff of the participating banks
and NABARD. Since NABARD has developed considerable expertise over the last four years in
propagating the concept of SHG financing by the banks, NABARD would be responsible for the
training of bank staff under the project.
11.         Special studies. Broadening of the savings base of the SHGs. At present,
members of SHGs have no direct access to their savings and in many SHGs, savings
contributions per member are fixed at a uniform rate. A study would be commissioned as part of
the M&E system to examine the possibilities for broadening the savings base to allow members
to have direct access to their savings and to make additional savings contributions when they
have surplus funds and to allow the SHGs to accept savings/deposits from non-members.
Detailed consideration would need to be given to administrative arrangements, accounting
procedures, non-contravention of Banking Regulations Act, ways of protecting savers and likely
impacts on the cohesion of the SHG.
F. Implementation Arrangements
12.         Management of credit funds. The SHGs' savings and credit operations would
commence with generation of funds from the members' savings and internal loaning based on
these funds in order to establish a track record for sound credit management. Approximately 6-
12 months after formation, efforts would be made to link SHGs with a bank through establishing
cash credit limit arrangements. This system enables groups to establish a line of credit from the
bank up to an agreed limit, initially equivalent to their own savings, which they can draw on as
required for consumption and/or other purposes. This approach provides group members easy
access to bank credit with the minimum of processing formalities and restrictions on the use of
funds. The cash credit limit is gradually raised as a proportion of the group's savings, generally
up to a limit of 4 times, in line with the improving credit record of the group. In the UP
experience, SHGs have on average been sanctioned cash credit limits after the group has been in
existence for nine months. To simplify the procedures, the banks have sanctioned telescopic
limits on the basis of thirty six months savings and in a ratio of 1:4 and the banks revise the



-119-
Annex 7
withdrawal limit on a quarterly basis. In this way repeated revisions of the documentation are
avoided which minimizes expenditure and inconvenience to the group members. The cash credit
system is more beneficial to the groups than a term loan as the group only pays interest on the
amount of credit which it draws whilst with a term loan, the group is liable to pay interest on the
whole amount sanctioned irrespective of whether the group is able to utilize the money
immediately or not. Experience from UP indicates that interaction with bank staff on an
individual basis would be beneficial in encouraging the participation of banks in financing SHGs
and expediting adoption of the cash credit limit arrangements. Over time the group may decide
to switch to a group term loan or it may prefer to continue with the cash credit limit
arrangements.
13.          Wherever SHGs are unable to access this facility from a bank, they may be
provided with funds from other financial institutions provided that the terms and conditions are
consistent with market interest rates charged to the SHGs and the SHGs able to deternine the
interest rate to be charged to their members. Other possible lending institutions would include
FWWB, NABARD's provision of bulk loans to NGOs for on-lending to SHGs, RMK (once its
lending terms are appropriate 6) or other non-banking financial institutions.
14.          Credit requirement under the project. The total credit required to be provided
by the lending institutions under the project would be determined by the number of groups and
the savings capacity of the SHGs. An indicative profile of a typical SHG on which the
calculations have been based is given in Attachment 2. Whilst the project would not be target
driven, for planning purposes it has been estimated that around 7,400 SHGs are likely to be
formed during the project period. This number will include some existing groups, particularly in
UP and Karnataka which are already linked to the banks. Estimation of aggregate savings is
based on 20 members per SHG saving Rs. 15/month or Rs. 3,600/ SHG/year. The cash credit
facility is assumed to be accessed after nine months, matching the group's savings of Rs. 2,700 at
this point. The cash credit limit as a ratio of the savings is assumed to increase each year up to a
ratio of 1:4 in the fourth year. With the increase in the groups own resources (savings + net
interest earnings), the amount of credit which the group can access is estimated at around Rs.
100,000 in the fifth year. It is assumed that 90 percent of the SHGs formed in Years 1-4 would
be linked to a lending institution by the end of the project period. This profile of loans to the
SHGs has been used, taking into account the age profile of the groups, to estimate the aggregate
credit requirements under the project. This is estimated at Rs. 295 million (see Attachment 3 for
the state-wise and year-wise breakdown).
15.          Credit terms and conditions. Loans provided by the banks to the SHGs would
be based on the bank's assessment of the creditworthiness of the group and its track record in
6   The present constitution of RMK does not allow SHGs the flexibility to determine interest rates but requires
RMK funds to be lent to the final borrower at an interest rate not exceeding 12%.
7 The likely spread between the states is Bihar - 600, Gujarat - 1,200, Haryana - 600, Karnataka - 1,200, Madhya
Pradesh - 1,000 and Uttar Pradesh - 2,800.
8   Calculations for UP and Haryana are based on 15 members per SHG and monthly savings of Rs. 15 per
member.



-120-
Annex 7
credit management. The lending institutions would be encouraged to provide cash credit
arrangements to SHGs. Lending terms and conditions to the final borrower would be determined
by the SHG, including the decision to finance a particular member, the purpose of loan, loan
amount, repayment period and interest rate. The appraisal of the beneficiary and the activity,
utilization and recovery of loans to members are the sole responsibility of the SHG. The bank
would be free to set its own interest on loans to SHGs. The SHG would determine the interest
rate charged on loans to its members. Most SHGs charge interest rates of 2-3 percent per month.
Any policy changes relevant to this project resulting from the Bank's on-going dialogue with
GOI on the rural sector reform program would be made applicable to the project's credit
activities.
D. Profile of SHG Savings and Credit Operations
16.         The profile of the model SHG given in Attachment 3 indicates the viability of an
SHG's savings and credit operations. It is assumed from Year 2 that the SHG will be fully
responsible for all the costs of its operations, including payment to a person to maintain the
accounts if this cannot be done by a group member and purchase of ledgers and other stationery.
These costs have been estimated at Rs. 600 per year. The analysis shows that with net interest
earnings in the second year of Rs. 1, 330 the group is easily able to cover these costs. By the
fifth year, net interest earnings are of the order of Rs. 10,000 per year. By the end of five years,
the average amount of credit available per member is around Rs. 6,000 and the average equity
capital per member amounts to over Rs. 1,300.
17.         Key features of SHGs' lending operations. Based on past experience with
SHGs in India, it is anticipated that the initial uses of credit would be for consumption, social and
health needs. This slowly shifts towards productive investment. The limited available data
indicate clearly defined trends in the pattern of lending by SHGs although the dimensions vary
considerably between groups depending on their circumstances. A broad-based study conducted
by NABARD revealed that while in the first year around 70-80 percent of the SHG credit goes
for consumption purposes and the remaining 20-30 percent for production and investment needs,
by the third year the position is reversed with consumption credit forming only 20-30 percent and
credit for productive purposes increasing to 70-80 percent.9 Data available on a sample of the
MYRADA sponsored groups indicates a similar trend with loans for consumption decreasing
from 46 percent of the number of loans granted (for SHGs less than 2 years old) to 26 percent
(for SHGs 3-5 years old). In terms of the total value of loans, the corresponding reduction in the
share of consumption was from 20 percent to 12 percent (see Attachment 4). SHGs rotate their
savings between 1 and 2 times per year but the figure can be as high as four times. For purposes
of analysis of SHG operations under the project, a conservative set of assumptions have been
used. These comprise: (i) the percentage of lending from the group generating incremental
economic benefits increases from 15 percent to 40 percent as the group matures; (ii) the rotation
of investment funds decreases from twice to 1.5 times per year; and (iii) 85 percent of the value
of production loans represents on-farm activities and 15 percent is for non-farm activities.
NABARD Quick Study on SHGs in Tamil Nadu, Karnataka and Andhra Pradesh, 1994



-12 1-
Annex 7
Comprehensive data on the lending operations of the SHGs would be collected by the M&E
system to improve the data base on group behavior.
18.          On the basis of the experience of existing groups, initial production loans are for
traditional oN-farm activities such as inputs like seeds, fertilizers, pesticides and implements;
livestock, particularly sheep and goats, and sericulture. Traditional off-farm activities would
include trading, shops and traditional handicrafts. The real diversification into higher income
generating activities would only come after 2-3 years and for this to take place on any significant
scale, past experience indicates that there is a need for other technical assistance inputs such as
awareness and entrepreneurship and skill development training.
