-127-
Annex 8
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
WOMEN'S PARTICIPATION IN ON-FARM ACTIVITIES
Strategy
1.          Experience of existing SHGs indicates that over 50 percent of the credit is used for
agriculture - for improved inputs (seeds, fertilizer), and small livestock. Investments in on-farm
activities would target three categories of women: (a) landless women with access to small
amounts of land around the homestead; (b) women with access to both homestead and field land
who are currently engaged in subsistence production; and (c) women with land who are already
engaged in cash crop production.
2.          Women would be assisted first to consolidate the food security base of the
household and then embark on a growth path through improvements in crop and animal
productivity and onto diversification. This work would begin with activities that increase the
sustainability of predominantly rainfed agriculture through the introduction of improved land and
water conservation activities. As group members progressively wish to optimize returns from
limited resources, extension support would be provided to introduce activities with potential for
greater economic realization such as cultivation of herbs, spices, fruits, vegetables and
floriculture. To minimize risk, only known technologies, which the women and their families
have been unable to access, would be promoted. Attempts would also be made to introduce the
women into market processes and other activities, e.g., storage, grading and packing, that add
value to the basic agricultural cycle.
3.          Livestock activities would focus on sheep and goats in small units which can be
easily managed by women alongside their other work. Necessary support would include the
dissemination of improved feeding practices and greater emphasis on fodder production and
animal health.
4.          To improve the terms on which women participate in markets, the project would
encourage the formation, later in the life of the project, of inter-SHG commodity
groups/producer associations focused on the production, processing and marketing of one
product and its by-products with SHGs taking responsibility for the running costs. Women
would also be introduced to new technology which could help to reduce the drudgery involved in
many agricultural tasks and food preparation.
5.          Potential investment opportunities. Given the wide variations in the resource
base across the project states and districts, the specific opportunities open to women will be
location specific . Hence, the project approach for land-based activities will necessarily have to be
flexible to accommodate local needs. In general, separate lines of action would be required for
semi-arid rainfed and largely rainfed areas (e.g., Panchmahals, Kolar, Chitradurga, and



-128-
Annex 8
Hoshangabad) and for better endowed areas (e.g., Muzuffarpur, Sehore, Sabarkantha and Ranchi).
An indication of possible opportunities in the initial project districts is given below:
(a)   Bihar. In dry areas, emphasis would be on moisture conservation measures;
expanding irrigation through water storage ponds and lift irrigation; improving the
productivity of homestead gardens; small animal management; post-harvesting
value addition; and access to larger markets through group marketing. Wasteland is
available in the hinterland of villages which could provide opportunities for group-
managed horticulture (guava, mango).
(b)   Gujarat. In Panchmahals, the primary action required is in-situ soil and moisture
conservation followed by improving the productivity of the traditional crop cycles
through green manuring, mulching and composting to ensure food security. For
income generation, dryland horticulture (ber/lac, amla, custard apple) with garlic
and ginger production and processing on homesteads and small animal management
for egg and meat production are possible starting points.
(c)   Haryana. Assured moisture in the rainfed areas will allow farmers to tap flower
and vegetable markets in Delhi, in addition to focusing on traditional cereals,
oilseed crops and potatoes. Tapping specialist and non-traditional markets such as
organic produce and cheeses offer other possibilities.
(d)   Karnataka. Most of the project area is characterised by low rainfall, poor
irrigation and inferior degraded soils. Little homestead land is available and initial
effort should be on improvements of field land through soil and water conservation
followed by consolidating the productivity of traditional field crops such as paddy
and millets, and dryland vegetables and horticulture. Linkage with existing
programs for catchment area management will be important. Ready marketing
opportunities offered by proximity to Bangalore provide scope for diversification
into high value crops (floriculture, vegetables, mango, pomegranate, jamun),
product processing (jams, pickles, condiments) and group marketing.
(e)   Madhya Pradesh. Improving productivity of homesteads, enabling access to
markets for minor agricultural produce, and value added activities such as flour
milling, grading and sorting, sun-drying, condiment-making are promising entry
points. In rainfed areas, possibilities exist for vegetable production, mushroom
growing and raising small animals (poultry, rabbits, quails) on homestead land.
(f)    Uttar Pradesh. Home-based value addition through medicinal plants, fodder,
mushroom cultivation and minor horticulture (lime) offer promising entry points.
Group management of wasteland for prosopis plantations for charcoal making
would be a possibility. The presence of the Land Development Corporation as a
partner would allow a more holistic approach to improving land and water resources
before embarking on agriculture and animal husbandry.



-129-
Annex 8
Financial models for a range of indicative activities are given in Annex 18.
Approach
6.          Participatory planning processes. The NGOs would promote inter-active
discussions between the women and the agricultural extension staff to assist women in the
identification of strategies and selection of activities which meet their specific needs. These
exercises would build on women's indigenous knowledge in conjunction with information on
available resources (land, family labor), women's work cycles, seasonal pattern of demand for
women's labor, women's perceptions and priorities, e.g., on food security versus cash income and
concerns regarding their roles within the family and community. Based on these exercises,
prioritised lines of actions addressing women's needs would be developed which would also
identify the support required in terms of training, exposure to technology, access to inputs and
development of market linkages.
7.          Consolidation of soil and water resources. As most of the women have
predominantly rainfed land, assurance of soil moisture would expand the range of opportunities.
The project would support the identification of locally suitable soil and moisture conservation
practices and their implementation with the support from the NGOs and line departments.
Typically, these measures would involve earthen contour bunds, trenching, diversion drains, nala
drains and percolation ponds, requiring labor and materials readily available to the household.
Exposure trips to watershed development projects would be arranged to expand women's
understanding of the impact of such measures.
8.          Improving productivity. To enhance the productivity of homestead lands, the
project would promote such interventions as: (i) soil improvement by green manuring, mulching,
vermicomposting; (ii) bund and border planting of fodder; (iii) hedge-cropping of legumes; (iv)
improved intercropping and crop rotation practices; and (v) promoting new vegetable crops and
small fruit tree cultivation. The lessons could subsequently be tried on the main fields.
9.          Diversification into high value crops. The choices for diversification into high
value crops both on homestead lands and fields would be based on ecological and social
considerations (e.g., impact of cash cropping on women's involvement in management and control
of income) as well as market demand. Activities that could emerge as viable would include
vegetables, floriculture, medicinal plants, herbs and spices. SHGs could consider group leasing of
common or government land for agricultural development, particularly for quick-yielding and
hardy crops like moringa or horticultural crops. The project would support exposure visits to
successful farmers and institutions to view these developments.
10.         Animal improvement and management. As tending livestock is primarily the
women's domain, lending for livestock is popular amongst SHG members, primarily for small
livestock. However, returns from livestock are frequently low due to the low productivity of the
animals and hence women would be assisted to acquire the necessary knowledge and access to



-130-
Annex 8
facilities to make their animals productive. Typical livestock activities are likely to be: (a) goat
rearing indicatively based on three does with a buck shared by a number of women in the SHG; (b)
goat kid fattening indicatively based on 5 kids and involving a 10 month rearing period; (c) lamb
fattening indicatively based on three lambs and involving a three-month fattening period enabling
at least three cycles of fattening to be completed within a year; (d) piggery particular amongst SC
communities; and (e) group poultry units of 500 layer birds in later stages of SHG development. In
the course of the participatory planning process, Animal Husbandry staff would assist the SHGs to
consider the crucial issues of availability of fodder, access to veterinary services, disease
management, marketing outlets and opportunities for value addition through processing. Since
many households may already have dairy cattle, women would be assisted to upgrade the
productivity of their existing animals through improved nutrition, health care and upgradation of
the stock.
11.         Processing of agricultural and animal products. Processing of products would
be promoted to increase storage life of perishable products, increase marketability and add value.
The initial focus would be on simple methods such as grading, sorting and packaging and
upgrading of on-going processing activities in the project areas such as sun-drying, pickling and
condiment-making. Thereafter, the NGOs, with the assistance of specialist inputs, would help
SHGs to identify new processing potentials with traditional and new products. Improved methods
of marketing would also be explored including linkages with urban marketing chain stores to
ensure guaranteed uptake of produce and stable pricing.
12.         Commodity Marketing and Producer Groups. To improve the terms on which
women participate in markets, the project would encourage the formation, later in the life of the
project, of inter-SHG commodity groups/producer associations focused on the production,
processing and marketing of one product and its by-products. The activities of these groups would
include, as appropriate, input management, common storage facilities, grading and packaging,
group marketing, market research and market outlet development, joint market negotiation and
transport organization. As the establishment of these institutions would take place once the SHGs
are mature and have established links with the banks, it is expected that any fixed and working
capital requirements involved would attract loans from the banks. The project would provide
support in the form of consultancy inputs to assess viability and design appropriate institutional
structures for these organizations. (See also Annex 6 which describes the possible role of SHG
clusters or Associations in forming and sustaining such groups)
13.         Technologies for drudgery reduction and increasing efficiency. To increase
women's productivity in routine agricultural/domestic tasks and assist in releasing women's time
for more productive activities, the project would fund demonstrations of implements, equipment
and techniques to increase efficiency and save time devoted to on-farm activities. Sourcing of
available appropriate technologies from various technology development institutes and specialist
NGOs nationwide would be undertaken by the CPSU and information fed to the participating
states.



-131-
Annex 8
Project Inputs
14.         Training. Initial training would be provided to all women in a group taking up
land based, including livestock, activities. Most of the training would be provided in the village.
The line departments would be the main source of training inputs supplemented by outside experts
where necessary. Training requirements would be identified and integrated with the overall
training plan.
15.   Technical Support. To promote the self-reliance of the groups, the project would support
the development of resource persons selected by the groups from amongst their own members or
from within the community to provide technical support to on-farm activities. With villages under
the project selected on a cluster basis, some resource persons would be able to be more specialised,
e.g., in specific crops or horticulture and serve neighbouring villages. Some would be trained as
para-vets to support animal husbandry activities. Intensive initial training, supplemented by annual
refresher training, would be provided to the resource persons in all important, easy-to-understand
technologies. Additional technical support would be provided by the line department extension
staff or by other agencies/consultants, e.g., NGOs who have technical staff, Agricultural
Universities, Krishi Vigan Kendras (KVK) who would be contracted by the WDCs.
16.         The NGOs would regularly compile a list of the services required by the women
in the groups which they are supporting and forward it to the WDC District Project Manager
(DPM) who would be responsible for identifying the appropriate service provider and making
the necessary arrangements for the delivery of the required inputs. For services to be provided
by the local extension staff, a detailed program would be worked out at the Cluster Level
Working Group meetings. The NGO would be responsible for following up with the identified
service provider to ensure the delivery of the required services. Any problems encountered
would be fed back to the DPM to resolve the matter through discussions at the district level or at
the District Project Coordinating Committee meeting.
17.         Demonstrations. On-farm demonstrations of technology packages for different
crops would be conducted by the resource person assisted by the line department field staff. The
demonstrations would be taken up on the fields of women group members and the project would
fund the cost of the inputs required. Demonstrations would focus on the technologies and crops
pertinent to the particular locality. These could include in-situ soil and moisture conservation; crop
production; vegetable/flower cultivation; kitchen gardens; techniques such as green manuring and
composting; fodder production, animal nutrition and animal care. On average it is proposed that
each year one demonstration of each appropriate category would be carried out in a cluster of 10-
20 villages. The first year would be spent in making preparatory arrangements with demonstrations
mounted each year thereafter.
18.        Exposure visits. SHG women, together with the resource persons, would make
visits to successful farmers or institutes to enable them to see the actual application of technologies
and their impact on productivity and income. The project would also support the visit of successful
farmers to the SHG members. These model farmers could also assist in the laying out of



-132-
Annex 8
demonstrations. As the project develops, successful farmers would be identified from amongst the
women participating in the project.
19.        Access to Infrastructure Support Under On-going Programs. Infrastructure
support for activities such as, soil conservation, watershed protection measures, irrigation wells,
and other irrigation schemes would be accessed through drawing on existing government
programmes. To this end the WDCs would prepare an inventory of all the government schemes in
areas of interest to the project. The WDC at the district level would be active in seeking to gain
nomination for project women for the infrastructure required and would seek to obtain a
commitment for an agreed allocation of relevant schemes to women. In states where development
schemes are being implemented through the panchayati raj institutions, SHGs would be helped to
develop synergies with these institutions in order to access these programs.
20.         Advisory Services. The project would assist SHG members with necessary advice
and guidance in the implementation of new initiatives, e.g., formation of producers associations or
other involvements in processing and marketing, through contracting short term inputs from
appropriate experts.
Potential Service Providers
21.         The technical line departments would be the principal providers of training and
other technical support services to women undertaking on-farm activities. The principal
departments involved and their responsibilities are outlined below.
22.         Agriculture. The Agricultural Department Extension Worker (or other designated
service provider) would be responsible for: (a) engaging in participatory assessments of the
resource base available to women, and their perceptions and priorities, identifying crop options and
providing guidance to women in the selection of appropriate crops; (b) training women taking up
crop production activities; (c) training resource persons and providing continued access to new
information through incorporation into the regular extension network; (d) providing technical
details and guidance on laying of demonstration plots and ensuring provision of required inputs on
time; (e) facilitating access to recommended varieties of seed if not freely available on the market;
(f) visiting women farmers at key times to review progress of their crops and provide additional
technical guidance; and (g) organising group action for marketing where appropriate.
23.         Horticulture. The Horticulture Department (or other designated service
provider) would be responsible for: (a) engaging in participatory assessments of the resource base
available to women, and their perceptions and priorities, identifying crop options and providing
guidance to women in the selection of appropriate crops; (b) training women taking up
horticulture activities; (c) training resource persons; (d) providing technical details and guidance
on laying of demonstration plots; (e) providing technical guidance on plantation establishment
including digging of pits, spacing, planting of seedlings, etc.; (f) providing guidance in the
establishment of nurseries or facilitating access to good quality planting material for timely
planting; (g) training women in in-situ budding (if appropriate) or facilitating access to services



-133-
Annex 8
of trained technicians at the correct time; (h) visiting women's activities at key times to review
progress of their crops and provide technical guidance, e.g., at time of pruning, spraying, pest
control, post harvest handling at the farm level; and (i) organizing group action for marketing.
24.         Land Development. The Soil Conservation Department (or other designated
provider of services) would be responsible for: (a) surveying women's land and drawing up plan of
treatments required in collaboration with the owners; (b) participatory budgeting exercises ; (c)
training resource persons in the use of A-frames for laying contour key-lines; for the purpose of
construction of contour bunds, graded bunds, contour vegetative hedges, etc.; (d) guiding women in
the establishment of nursery for vegetative material; and (e) regularly supervising the progress of
work undertaken by the women;
25.         Irrigation. The Irrigation Department (or other designated provided of services)
would be responsible for: (a) surveying and determining appropriate location of well or other
irrigation system; (b) providing guidance on implementation of construction; (c) regularly
supervising the progress of work; and (d) providing advice on procurement of materials, pump, etc.
26.          Animal Husbandry. The Animal Husbandry Department (or other service
provider) would be responsible for: (a) advising women on appropriate livestock enterprises and
orienting them to the need to ensure access to adequate feed resources; (b) train women in livestock
management, health care, and feed regimes; (c) train some members of the SHGs as para-vets; and
(d) provide regular animal health care services to women.






-135-
Annex 9
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
SUPPORT FOR NON-FARM INCOME GENERATING ACTIVITIES
A. Objectives
1.          The objective of this component of the project is to raise the level of women's
involvement in non-farm activities from subsistence to more profitable levels; facilitate new
entrants in starting income generation activities; and facilitate skill and capacity building including
the development of abilities in decision making and management of resources. Women will have
different priorities in taking up non-farm activities ranging from those viewing an income
generating activity as a supplementary source of income to those with the ability to establish a more
complex micro-enterprise activity as a full time occupation and primary source of income, requiring
a higher level of business skills. Assistance provided under the project would need to
accommodate these diverse needs.
B. Constraints
2.          Past efforts to promote non-farm activities have frequently failed as the selection of
activities has been production, rather than market-driven, and training has frequently been provided
in isolation from linkages with finance, production technology and market opportunities. Other
constraints experienced include the difficulty of identifying financially viable activities; inadequate
processes for training potential entrepreneurs particularly from amongst poor women; confusion of
welfare and business objectives on the part of facilitators; lack of regular support to entrepreneurs
after the initial launch of the activity and weaknesses in technology transfer, product development
and marketing. Particular factors constraining the promotion of non-farm activities amongst poor
women include:
(a)   Women have problems in getting access to credit due to lack of ownership of land
and property that can be used as collateral. Timely availability and adequacy of
finance are critical.
(b)   Low levels of literacy.
(c)   Lack of knowledge with regard to bookkeeping and accounts.
(d)   Rigid mind sets, paternalistic behavior and lack of gender sensitivity of the line
agencies. Hence the need to sensitize them to gender and poverty issues.



-136-
Annex 9
(e)   Lack of familiarity with the external environment beyond the village thereby
limiting mobility.
(f)    Lack of confidence in dealing with officials in the districts and in sector-based
corporations resulting in poor access to existing infrastructure.
(g)   Most of the women in the target group are at or below subsistence level. There is no
room for making incorrect choices as the costs of failure are very high. Hence the
importance of appropriate guidance from the initial stages of the program.
(h)   In cases where the raw material is provided to groups of women and the product is
marketed by an intervening agency, the women are far from empowered:
(i)    The women remain at the level of wage Labour and perhaps graduate to
piece rate work.
(ii)   Payment levels are extremely low and there is a sense of exploitation as well
as dependency.
(iii)  The agency may not have marketing know-how and the women are blamed
for inventory pile-up.
(iv)   There is no link between the women and the customer so no feed back for
the women producers regarding quality/market need.
(v)   Complete dependency of the women's groups on the agency.
Hence the importance of providing entrepreneurship training and skills to the self-
help groups.
(i)    NGOs may not have skills in identifying appropriate activities for income
generation. Hence the need to provide this orientation as well as involve
organizations/individuals with entrepreneurship skills as resource agencies for
business identification, market surveys, skill provision, follow-up and back-up
trouble shooting.
(j)   If credit is taken for developing new skills or activities without ensuring adequate
market demand the risk is high.
C. Approach
3.           To counteract these weaknesses, the approach adopted under the project emphasizes
the identification of potentially profitable activities in a specific location based on raw materials,
existing skills and market opportunities specific to that location. Non-farm activities would be



-137-
Annex 9
selected by the women, based on individual choice. Production could be at the individual or group
level. Selection would be from a wide range of production, trading and service activities. The
project would expose the women to a range of market determined traditional and non-traditional
income generating activities through participatory action around a menu of options of feasible
economic activities.
4.           Experience indicates that it is easier to build on existing skills and familiar
products than to introduce new products/activities and develop new skills. Similarly, it is easier
for the target group to market their goods locally than seek marketing opportunities in distant
urban markets. Hence, the identification process should follow a sequential approach, starting
with examining the scope for the easiest activities/products to be promoted, based on existing
skills and local resources and markets, before moving towards the more difficult areas of the
introduction of new products and new skills. Such an approach would suggest the following
hierarchy of activities for analysis: (a) expansion of traditional activities in the project area
which offer growth prospects; (b) identification of existing products for which there is a
deficiency of supply in local markets; and (c) identification of new products in fast growing
sectors so that production from the project area is fulfilling incremental demand rather than
competing with existing producers who have a head start.
5.           The activities in which rural women are typically engaged can be divided into six
broad groups of activities.
(a)    Trading activities such as vending vegetables, fish, milk, and fruits, breaking bulk
and grading or sorting.
(b)    Activities based on traditional skills and using local raw materials such as leaf plate
making, brooms, embroidery, bead work, making ropes, mats, bamboo products,
laces, pandal making, durrie weaving, fabric weaving, pottery, etc.
(c)    Production and trade in processed foods such as parboiled rice, jams, jellies, fruit
bars, papads, honey, bread, biscuits, snacks, tamarind powder, spices, salt, etc.
(d)   Non-traditional activities such as making cards, notebooks, files, envelopes and
raising broiler chickens.
(e)    Traditional service activities such as running small hotels, provision stores and tea
stalls or hiring out bicycles or utensils.
(f)    Skill-based non-traditional service activities such as hand pump repair, radio repair,
running STD booths, cycle repair, photography, plumbing, welding, zip repair, etc.
6.           Substantial improvement in the returns from any of these activities is possible with
marginal interventions. For instance, women in trading activities such as vegetable vending could
benefit substantially from access to timely and adequate credit on commercial terms. Credit



-138-
Annex 9
available from the SHG at even 24 percent would represent a significant benefit given that the local
moneylender charges as much as 120 percent. Similarly linkages could be established with larger
markets thereby enabling higher net returns. Access to credit for purchase of a bicycle or push cart
would reduce the drudgery of carrying head loads to the market and increase the number of villages
covered.
7.           Similarly women using traditional skills in embroidery, leaf plate making, durrie
weaving, pottery, etc., could benefit from interventions pertaining to design and color
combinations, thread quality, technology and tools and marketing as also from the availability of
cheaper credit than moneylenders. FoodprQ.oceing activities would benefit from interventions
that improve marketability through quality control, brand name, licenses, measuring and testing
instruments and a clean production environment or common production facility center. Services
such as hand pump repair, cycle repair, plumbing, etc., would need skill development training and,
subsequently, on the job apprenticeships followed by credit to enable purchase of the necessary
tools and possibly work spaces.
8.           Financial models have been prepared for nine representative activities. If the credit
intervention is for existing non-farm activities the initial returns are high. If the credit intervention
is for a new activity without determining marketing conditions and linkages, the risk is high.
Income levels for women in non-farm activities vary from as low as Rs. 15 per day to as high as Rs.
75 or more per day depending on the activity and the area under consideration.
D. Project Interventions
9.           Project interventions to support non-farm activities would comprise the following
sequential steps:
(a) Development and Operationalisation of Strategy for Support to Non-Farm
Activities
* Given the weakness of the support structure for non-farm activities in most states, the
project would contract agencies with recognized expertise in entrepreneurship and
business development to develop the overall strategy for support to non-farm activities,
identify local agencies/individuals capable of providing the necessary support and
develop their capacity to transfer business, technical and marketing skills to SHGs.
This input would comprise:
(i)    Organization of a visioning-cum-planning workshop at the state level to
develop the overall strategy and approach for the promotion of non-farm
activities under the project. Participants in this brainstorming exercise
would be the WDC, partner NGOs and agencies within the state with
experience in non-farm activities;



-139-
Annex 9
(ii)  Identification of resource agencies/NGOs/individuals capable of providing
support in the various aspects required, e.g., entrepreneurship development.
In addition to identifying state level resources, this exercise would be carried
out for each project district. This would also include orientation to project
implementors at the district level to the overall strategy and approach to the
promotion of non-farm activities under the project.
(iii)  Entrepreneurship sensitization for NGOs (see below) and other support
agencies through organization of a 4-day workshop for NGOs and 2-day
workshop for other support agencies. The objective of the workshops would
be to sensitize and develop an understanding among the NGOs about
entrepreneurship and the support they would need to provide to the groups
or individual women in the process of setting up any economic activity.
(iv)  Identification of resource agencies to provide vocational skills training in
key areas (as indicated by the techno-economic studies) and interaction with
EDP agencies to tailor entrepreneurship development training programs to
match local needs;
(v)   Identification of best practitioners, i.e., successful entrepreneurs in relevant
income generating activities in the project districts willing to provide back-
up support to women producers and networking them with the NGOs and
SHGs.
(vi)   Conduct of model EDP in each district for the trainers of the identified EDP
training agencies, NGOs, other local resource persons and representative
women producers from the SHGs. The objective would be to orient and
guide the trainers in the implementation of EDPs geared to the specific
needs of mainly illiterate women.
(vii)  Follow-up, including review of the performance of strategy implementation,
development and operationalization of mechanisms for scaling up income
generating activities when they plateau and provision of back-stopping
support to resolve particular problems, both at the macro-level and in
relation to specific women producers.
* The WDCs would have the option of contracting agencies with recognized expertise
within the state to provide these services or contracting experienced national agencies.
Two national level agencies with the appropriate expertise whose resources could be
used are the Asian Center for Entrepreneurial Initiatives (ACE) based in Bangalore and
the International Center for Entrepreneurship and Career Development (ICECD) based
in Ahmedabad. Terms of Reference are given in Attachment 1.



-140-
Annex 9
(b) Techno-economic surveys
*  Techno-economic surveys would be conducted at the village level for each of the
districts identified for the project. These surveys would start with the identification of
existing local skills and resources and determine their marketability. The surveys would
also grade the locally available skills, identify methods to upgrade them and determine
the interventions necessary to make them marketable in traditional and non-traditional
markets.
*  In addition, the surveys would provide information on the population of the village, the
age profile, gender classification, literacy and numeracy levels, existing skills,
occupational distribution, income levels, non-farm activities, turover levels, major raw
materials used, raw materials available, land-use pattern, natural resources available,
consumption pattern, availability of electricity and other amenities, commercial and
educational infrastructure, markets in and around the village, frequency of haats,
existence of road and rail networks, type and frequency of transport facilities, etc.
Terms of Reference are given in Attachment 1.
(c) Market Surveys
* Market surveys would be undertaken for the products identified by the techno-economic
surveys on the basis of the availability of skills and local resources. These surveys
would focus on analyzing market demand, identifying marketing channels and outlets,
developing a marketing strategy, identifying market segments, positioning the product
in the market and pricing it appropriately. The surveys would encompass the whole
range of potential market outlets appropriate to the concerned products which may
include departmental stores in cities, government run emporia and corporations, retail
outlets, petty traders, occasional fairs and exhibitions. Particular attention would be
given to local haats which provide a low cost and regular means of marketing products
in rural areas. Consideration would be given to interventions that can help women
access haats and reduce the drudgery of getting and trading there. These could include
provision of bus services to and from haats held in nearby villages and towns,
construction of cement platforms and thatched roofs or tarpaulin shelters to reduce the
impact of rain and sun and spoilage of produce, construction of public toilets and
construction of godowns for safe storage of unsold stocks. Terms of Reference for
market surveys are given in Attachment 1.
(d) Feasibility Studies for Specific Products/Activities
* Where necessary, specialist agencies would be contracted to undertake more detailed
feasibility studies for specific products/activities. This is likely to apply to products
which are widely adopted by women, those whose viability may be marginal and new
products.



-141-
Annex 9
(e) Menu of Options
* Based on the techno-economic and market surveys, the menu of options of feasibility
activities for each locality would be drawn up. Activities would be divided into
permanent, seasonal, short duration, etc. to assist women in choosing activities to meet
their needs. Menu cards for the activities would be prepared giving information on
investment, skills required, minimum scale of operation, aptitude required,
minimum/maximum number of days operation per year, net surplus, and return per
manday. The menu of options and menu cards would be central to the participatory
process of selection of income generating activities by the women.
(f) Entrepreneurship and skill development
* Once women have selected their activity, their requirement for skill development and
entrepreneurship training would be assessed. While standard Entrepreneurship
Development Programs (EDPs) are conducted by several agencies, work with
grassroots groups needs the design and development of specific training programs in
opportunity sensing, business feasibility and business management skills for the key
persons in the groups. Such a program will help the women identify suitable product
choices and help them set up and run the business activity satisfactorily. The
complexity of the entrepreneurship training required will vary with the nature of the
activity taken up. Many women, who are essentially up-grading existing activities,
would only require a 3-5 day course covering basic business concepts, accounts, product
costing and calculation of margins, and marketing strategies including practical
exercises to study the local market demand and marketing channels for their products.
Others taking up a more complex micro-enterprise would require a more intensive
training of around 3-4 weeks duration including, in addition to a more detailed study of
the above subjects, awareness of registration processes and regulations affecting the
running of a business. During these entrepreneurship development programs, the
trainees would be required to conduct market studies for their proposed activities to
assess the size of their markets and the possible channels for marketing their products.
In the initial stages until the women are able to travel to nearby towns independently,
groups of women should be accompanied by the NGO or other support agency staff to
conduct market studies and talk to wholesalers/retail traders and institutional buyers to
see what the possible channels are for marketing a given product. The project would
finance inputs from recognized specialists (see para --) to assist the EDP trainers in the
state to tailor EDP programs to the needs of the women. Skill development training
would be provided through participation in existing courses or by engaging
trainers/mastercraftsmen to train the women in the village.
(g) Systems for trouble-shooting, hand-holding and follow-up
* The majority of business failures occur within the first year. In order to reduce the
number of casualties the project would identify successful entrepreneurs in the



-142-
Annex 9
identified field to hand-hold the new entrant through initial problems for a year or more
based on need. These links with best practitioners who can act as mentors and
counselors would provide for business guidance, problem solving, and monitoring of
accounts and the overall progress of the business. Their inputs would be supplemented
by the support systems provided to the groups by the NGOs backed by back-up trouble-
shooting by the nodal entrepreneurship agencies (see paras --). Such interventions
would reduce the chances of micro-enterprise failure by providing opportunities for
close monitoring and immediate corrective action in the case of problems.
*  The system of linking new entrants with successful entrepreneurs in that specific
activity for purposes of support, hand-holding and follow-up through the initial years of
understanding the business, has yielded excellent results as shown by the success of
AWAKE. Identification of a network of best practitioners in the relevant areas and
networking them with the NGOs and SHGs will be sustainable in the long run if the
relationship is symbiotic, i.e., the grassroots activities can be commercially linked with
the best practitioners. This could provide the SHGs with market access while the best
practitioners can use the linkages for sourcing some of their requirements. Care would
be needed to ensure that no exploitation of the grassroots groups takes place.
(h) Design and Technology Upgradation
D For several groups of products made by the rural poor, market need-based design
interventions could make a substantial difference by allowing access to the upper end of
the mnarket, reducing the risk of inventory pile-up and increasing the returns to the
artisan. An indicative list would include groups of products such as forest fibers, leaves,
bamboo, lac, coir, wood, mirror work, embroidery, durrie weaving, fabric, linen,
pottery, terracotta and metal. This intervention would also address issues pertaining to
quality control and standardization to reduce rejection rates and promote marketability.
The project would provide for the hiring of design institutes or freelance designers to
modify existing designs in the light of market needs, train artisans at the upper end of
the skills scale as mastercraftspersons or trainers to standardize production techniques,
upgrade skills, and ensure quality control so as to minimize losses due to rejection and
maximize returns to the artisan by reaching larger markets. A similar approach would
be taken to improving production technology through hiring specialist
agencies/individuals dealing with specific products to provide advice and guidance to
women producers. Terms of Reference are given in Attachment 1.
(i) Market Linkage Assistance
* Before starting a new business or increasing the scale of an on-going activity, action
must be taken to determine the market outlet and the means of accessing the market. As
part of the EDP, women would be required to conduct market studies and identify the
marketing channels for their selected product. Where necessary, the project would
contract marketing agents to provide advice on marketing strategies and assist with



-143-
Annex 9
establishing market linkages for the women producers. These could include the
possibilities of linking up with existing private sector outlets; government emporia;
retail outlets, particularly for handicrafts, run by NGOs (e.g., Dastkar and Self-
Employed Women's Association); or participation in exhibitions and fairs held in
villages, towns and cities. Linkages with institutional buyers such as schools, factories,
restaurants and hotels near a village or with existing industrial units would also be
investigated.
* While there are a large number of government marketing and technical support agencies
at the district, state and national levels, systems must be evolved and linkages
established if the women's groups are to take advantage of them. Since it is virtually
impossible for poor rural women to individually access these organizations, structures
will have to be built that can link the women with existing government infrastructure.
One means of doing this would be for women in a cluster of SHGs involved in the same
activity to form a producer group/association.
(j) Communications and the Media
* A communications component would be an integral part of the program and would
focus on promoting exposure to alternate income generating activities and access to
information. Starting up and sustaining an economic activity is a complex process and
video films would be used to demonstrate the steps in setting up a business and
managing it. In addition, role models can provide inspiration, motivation and an
opportunity for learning. Video films documenting such cases could be a very powerful
way of reaching large numbers of existing and potential women producers. Existing
videos on these subjects prepared by such agencies as the National Institute of
Entrepreneurship and Small Business Development (NIESBUD) and NGOs such as,
Participatory Research for Asia (PRIA), would be screened for their suitability for use
under the project. If necessary, the CPSU would commission the production of these
videos which could then be used, with dubbing into local languages, by all the
participating states. Manuals would be prepared to go with each film. Booklets and
pamphlets, audio cassettes and radio programs relating to different microenterprises
would also be prepared. Again, the suitability of existing material would be assessed
before states commission the preparation of new material.
10.         Two options with regard to the video films are:
(a)   Preparation of a general 30 minute awareness raising film on income generation.
(b)   Preparation of a detailed training package in four parts of 30 minutes duration each.
Terms of Reference are given in Attachment 1.



-144-
Annex 9
(c)   An overview of business and the use of case studies to highlight the impact of any
business on creating wealth, employment, value addition to the community, income
to the family and economic upliftment of the entrepreneur.
(d)   The role of the entrepreneur in the success of the business, selection of the business
and the chazacteristics and strengths needed to set up a business.
(e)   Understanding the business environment, opportunity sensing, rapid market
appraisal, formalities, sourcing technology, technical skills, finance, viability of the
business and sources of support from government and non-government agencies.
(d)   Coping with start-up, sustaining the business, avoiding pitfalls and sickness,
managing money, group dynamics, record keeping and planning for growth.
(e)   Terms of Reference for the four part film are given in Attachment I.
E. Implementation Arrangements
11.         Implementation of the interventions and support services required for successful
promotion of income generating activities, will require contracts with specialist agencies with
proven skills in the given area. Such contracts will be necessary for conducting techno-economic
surveys; for market intelligence, conducting market surveys and establishing market linkages;
provision of business and technical skills to the SHGs and development of entrepreneurship
programs tailored to local needs; sensitization of NGOs with regard to entrepreneurship, setting up
systems for trouble-shooting, hand-holding and follow-up; upgrading of design skills and
development of video films and other materials for publicizing role models and for training. The
proposed interventions and implementation arrangements are summarized in the matrix in
Attachment 2.
12.         The Business Development Officer in the WDC district unit would be primarily
responsible for coordinating the provision of support services to women producers. The WDC
Marketing Officer at the state level would provide the necessary contacts with marketing agents
specialized in various products. The WDC Training Coordinator would be involved in liaising with
the training resource agencies identified by the nodal entrepreneurship development agencies.
Role of NGOs
13.         Given their close contacts with the SHG members, the NGOs sponsoring the SHGs
would be expected to play a key role in promoting non-farm activities. They would assist the
women in the selection of appropriate income generating activities from the menu of options, help
the women overcome constraints such as lack of literacy, problems in mobility, and lack of
confidence in dealing with officials and coordinate the provision of the necessary support services
through accessing the support networks set up by the nodal entrepreneurship development
organizations. The successful implementation of this program requires the orientation of the NGOs



-145-
Annex 9
towards issues pertaining to entrepreneurship and the support needed by SHG members setting up
income generating activities. However, most NGOs are very weak in this area. Hence the NGOs
sponsoring the SHGs would be given intensive training in business development by the nodal
entrepreneurship development organization so that they are able to effectively assist the women in
the establishment of their businesses. The NGOs would also be assisted to network with the locally
identified resource agencies/individuals to facilitate ready access to additional support.
Potential Support Agencies
14.         The depth and quality of resources which the WDC can draw on to support the
promotion of non-farm activities varies between the participating states. This highlights the
importance of networking between the states to identify outside resources where necessary. Local
training organizations or resource partners could include organizations such as any of the Rural
Development and Self-Employment Training Institutes (RUDSETI) centers or Rural Technology
Institute at Gandhinagar, Gujarat or the Canara Bank Platinum Jubilee Rural Development Trust,
Bangalore, or Central Food Technology Research Institute. In addition, there are several
cooperatives and NGOs in the country that have developed skills in building group-based
entrepreneurial skills, e.g., Self-Employed Women's Association (SEWA), the Lijjat groups, and
PRADAN. With the passage of time, mature groups can be used to develop capacity where
possible so that the groups can work towards a sense of owning and managing the program.
15.         Other institutions supporting micro enterprise development include the state level
technical consultancy organizations such as TECSOK, GITCO, MPCON, BITCON, UPCON and
HARDICON which could prepare techno-economic feasibility reports, conduct market surveys,
identify investment opportunities and help implement projects. Marketing organizations such as
Development Commissioner of Handicrafts, Development Commissioner of Handlooms,
Handicrafts and Handloom Export Corporation, Central Cottage Industries, State Emporia for
handicrafts and handlooms, export promotion councils, and organizations specializing in marketing
products produced in rural areas, such as GRIMCO in Gujarat have massive infrastructure that can
be utilized for marketing some of the products produced by the SHGs as also for determining
market trends, conducting market surveys, and accessing new designs. Other institutions would
include the district industry Centres, Khadi and Village Industries Commission, Khadi and Village
Industries Board, Council for Advancement of People's Action and Rural Technology (CAPART),
and specialist marketing boards for various products such as for silk, coir, etc. However, the track
record of several of these organizations in terms of providing access to poor rural women is dismal
and the project would need to explore mechanisms for improving the access of poor women if the
women's groups are to take advantage of the technical and marketing support that they can provide.
16.         Several organizations conduct vocational and entrepreneurship related training for
different products. These include CFTRI, Mysore, CLRI, Madras, organizations like Centre for
Entrepreneurship Development at Chandigarh, Dharwar, Madurai and Ahmedabad, CEDOK,
Dharwar, Rural Technology Institute, Gujarat, AWAKE, Bangalore, ACE, Bangalore, EDI,
Ahmedabad, ICECD, Ahmedabad, NIESBUD, Delhi, NISIET, Rural Development and Self
Employment Training Institutes (RUDSETI) at 11 centers in the country, Canara Bank Platinum



-146-
Annex 9
Jubilee Rural Development Trust, Bangalore and Bharat Yuvak Shakti Trust, CII, Delhi among
others. Many of these organizations have resources and infrastructure that can be valuable in
implementing the project. For instance, CFTRI has played a major role in developing and
disseminating technology pertaining to food processing and development of balanced meals for
school children. In addition there are several agricultural universities and institutes with a wealth of
knowledge that can be drawn on for agro-related income generating activities.

Attachment 1
STUDIES RELATED TO WOMEN'S PARTICIPATION IN NON-FARM ACTIVITIES
TERMS OF REFERENCE
A. Terms of Reference for the Development of Non-Farm Income Generating Activities
I1.         Terms of Reference (TOR) for the Nodal Local or National Entrepreneurship
Development Agencies:
(a)   determine the criteria for selecting appropriate NGOs and grassroots organizations
for implementing this program. Together with the WDC, identify suitable NGOs
for this task in the relevant districts;
(b)   conduct a visioning cum planning workshop in each state with the objective of
developing an integrated program for the entire state. Also identify suitable
indicators for use by NGOs in monitoring and self-evaluation.
(c)   conduct sensitization workshops for the NGOs and support agencies working with
the SHGs with the objective of developing an understanding among the NGOs
about entrepreneurship and the support that they would need to provide to the
SHGs in the process of setting up any income generation activity. Help initiate
networks between the different agencies involved.
(d)   For the districts in which you will be working, identify specific business and
vocational opportunities and develop and tailor entrepreneurship development
programs to match local needs.
(e)   Identify and evaluate local potential providers of training in skills, they should be
able to start viable income generating activities.
(f)    Establish a system for troubleshooting, handholding and follow-up for each
district. Identify best practitioners in different fields who can provide back up
support to the new businesses. Establish networks between these best
practitioners, NGOs and the SHGs to reduce the chance of losses due to failure.
Establish systems for monitoring the performance of new businesses.
(g)   Conduct feasibility studies to determine business opportunities for the specified
districts.
(h)   Conduct the first entrepreneurship development training program in each district
to enable the local resource team and NGOs to jointly identify potential trainees,
identify vocational training needs, develop and tailor programs to match local



-148-
Annex 9
Attachment 1
needs, conduct the business and technical skills workshops as needed and help
monitor start-up.
(i)    Set up systems to help scale up income generating activities when they plateau.
Help in growth of such activities as needed.
B. Terms of Reference for Conducting a Techno-Economic Survey at the Village Level for
the Identified District
(a)    provide information on the population of the village, the age-profile, gender
classification, literacy levels, numeracy levels, existing skills, land-use pattern,
natural resources of the village, occupational distribution, income levels,
consumption pattern, availability of electricity, commercial and educational
infrastructure, etc.;
(b)   provide detailed information on existing off-farm activities in the village, turnover
levels, income generated, employment generated, major raw materials used, raw
materials available, markets in and around the village, frequency of haats,
availability of transport, etc.;
(c)   identify existing local skills and resources and determine their marketability;
(d)   grade the locally available skills and identify methods to upgrade them;
(e)    determine the interventions necessary to make the skills marketable in traditional
and non-traditional markets;
(f)   identify means of accessing markets at different locations; and
(g)   identify the interventions necessary for creating new and upgrading existing
income generating activities.
C. Terms of Reference for Market Survey
2.    Identify all possible market opportunities for the groups of traditional and non-traditional
products and services produced by the women in the SHGs. The activities in which rural women
are engaged include:
(a)  trading activities such as vending vegetables, fish, milk, and fruits,
breaking bulk, and grading and sorting;
(b)   activities based on traditional skills and using local raw materials such as
leaf plate making, brooms, embroidery, making ropes, mats, bamboo



-149-
Annex 9
Attachment 1
products, pandal making, durrie weaving, fabric weaving, pottery, etc.;
(c)   production and trade in processed foods such as parboiled rice, jams,
jellies, fruit bars, papads, honey, bread, biscuits, snacks, spices, salt, etc.;
(d)   non-traditional activities such as making cards, notebooks, files,
envelopes and raising boiler chickens;
(e)   traditional service activities such as running small hotels, provision stores
and tea stalls; or hiring out bicycles or utensils; and
(f)    skill-based, non-traditional service activities such as hand pump repair,
radio repair, running STD booths, cycle repair, photography, plumbing,
welding, zip repair, etc.
3.             For all such activities, identify:
(a)    the feasible markets and marketing options within the village, in nearby villages,
at the haat, in nearby towns, in larger cities, possibilities of linkages with private
sector traders, exhibitions, tie-ups with institutional buyers and government
marketing outlets;
(b)    the frequency and location of haats in the area;
(c)    the products sold at these haats, the time taken to sell the different items, the
quantities sold, and the types of buyers;
(d)    fast and slow moving items at the different haats, and reasons for differentials in
speed of off-take;
(e)    products used in the village that are procured from outside an the price at which
these are sold. Also the possibility of sourcing these products from the SHGs;
(f)    institutional development in the district and the products and services required by
the institutions that are procured from outside. The prices at which these are
currently obtained. Possibilities of any of these needs being met by the SHGs;
(g)    possibilities of linkages of SHGs with existing government marketing
infrastructure and the products and prices at which this will be feasible;
(h)    possibilities and relative profitability of supplying products to wholesalers and
retailers;



-150-
Annex 9
Attachment 1
(i)    possibilities and relative profitability of groups of women in the SHGs marketing
the products directly in nearby towns;
(j)    constraints and opportunities to marketing the products and services produced by
the women in the SHGs;
(k)   project interventions that can enable easier access to haats and other market
options;
(I)   future growth areas in the district so that appropriate skills can be provided to the
women in the SHGs;
(m)   appropriate marketing product positioning and pricing strategies for the activities.
Also assess the marketing support required by the groups and the sources and
channels of such support that can be utilized by the groups; and
(n)   possibilities of reducing the difficulties and drudgery in accessing and trading at
haats, such as possibilities for improving the transport services or construction of
toilets, provision of safe drinking water, and construction of godowns.
D. Terms of Reference for Design
4.           For some of the activities in which rural women are engaged, especially activities
based on traditional skills and using local raw materials such as leaf plate making, brooms,
embroidery, making ropes, mats, bamboo products, pandal making, durrie weaving, fabric
weaving, pottery, etc. and also non-traditional activities such as making cards, notebooks, files
and envelopes, design interventions can make a major difference to the quality of the product and
to its ability to reach the more affluent markets. This may result in substantial gains to the
artisan.
5.           In this context, identify:
(a)   the products in which market need based design interventions could make a
substantial difference to the marketabilitv and profitability of the micro-
enterprise;
(b)   modify existing designs in light of market needs and in view of raw material
availability;
(c)    grade the artisans in the village on a skills scale; and



-151-
Annex 9
Attachment 1
(d)   train artisans at the upper end of the skills scale as master craftspersons or trainers
to ensure standardization of production techniques, upgrade skills, and ensure
quality control with a view toward minimizing losses due to rejection.
E. Terms of Reference for Video Film
(a)   prepare a detailed training package consisting of a film of four parts;
(b)   each part of the film will be of thirty minutes duration and will be accompanied
by a training manual. The film will be available for use in all the districts; and
(c)   part (a) of the film will provide an overview of business and use of case studies to
highlight the impact of any business on creating wealth, employment, value
addition to the community, income to the family and economic upliftment of the
entrepreneur. Part (b) of the film will depict the role of the entrepreneur in the
success of the business, selection of the business and the characteristics and
strengths needed to set up a business. Part (c) will provide inputs on
understanding the business environment, opportunity sensing, rapid market
appraisal, formalities, sourcing technology, technical skills, finance, viability of
the business and sources of support from government and non-government
agencies. Part (d) will consist of issues such as coping with startup, sustaining the
business, avoiding pitfalls and sickness, managing money, group dynamics,
record keeping and planning for growth.
