Annex 10
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
SOCIAL DEVELOPMENT
I. STAKEHOLDER PARTICIPATION
I .    The project has been prepared with the extensive participation of a range of stakeholders,
and finalised after intensive consultations with them during pre-appraisal and appraisal. The
participants include:
  potential women beneficiaries, women's groups, panchayat members and other
village leaders, government workers and officials at the vilage, block and district
levels, with whom the key implementing agencies, such as the Women's Development
Corporations, state nodal departments, NGOs collaborating in preparation of the project,
and IFAD and joint Bank-IFAD missions have discussed needs and problems faced in
key project areas;
*  intermediary field, resource and networking NGOs; officials from government line
departments in district administrations and at state level, such as the agriculture,
animal husbandry, forestry, rural development, education, health, and ICDS
departments, who have participated in discussions, field visits, meetings and workshops
and provided information and views which have influenced the development of project
objectives, scope and activities;
* "apex" resource institutions (e.g., Friends of Women's World Banking, the Bankers'
Institute of Rural Development in Lucknow, the Indiar. Institutes of Management at
Ahmedabad and Bangalore), banks and NABARD, and state government agencies
(such as the U.P. Land Development Corporation and Haryana's Mewat Development
Agency), who have discussed important project support activities, including training,
banking mechanisms, enterprise development, social components, and monitoring;
* state nodal departments (Departments of Women and Child Development/Social
*  Welfare/Industries) and Women's Development Corporations, and the Government
of India's Department of Women and Child Development, who have led and
managed the project preparation process and participated fully in discussions, visits and
workshops, with IFAD and joint IFAD-Bank missions; and



-156-
Annex 10
* multilateral and bilateral agencies, including the United Nations Development
Programme (UNDP), UNICEF, GTZ (the German government technical cooperation
agency), the British Council, CARE International. Several of these agencies support
projects or activities relevant to the objectives and scope of this project, and have
indicated interest in collaborating with IDA, or in financing activities in parallel with
the project to support it during implementation. A workshop organized during Appraisal
by MYRADA, a collaborating NGO, to discuss the project with representatives of
different stakeholders was financed by PLAN International.
II. SOCIAL ASSESSMENT
2.    Participatory approaches are central to (a) the identification of the project's target group; (b)
the micro-level planning envisaged in the project, particularly for enterprise development and the
social components, which will involve eliciting views and preferred development options; and (c)
overall implementation of the project. In effect, the project concept and approach are themselves
a continuous process of social assessment which will occur throughout implementation, as they
are highly participatory and demand driven. For example:
-  Implementing NGOs will be selected by the WDCs in consultation with village women,
other residents and leaders, and district officials, thus ensuring that they are acceptable
to all.
*  The selection of "target beneficiaries" by the NGOs involved in group formation would
involve social assessment processes. The NGOs will know the villages in which they
work and engage their communities in identifying target households. While nurturing
the SHGs, the NGOs will consult with communities widely about what problems the
community/women have experienced which need to be addressed during
implementation, what program/infrastructure linkages need to be strengthened in the
particular village, what micro-enterprises are acceptable to the women, and so on.
During these processes, the NGOs will also become aware of the need for and
appropriate strategies to implement aspects of the program such as gender sensitization,
awareness creation, etc.
*  The group formation and maturing process itself entails a constant sharing of
experiences, analysis of needs, strategies, and outcomes by group members and
implementing agencies.
*  The SHGs would be involved in a number of activities which ensure that their views as
well as those of their village communities are taken into account during project
implementation. These include participatory training activities, community
consultations for the purposes of determining what community assets need to be
created, and self-assessment techniques used during monitoring and evaluation to assess
the performance and impact of the project and promote sustainability.



-157-
Annex 10
3.          Therefore, while no fonnal social assessment "study" has taken place, other than the
assessments described in Annex 15 for tribal populations, project preparation and appraisal
processes identified the wide range of stakeholders described above, obtained their views, and
carried out social analysis. A number of significant issues which were identified have been taken
into account in project design. For example:
*  Concern was expressed that the project should not focus only on women below the
poverty line. Haryana, for example, is a state with a relatively high per capita income
and a low percentage of people below the poverty line, and it is clear that even women
above the poverty line are severely disadvantaged socially. Alternative approaches
were discussed widely. Consequently, the project proposes to concentrate economic
activities on the poor, but has developed small sub-components (such as awareness
creation) which will reach out to other disadvantaged women and address their needs.
*  In some areas, the friction between panchayats and field NGOs creates the risk of a
powerful force of "negative stakeholders". It was agreed that in order to reduce such
potential tension during project implementation, the WDCs would ensure that the
implementing NGOs have the full support of local self-government groups as well as of
potential beneficiaries. This would be done through a village level consultation process.
In addition, panchayats and other community members would be oriented fully to the
project concept and its approaches. Further, conflict resolution will be an important
aspect of the training imparted to the NGOs, SHGs, etc.
Another potential group of negative stakeholders are me in the communities, who may resent the
project for its empowering effects on women. A consequence of this resentment is often increased
domestic or community violence against women. The project plans to include gender sensitization
as a community activity, and will increase women's awareness of their rights. In addition, the
solidarity achieved among women through group formation and action would assist women in
dealing with this problem individually and collectively.
III. MACRO-INDICATORS OF SOCIAL STATUS IN THE PROJECT DISTRICTS
4.           The project will be implemented in selected districts of six states, numbering 35 in
all. The districts have been chosen, firstly, on the basis of poverty criteria, viz. the percentage of
population below the poverty line (BPL), sex ratio, female literacy rate, and percentage of
Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes (SC/ST) in the population. The main reason for use of
poverty criteria is that the project aims to improve the economic and social situation of poor women
in areas where their opportunities have thus far been limited. In some states, such as Bihar and
Madhya Pradesh, the selected districts represent substantially different geo-cultural zones. A
reason for such representation is that, in time, expansion of the project to a much larger program
would be facilitated if success can be shown to have been achieved in a variety of settings. In



-158-
Annex 10
addition, district selection has also taken into account the availability of NGOs to implement the
project.
5.           The states and districts selected represent a range of different social situations.
Female literacy levels in the districts range from 7 percent in Tikamgarh, M.P., to 50 percent in
Bharuch, Gujarat. The districts' sex ratios, representing the overall survival of females relative to
males, vary from 818 to 1007. The poor comprise a high proportion of the population in Bharuch
and Panchmahals, Gujarat, and in Betul, M.P., as represented by the percentage of the population
who are SC/ST, but are as low as 12 percent in Surendranagar, Gujarat. Although these macro-
indicators are not likely to be significantly impacted by the project during its five-year duration,
because the coverage of each district by the project will be limited, they are, nevertheless, important
indicators of the status of women in the project areas.
6.           The key target group for the project is women below the poverty line, defined by the
Government of India as an income of Rs. 11,000 per household (consisting of an average of 5.5
persons) in rural areas. Most women in this category are likely to be from landless households, or
small or marginal farm households. Emphasis will be placed on including women who are heads of
households, who are among the most severely disadvantaged. Many of these poor women face a
multiplicity of adverse circumstances: not only are their economic situations strained, but they are
also faced with eking out livings under constraints of infrastructure (in part on account of the areas
in which they live), low access to technologies for work or drudgery-reduction, poor access to
social services, and heavy family responsibilities.
IV. KEY SOCIAL ISSUES AND RELATED PROJECT ACTIONS
7.           A number of issues which are related to the project's objectives, concept and
approach are raised by the social indicators in the project areas. These are discussed below, along
with descriptions of how the project addresses them.
A.    Literacy
8.          In most of the project districts, female literacy is lower than the national average and
the average of the state in which the district lies. This is to be expected since the districts were
chosen on the basis of poverty criteria, and female literacy is generally well correlated with poverty.
Similarly, at the level of blocks and villages selected for implementation, female literacy is low,
and there are likely to be very few literate women in the majority of Self Help Groups which are
formed.
9.           Experience elsewhere in the country (e.g., in the IFAD-supported project in
Dharmapuri, Tamil Nadu, as well as in projects implemented by a number of NGOs, some of which
will be involved in this project) has shown that low (or virtually no) literacy among group members
is a severe constraint to the development of group activities. In particular, group members find it
difficult to keep accounts, to access services which require the filling of forms including bank



-159-
Annex 10
services, or even to monitor their savings. As a result they become dependent on the individual
who maintains their books and accounts. Consequently it was felt that providing group members
basic "functional" literacy is critical to the success of the project, and hence should be a project
component. Acquisition of functional literacy skills by SHG members would facilitate the
working of the groups broadly, including the maintenance of accounts and records, enabling
women to take on leadership responsibilities, and facilitating access to information for both
economic and social development.
10.         Accordingly, the project has made provisions for the SHGs to acquire functional
literacy skills building on what has been achieved through other programs, harnessing resources
(personnel, material and financial) available under other schemes such as the total literacy/adult
literacy activities of government or even non-governmental agencies in the area, and supplementing
them where necessary to strengthen their focus on project-related literacy and numeracy skills. The
literacy teaching would be provided only when group members express a demand for it, and the
group is willing to bear the cost of sustaining the activity, if necessary, beyond the 18-month period
which is deemed a reasonable time for group members to acquire functional literacy. The groups
would plan and organize literacy classes in collaboration with the implementing NGO. A tutor
would be chosen from among group members or other community residents who have appropriate
qualifications. The project would provide for training/retraining of tutors before commencement of
classes, and for a nominal honorarium for them. Purchase of teacher and learner materials and
miscellaneous equipment would also be financed, as well as the costs incurred by the NGOs
managing the activity. Specialist NGOs may be engaged by WDCs to carry out the activity where
necessary.
11.         In areas where the Total Literacy Campaigns have been concluded successfully,
post-literacy activities could be supported, if necessary, or supplemented, if on-going. A special
need related specifically to the project's credit and micro-enterprise activities is the development of
teaching-learning materials for accounting and business purposes. Under the project, organizations
which have specialized capabilities in this field will be engaged to collate/develop and produce
materials in the local languages. Post-literacy materials would be suitably modified. By the end of
the project, it is expected that the SHGs formed would have sufficient literacy among their
members to function smoothly and to feel they have been empowered through its acquisition.
12.         In some states, such as Haryana, other special needs arise in the context of
functional literacy because of very adverse social conditions affecting women. Haryana is
particularly interested in creating awareness of the legal and familial rights of women on account of
widespread violence against them, as well as of underlying social problems such as alcohol abuse
and drug addiction. Consequently, the functional literacy component will encompass such
awareness creation.
B.    Access to Health Services
13.         The project districts are also characterised by poor health among their populations.
The poverty of these districts is synonymous with (a) greater susceptibility of their populations to



-160-
Annex 10
illness on account of the major common infections, including childhood infections; (b) poor health
among women, especially anemia, which reduces women's capacities to work and makes them
greatly susceptible to illness, maternal mortality, and pregnancy and infant loss; and (c)
underdevelopment of the public health care infrastructure, including a lack of facilities, staff and
supplies, as well as poor private health services. Access to health services is poor as the
government health system, which is structured to provide a facility for each cluster of 4-5 villages,
is often non-functional in many backward areas. Similarly, access to private health services is often
minimal as "modem" medical practitioners prefer to practise in towns or large, better-off villages.
Even traditional practitioners are increasingly becoming "urbanized", leaving poor rural
populations to the less trained.
14.         All these problems represent major constraints to the development of women's
economic status and the improvement of household well-being. Greater illness among household
members and children means that adult women spend more time caring for sick members of their
households. It also places a greater burden on both women and children to work, in order that
household income is assured in the event that the male head of household falls ill. Where illnesses
such as tuberculosis and malaria have debilitated adult male members of households, women are
often the effective heads of households, and face a heavy double burden. In addition, neglect of
their own health or illness, particularly if their households are dependent on their earnings, initiates
a further downward spiral - loss of wages, poor and worsening nutrition on account of household
poverty, greater debility and further diminished work capacity.
15.         It is well established that illness is a major cause of indebtedness (to moneylenders)
among poor families throughout the country. Women members of thrift and credit groups often
report that they borrow from group savings to pay the costs of health care. Health emergencies are
particularly devastating. Consumption loans, also, are often taken because the illness of a chief
wage eamer has reduced household income to the point where even basic food requirements cannot
be met.
16.         Several NGOs working with women's self help groups (e.g., SEWA and Utthan in
Gujarat, and Adithi in Bihar) have found that providing basic health care that is easily accessible to
women and low cost is effective in releasing poor households from the vicious cycle of low
economic status and ill-health. Where groups are spread out geographically, and health facilities
are distant, the most common approach has been to train village-level health workers to provide
basic preventive and curative health care and health education to women. In places where health
facilities and practitioners are relatively more accessible, and large numbers of women can be
organized (as SEWA has done in Gujarat), efforts encompass health insurance schemes and
maternity benefits.
17.         Taking this into consideration, the project has made provisions to assist women to
reduce the burden of ill-health in order that they can benefit fully from the economic activities.
Where women members of Self-Help Groups express basic health care as a priority need, and there
are gaps in the availability of basic public health services, the project provides for an appropriate
local NGO to train selected women volunteers in basic health care. It is anticipated that about 60



-161-
Annex 10
percent of SHGs would opt for a strategy to address the health of their communities. As the SHGs
grow in strength, their ability to lobby the health system for an improved village-level health care
would increase.
18.         The health volunteer would be a woman selected by the group in consultation with
the wider community. Once trained, she would (a) help to disseminate information on health
related issues and promote preventive health care; (b) help group members and other women in the
village to access the formal health system or private health facilities, with a focus on women and
child health; and (c) where necessary, provide simple basic health care, such as first aid or oral
rehydration salts. The project would also provide health education materials, a simple basic kit of
equipment and supplies to the trainee. The contents of the kit would be in keeping with practices
approved by the state Health Department for primary health care workers.
19.         A stepwise approach is envisaged with regard to the activities of the health worker.
After training, the focus of her work would initially be with the group. This would entail only a
part-time commitment and, in the context of mutual help within the group, it is envisaged that no
stipend would be necessary. As she gains in confidence and the value of her services is perceived
by the wider community, her efforts could extend to the community in response to their demand.
At this time, the health worker would work out a mechanism with the community for compensation
for her services and replenishment of her supplies. The project may however provide refresher
training for the health worker.
C.    Child Care for Working Mothers
20.         Given the already heavy workloads of women, several stakeholders expressed
concern that women would only be able to engage in additional income-generating activities if
some facilities were available for child care. Traditional child care arrangements have all but
broken down with the nuclearization of families, with greater responsibility devolving on older
siblings (particularly girls) to care for young ones. Although the government has instituted the
Integrated Child Development Services (ICDS) program widely, this provides only limited day care
to working mothers. In view of this, the project has made provisions for day care to be organized
under certain circumstances. For example, where women are working at some distance from their
homes, and have no family or community support network, they could organize themselves to
engage a child care worker.
21.         The approach calls for the SHG to first lobby for the extension of existing programs
(such as ICDS) to their village, or for the program to address their specific needs effectively, for
example, through better location of the ICDS center (anganwadi) or modification of its working
hours to fit with their work patterns. Where this is not achievable, the project provides for
established SHGs (at least one year old) to receive assistance towards the honorarium of a child
minder for a period of two years on a sliding scale: 90 percent in the first year and 60 percent in the
second year. Thereafter, the SHG would take responsibility for financing this service. In
establishing honoraria for the child minders, the SHGs would be encouraged to keep in mind the
scales and pattern of other schemes for day care centers/creches in the area. The project would also



-162-
Annex 10
provide some initial equipment. Once again, these provisions would be made available only to
SHGs which have expressed need and demand for it, and are contingent on the SHG being willing
to meet the costs of sustaining the activity after the two-year period.
D.   Assistance to Leverage Funds for Community Asset Creation
22.         The project would assist communities to leverage resources from on-going
programs to create community assets which improve the quality of women's daily lives by
reducing drudgery and the time taken by routine domestic chores. This would enhance women's
abilities to achieve higher economic levels. The extent of women's participation in economic
work and community activities, and the benefits accrued from these by women and their families, is
often affected by the degree of drudgery faced by women in tasks such as fetching water, collecting
or cutting fodder and fuelwood. The project provides a fixed monetary sum per "natural" village,
which may be spent on several items in small amounts or all together on a single major asset.
23.         The basic principles of this component are: (a) the project would facilitate
community access to those services and development activities that contribute to drudgery
reduction and hence to greater productivity, and which enhance the status of group members in the
eyes of their community; (b) the funds would be available only after a group has stabilized (e.g.,
after the second year of its existence), (c) the decision to acquire/create the asset would be taken by
the community as a whole, and the asset would be accessible to the wider community for use; (d)
the funds provided by the project would be used to promote convergence between activities
selected for support and on-going programs; (e) the relatively small amount of funds provided
would be used to leverage resources in cash or kindfrom other development schemes, as well as
community contributions; and (f) implementation would be with community participation.
24.         The following steps would be taken to utilize this assistance:
*  The SHG, assisted by the local implementing NGO, would seek the views of their
community to determine priorities.
*  With the help of the implementing NGO, the SHG would prepare a proposal for
submission to the state WIDC. Panchayats would play a key role in these two processes,
particularly where they have access to development funds for community asset creation.
Interactions between the women's groups and panchayats, including women panchayat
members, could help to improve the position of women in their communities by
enlarging the role they play in such community decision-making.
*  The proposals would be forwarded to the Women's Development Corporation for
review, approval and financing. Each proposal would identify the various sources of
funds for the asset creation (e.g., line departments, panchayat, community and WDC).
*  Once approved, a Memorandum of Understanding would be developed with the help of
the Project Manager in the WDC and the District Administration, and signed by all



-163-
Annex 10
relevant parties, to commit the proposed contributions from all sources. The SHG
would be responsible for the management of the asset once created, and may introduce
user/maintenance fees to ensure sustainability.
* The WDC would provide appropriate and timely feedback to the NGO and the SHG on
the status of their proposals; and the NGO and SHG would in turn report to the
community on the progress of the proposal, its implementation, and utilization of funds,
to ensure full accountability for the monies and for the assets created.
25.         All the social development activities described above would be supported by
appropriate information, education and communication activities and materials. In addition, in
order to create an enabling environment for women's efforts for social change, the project would
support gender sensitization programs which facilitate a positive attitude within communities
towards improved women's status.

INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
PROJECT ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Introduction
1.    The project would be implemented as a centrally sponsored scheme with the Department of
Women and Child Development (DWCD), Ministry of HJuman Resource Development, GO as the
overall nodal agency for the project. Within the states this function would be performed by the
State Department of Women and Child Development (Madhya Pradesh, Kamataka and Uttar
Pradesh), Department of Social Welfare (Bihar and Haryana) or Department of Industries and
Mines (Gujarat). The project would be executed by the State Women's Development Corporations
(WDCs)-1. The organizational structure for the project is shown in Charts I to 3.
B. Project Organisation
Overall Approach
2.    The primary approach of the project is to empower women through the creation of
effective self-help groups (SHGs). A key element in this would be the establishment of a trust-
based relationship between the women of the SHG and the NGO field worker. The
organizational design recognizes that the women of the SHG hold primary responsibility,
decision making authority, and accountability for their own success, and that the field worker
would advise and support them. A lot of effort would be placed upfront in orienting participating
NGOs/intervenors to the project's aims and approach.
3.    To ensure that the SHGs achieve success, the project seeks to strengthen over time the
institutions which would facilitate further development of the SHG concept and provide
specialist services to the women in response to emerging demands from the groups. The key
areas of specialist expertise include: (i) commercial and regional rural banks to provide credit to
SHGs; (ii) line departments to provide technical support services; (iii) specialist
consulting/training organizations to provide training to project participants; and (iv) specialist
agencies to provide assistance with product design, production techniques and marketing. The
interventions to support the participating public sector institutions in the context of the proposed
project are discussed below. Annexes 4 and 5 describe the organization and management role of
the SHGs and NGOs.
In Gujarat the term used is the Women's Economic Development Corporation and in Uttar Pradesh, the Women's Welfare
Corporation.



-166-
Annex 11
Organizational Structure and Responsibilities
Central Project Support Unit, Department of Women and Child Development.
4.    As the Central Government department with the mandate for guiding and assisting states in
implementing projects to aid women's development, the Department of Women and Child
Development (DWCD) would be responsible for overseeing the implementation of the project. The
lead responsibility for supporting the project would be given to the Secretary, DWCD who would
be assisted by a Central Project Support Unit (CPSU).
5.    The main role of the CPSU would be to facilitate implementation of this decentralized
project with a focus on capacity building of the project implementors, information exchange and
coordination of the monitoring and evaluation of project performance. Within this framework, the
CPSU would be responsible for: (a) facilitating information exchange across the participating
states; (b) providing central administrative functions; (c) facilitating the collation nationwide and
dissemination of information on areas of common interest to the project through contracting out
periodic reviews/studies to specialist agencies and individuals; (d) implementing special studies;
and (e) organising any services which may span participating states, e.g., design of the M&E
framework, generic training activities and production of communications materials. Further details
on the functions of the CPSU are given in Attachment 2.
6.    The CPSU would serve as a discussion partner with IDA/IFAD, GOI, state nodal
departments and WDCs on policy issues and at key milestones such as adjustments in project
design during implementation, the Mid-Term Review and further project development. The
CPSU's role at these times is intended to be broad direction setting.
7.    The CPSU would also be responsible for preparing an inventory of experts nationwide in
fields of direct relevance to project implementation, e.g., micro-credit, micro-enterprises, gender
sensitisation, appropriate technologies, etc., from which the participating states may draw as
required, on a contractual basis, to assist in project implementation.
8.    As part of its overall responsibility to commission studies on various aspects of project
performance, the CPSU would commission a special study by the end of the second year to
review the performance of the NGOs and the participatory management aspects of the project.
State Level
Women's Development Corporations (WDCs)
9.    Since the WDCs were established with the specific mandate to assist women in their quest
for economic independence, they represent the most appropriate institution at the state level to take
responsibility for overseeing the implementation of the project. Their mandate gives them a broad
focus whilst their establishment as Corporations confering greater independent decision making



-167-
Annex 11
powers gives them greater operational flexibility than a government department in working with
NGOs and other private sector agencies.
10.   The WDCs would play an important role in promoting, guiding, coordinating and
monitoring the implementation of the project. Overall responsibility for the execution of the
project would be vested in the Managing Director, WDC. The management of the project would be
integrated into the regular organizational structure of the WDCs which would be modified to allow
for the establishment of administrative, finance, marketing and training and information units to
take responsibility for coordinating project implementation in their respective areas. Further details
of the responsibilities of the WDCs are given in Attachment 2.
11.   District level units, headed by a District Project Manager (DPM), would be established in
all the project districts (with the exception of UP). In UP, management of the project activities
would be sub-contracted by the WWC to the UP Land Development Corporation (UPLDC) in the
13 districts where formation of women's SHGs in on-going under the UP Sodic Lands
Development Project. UPLDC would manage the project through its existing management
structure in return for a management fee.
12.   The line management responsibilities for project implementation would be driven by the
Project Director working under the overall direction of the Managing Director. In addition to its
line management functions, the WDCs would also provide, at the state level, several specialist
functions which are needed at the field level but which are most economically shared across the
participating districts. These include training, marketing and communications.
13.   At the district level, the DPMs would play a critical role in building a "team" of those
assisting project implementation and facilitating the many linkages required between the SHGs and
NGO field workers, line departments, banks and training and support organizations. The DPMs
will provide feedback to the higher level management. In addition, the DPMs would ensure that
the NGOs and SHGs adopt the broader philosophies required to achieve the holistic objectives of
the project.
Public and Private Sector Technical Support Agencies
14.   Support for On-Farm activities. The technical line departments would be the principal
providers of training and other technical support services to women undertaking on-farm activities.
They would work closely with NGO field staff and resource persons identified by the group. In
providing technical support participatory assessment of the resource base available to women and
their perceptions and priorities would be undertaken before commencing the work. This will help
in selection of appropriate farming systems options. The principal departments involved would be:
Agriculture, Horticulture, Land Development/Soil Conservation, Irrigation, Minor Irrigation, and
2/    UP Land Development Corporation is a Government of UP owned agency. It is currently implementing an
IDA assisted and a EU assisted sodic lands reclamation project in 13 districts of the state. These projects
are making excellent progress. The Corporation is in full compliance with IDA requirements with regards
to financial management and all other legal requirements under the project.

Annex 11
Animal Husbandry. More specific information on the support to be provided under the project is
given in Annex 8. Provision is also made for funds for short consultancies for accessing specialist
expertise as required.
15.   Support for Non-farm Activities. Various private and public sector technical agencies
would be involved in providing support services for non-farm activities. These would include
state technical consultancy organizations or private consultancy groups who would undertake
techno-economic and market surveys and feasibility studies of potential off-farm enterprises.
Public sector design institutes, such as the National Institute of Design, and private designers
would provide assistance with upgradation of designs and production techniques, training of
mastercraftsmen and skill development training. Private sector market research organizations
would undertake market surveys and assist women producers in establishing market linkages.
Public and private sector entrepreneurship development organizations would be responsible for
providing entrepreneurship training. NGOs supporting the SHGs would be trained in business
development to improve their ability to guide women in the selection of appropriate activities
and to link women producers with the required support services. More information on specific
support to be provided under the project is given in Annex 9.
C. Capacity Building
16.   In view of the limited manpower resource and capacity of the main intervenors, the
project would assist in equipping them both to manage the project and to improve their ability to
provide better assistance to women in the longer term. In the center, DWCD will require
additional resources in order to oversee the implementation of the project. Since their
establishment the WDCs have not been adequately staffed nor provided with the requisite skills
such as business development, marketing, training and finance to effectively fulfill their
mandate. They have also suffered from discontinuity in top management due to a high turnover
of Managing Directors resulting in lack of leadership. In particular, they lack a field structure
preventing them from having the direct contact with their client group required to gain a sound
appreciation of the needs and priorities of women. To address these constraints, the project
would finance the provision of additional professional staff for DWCD and WDCs and necessary
support facilities and provide appropriate training and manpower development as specified
below. Inputs for strengthening other project implementors (NGOs, banks and line departments)
are described in Annexes 5, 7 and 8, respectively.
Manpower Resource
17.   CPSU. The CPSU would be staffed by three senior professionals-a Women's
Development Expert preferably with NGO experience; a Project Implementation Expert and a
Finance/Monitoring Expert-two second level officers (Administrative Officer and Accounts
Officer) and four support staff (two stenographers, one accounts assistant and one driver-cum-
peon). The key professional staff should preferably be women. Recruitment of these incremental
staff would be in phases in relation to the increasing workload of the project, and on such
contractual terms as not to involve any long-term liability on the part of GOI. Recruitment of the
three senior professionals would be through open competition and preferably from the open market



-169-
Annex 11
but serving government officers may participate in the competitive selection procedures with a
commitment to an extended tenure (at least three years). Terms of reference for the professional
staff are given in Attachment 3. In addition, the CPSU would have sufficient funding to contract
outside groups for key study/expert advice.
18.   WDCs. The key incremental professional staff required for the WDCs at the state level
would be a Project Director, (occupying the position of Chief General Manager in the WDC
hierarchy); Monitoring Officer; Marketing Coordinator; Training Coordinator; Communications
Officer; and Finance Officer. Incremental support staff would comprise accountant,
secretary/data processor, administrative assistant, office helper/peon and drivers as requireda All
incremental staff would be fitted into the modified organisational structure. In addition to the
provision of full-time staff, provision is made for contracting of specialist advice to assist in
resolving any project management issues or to improve the overall implementation of the project.
19.   The staff provided for the district offices would be a District Project Manager (DPM)
supported by a Business Development Officer, data processor, accountant, clerk/typist and one
support staff. All staff employed for the project would be expected to be computer literate. Terms
of Reference for the incremental WDC staff at the state and district levels are given in Attachment
4.
20.   In order to overcome the high turnover of government officers posted to the WDCs, all
staff at the state and district levels would be hired on contract through open competition and not
through direct transfer within the government system. Serving government officials may also be
considered for selection through competition. To recruit suitably qualified candidates for these
posts, the WDCs would constitute a Selection Committee comprising MD/WDC, a representative
of a prominent NGO in the state working on women's issues and a representative from the lending
institutions, preferably NABARD. Hiring of selected candidates would be subject to ratification
by the WDC Board. In hiring staff preference would be given to women. Recruitment of WDC
staff would be in phases in relation to the increasing workload.
Manpower Development and Training
21.   Implementation of the project will require orientation to concepts and approaches which are
presently not fully understood by many project intervenors. In particular, the characteristics of
SHGs is not widely appreciated whilst most project staff are not familiar with the participatory
approaches which will be central to the implementation of the project. These deficiencies would be
addressed under the project through orientation workshops and training as an on-going activity.
22.   State and District Level Orientation Workshops. An orientation workshop would be
held at the state level for members of the SPSC and SPWG to outline the project objectives,
underlying principles of the project, philosophy and concept of SHGs, role of the different partners
in project implementation and general awareness of women's perceptions, needs and priorities.
3/    Requirements vary between states depending on existing staff complement

Similar workshops would be held in each district at the commencement of project activities for all
WDC district level staff, District Collectors/District Development Officers, Zilla Parishad/Taluka
Parishad presidents (of project talukas), district line department staff, bankers and other concerned
parties. The workshops would last for 1-2 days. The workshops would be repeated in Years 3 and
4 to allow for the turnover of staff.
23.   Orientation Workshop for WDC Staff. A 7- 1 0 day workshop would be organised by
each WDC for its key state and district level staff focusing on the following key areas: (a) project
objectives, strategy and components; (b) philosophy and concept of SHGs; (c) participatory
planning processes; (d) functioning of savings and credit; (e) role of NGOs in project
implementation; (f) awareness of women's perceptions, needs and priorities; (g) government
schemes relating to women; (h) communication and motivating skills and team building; (i)
administration and accounts systems for project; and () monitoring and evaluation arrangements.
The workshop would draw heavily on outside resource persons, particularly from NGOs
experienced in SHG programs.
24.   Familiarisation through Visits to Existing SHGs. During the first year of project
implementation, all senior WDC project staff at the state and district level would go on visits to
successful NGO-sponsored SHG programs either within the state (e.g., Bihar, Kamataka, Gujarat,
UP) where such strong SHGs exist or in other states such as Kamataka, Maharashtra, and Tamil
Nadu where NGOs such as MYRADA, Chaitanya and PRADAN have promoted SHGs over a
number of years and linkage with the banks has made most progress.
25.   Management Training. In order to build up the planning and management capacity of the
WDCs, provision would be made for management training for WDC staff on a continuing basis
either through contracting private management experts to design and conduct a series of workshops
or sending WDC staff on courses organised by reputed management and/or public administration
institutes. Topics to be covered would include personnel management, team building, planning and
scheduling, problem solving and time management techniques, and management systems.
26.   Computer Skills. Training in computer skills would be provided for all WDC professional
staff, data processors, accountants and typists so that all project records are computerized.
Financial Management at WDCs
27.   In order to strengthen their accounting and financial control procedures, the WDCs would
engage experienced independent financial management consultants to review the existing
accounting, audit and financial control procedures and where necessary provide guidance on the
modifications rquired in order to establish accounting, auditing and internal practices acceptable
to IDA. Terms of Reference are given in Attachment 5.

Annex 11
Long-Term Role of WDCs
28.   Recent studies- commissioned on the role and perfornance of the WDCs highlighted a
number of areas of weakness and recommended strengthening them to make them more effective
promoters of the all round development of women (see Attachment 1). Until now these
recommendations have not been acted upon and the actions proposed under the project (as listed
above) represent the first step towards addressing these issues. The next step would be, after a
reasonable period of time, to assess the performance of the WDCs as the key project
implementing agencies and to assess their long term role in assisting women's development and
empowerment. Accordingly, the CPSU would commission a study prior to the Mid-Term
Review to re-assess the future role of the WDCs against the background of the overall changing
environment within the country and the broadening of the activities of the WDCs brought about
under the project. The findings of this review would be used at the Mid-Term Review to guide
the introduction of necessary changes in organizational structure, management systems and
human resource development strategy. Terms of Reference for the study are given in Attachment
6.
D. Policy Guidance and Coordination Assistance
29.   A large number of institutions will need to effectively work together and coordinate
activities to achieve the full impact of the project. To provide necessary guidance and coordination
support a number of committees and working groups would be established (Chart 2) to guide
implementation as per the guiding principles of the project. The committees and working groups
would meet at regular intervals to facilitate effective execution of the project.

chaired by the Chief Secretary (or his nominee) with the Project Director as Member Secretary.

would be:

with particular reference to the linkages with the banks and line departments, through providing

in order to push forward project implementation. The SPWG would meet bi-monthly or more
frequently if necessary.

-     key line department extension workers;
-     bank branch managers;
-     4-6 selected SHG representatives.
38.   A WDC district representative would initially be involved as the convenor but this function
would be handed over to a local representative elected by the CLWG. The functions of the CLWG
would be to provide a forum for regular interaction between beneficiaries and project implementors
to ensure the responsiveness of the project to the expressed needs of the beneficiaries; to facilitate
the provision of the necessary support for the groups' economic and social activities; to maintain
oversight on the progress of the project and to establish linkages between SHGs and the service
providers required to sustain and further the development of the SHG members in the long term.
The CLWG would meet monthly.
E. Project Management
39.   A reputed management institute, would be contracted by the CPSU to develop a
Computerised Project Management system (CPM) and Management Information System (MIS)
(as part of the overall M&E system design) for use (with refinements as required) by all the WDCs
in the participating states. Each participating state would hire an independent M&E agency to
initially operate the CPM/MIS systems following training by the lead agency. The state M&E



-174-
Annex 11
agencies would train the WDCs in the operation of the CPM and MIS systems so that they are
gradually able to take over the monitoring role to support project management. (See Annex 14 for
details of the monitoring and evaluation system).
40.   Annual Action Plans would be developed from the CPM, which would be adjusted as
required to reflect changes that have to be made to the initial plans as a result of actual field
experience in project implementation. This will provide details of all tasks to be undertaken during
the year including timing, manpower and financial resources and established milestones. The
Annual Action Plans would provide the basis framework for organising the project activities.
However, to ensure that the project remains responsive to the needs of the SHG members, the
Annual Action Plans would be revised on a quarterly basis in the light of the specific requests for
assistance expressed by the SHGs.
41.   Review Processes. Annual review and planning workshops and annual beneifciary
workshops would be held at the state and district levels providing an opportunity for project
beneficiaries and implementors to inter-actively review project performance and incorporate
improvements into the implementation of the project. In addition, inter-state workshops would
be held to facilitate information sharing between states enabling states to benefit from the
learning experiences of other states in implementation of various project activities.

Attachment 1
BACKGROUND AND ASSESSMENT
OF WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT CORPORATIONS
1.    As an outcome of the International Women's Decade which ended in 1985, GOI
requested all states to set up Women's Development Corporations (WDCs) as part of the strategy
to switch the focus of women's development initiatives from welfare to development. The
WDCs were established as autonomous government undertakings in which the share capital
would be contributed by GOI and the state governments in the ratio of 49%:51%. The
authorized share capital of the WDCs is generally around Rs. 50 million of which around Rs. 20-
25 million has been paid up in the case of most of the WDCs.
2.    Nodal agency and incorporation status. Amongst the WDCs participating in the
project there are some variations in terms of their parent department and incorporation status. In
Karnataka, Madhya Pradesh and Uttar Pradesh, the nodal department for the WDC is the
Department of Women and Child Development. In Bihar and Haryana, the WDCs come under
the auspices of the Department of Social Welfare whilst in Gujarat, the Department of Industries
and Mines is the nodal department.
3     Most of the WDCs (Gujarat, Haryana, Karnataka, and Uttar Pradesh) are incorporated
under the Companies Act with the WDC in Madhya Pradesh incorporated under the MP Non-
Trading Corporations Rules and Act. The main exception is the Bihar WDC which is registered
under the Societies Registration Act. Many of the WDCs are seeking exemption from income
tax through classification as non-profit organizations under Section 25 of the Companies Act (or
other provisions specific to their states). The activities and operational procedures of the WDCs
are controlled by the Boards of Directors and they are not obliged to follow government norms
and procedures.
4.    Each WDC has a Board of Directors of between 3 and 15 persons. The prescribed
composition of the Board between officials and non-officials varies between WDCs. In Gujarat
and MP the provisions allow for at least half of the directors to be non-officials, who are
generally political appointees. In Karnataka all directors are appointed by the state government.
In most cases the Boards have been superseded and are presently composed solely of officials.
5.    Only Haryana and Gujarat WDCs have their own district managers or field officers with
limited supporting staff. The other WDCs function at the district level through the staff of other
departments, who are generally paid an additional small stipend.
6.    The principal role of the WDCs is to promote the economic development of women
through: (a) facilitating access to training and entrepreneurship development; (b) helping women
entrepreneurs to gain access to credit available under various bankable schemes through
sanctioning women's applications, and through the provision of capital subsidy or margin money



-176-
Annex 11
Attachment 1
(low interest loans) to enable women to fulfill the banks' requirements for borrower's equity
contribution; (c) facilitating access to markets through organizing periodic exhibitions/sales,
financing women producers to attend such sales outside of the state; tie-up arrangements with
state level marketing organizations or government departments; or through assisting women
producers to establish marketing cooperatives; and (d) conducting of camps, seminars, etc., to
increase women's general level of awareness.
7.    Assistance is provided to all women, rural and urban, although some schemes which the
WDCs implement are targeted at women below the poverty line. The WDCs organize skill
development training in a range of skills, both conventional and non-conventional, including
tailoring, knitwear, typing, beauty culture, electronics and electrical repairs, computing, etc.
Training is conducted through direct engagement of specialized trainers by the WDCs as required
or through contracts with NGOs. All the WDCs participating in the project consider their major
responsibility is to play a promotional and catalytic role and, unlike some WDCs, none of them
are involved in the direct management of production units to provide employment for women.
8.    Budgets. The level of operations currently implemented by the WDCs is modest with
annual expenditures on programs in the range of Rs. 7-26 million per WDC. The main sources
of funds for programs are: (a) funds received from DWCD under special programs, e.g., NORAD
funds for training, STEP program; (b) contributions from state governments; and (c) interest
earnings generated by the WDCs on their share capital. Some WDCs (Bihar, Karnataka) do also
receive budgetary support for their administrative costs, whilst others (Uttar Pradesh, Madhya
Pradesh) are totally reliant on the interest earnings on their capital to pay for the operating costs
of the Corporation.
Assessment of WDCs
9.    The WDCs were established as there was no nodal point at the state level for women's
economic development. They were given a broad mandate but since their establishment, they
have suffered from a number of major constraints which have precluded them from performing
their intended role effectively. These include:
(a)   Lack of adequate finance. Lack of resources has limited the scale of activities of
the WDCs. Lack of clarity over their role as promotional or commercial
organizations has frequently left the WDCs without adequate coverage of their
administrative costs or the capacity to expand their promotional role. This has
also resulted in some cases in the share capital being eroded to finance
administrative expenses.
(b)   Inadequate staffing. The WDCs have not been adequately staffed nor provided
with the requisite skills, viz., business development, marketing, training and
finance. They have suffered from discontinuity in top management through the



-177-
Annex 11
Attachment 1
high turnover of Managing Directors who are generally IAS officers on
deputation. This has resulted in a lack of continuity in the strategies adopted to
achieve the aims and objectives. In particular, they lack a field structure
preventing them from having the direct contact with their client group required to
effectively implement programs and to gain a sound appreciation of the needs and
priorities of women which they are expected to address.
(c)   Weak management procedures. Inadequate attention has been paid to establishing
effective management procedures for the WDCs. As a result, accounting systems,
auditing and planning procedures and monitoring and evaluation systems are
deficient.
(d)   Lack of follow-up support to women entrepreneurs. The WDCs have lacked the
resources to provide any back-up support to the self-employment activities they
have been instrumental in promoting.
9.    Two recent appraisals of the role of the WDCs6 commissioned by DWCD and DANIDA
have supported the need to strengthen the WDCs to enable them to be more effective promoters
of women's economic development. They have concluded that it is essential for the WDCs to
exist and to be strengthened in order to be able to more effectively champion the cause of the all
round development of women. These studies have highlighted the following points:
(a)   Strengthening the organizational structure and clarifying the role of the WDCs
would facilitate their efforts for the enhancement of women's economic status;
(b)   WDCs should play a promotional, catalytic and coordinating role;
(c)   While WDCs should aim to serve all women, priority should be on reaching rural
and urban poor women;
(d)   WDCs should widen their horizons and become more involved in the overall
socio-economic advancement of women including playing a greater advocacy
role;
(e)   WDCs should be provided with adequate budgetary support to cover their
promotional activities;
6/    Appraisal of Women's Development Corporations; Women's Development Division, Nationai Institute of
Public Cooperation and Child Development, September 1989
Role of Women's Development Corporations; Sarojini Kumtakar, Council for Social Development,
January 1994



-178-
Annex 11
Attachment 1
(f)   WDC staff strength should be augmented, greater stability of tenure of the
Managing Director ensured and provision made for WDC to have its own staff at
the district level. All staff should be oriented to recent developments on women's
issues, problems and developmental programs;
(g)   WDCs should provide back-up support to the self-employed beneficiaries
through facilities for technical consultancy. To this end, the WDCs should have
access to consultants to provide specialist inputs as required in support of the
activities promoted and to provide advice and guidance to the WDCs.

Attachment 2
RESPONSIBILITIES OF KEY IMPLEMENTING AGENCIES
Central Project Support Unit (CPSU)
I1.    The role of the CPSU would be capacity building of the project implementors, information
exchange, and coordination of monitoring and evaluation of project performance.
2.    The principal responsibilities of the CPSU would be to:
(a)   Facilitate information exchange across states. It is hoped that the states will move
towards rich information sharing (e.g., training and communications materials,
research, lessons learned) by establishing an informal network amongst themselves
including organisation of a program of inter-state workshops hosted by states in
turn. The CPSU would monitor to make sure that this is happening, and propose
any forums, for sharing mechanisms which are not naturally being developed.
(b)   Provide central administrative fnctions. These include: (i) facilitating release of
funds from GOI to the participating states; (ii) ensuring submission of annual action
plans and progress reports by the states for onward submission to WB/IFAD; and
(iii) contracting any services which may span participating states, e.g., contracting
the lead M&E agency to develop the CPM/MIS systems across the states;
commissioning of production of films, e.g., for the promotion of micro-enterprise
development, which can be used across all the states.
(c)   Facilitate learning over time. This would include facilitating the collation and
dissemination of information on key areas of relevance to project implementation
through contracting out periodic reviews/studies to specialist agencies or
individuals. Key areas which can be identified at the outset would include:
(i)  collation of information and experience nationwide on women's
development efforts and approaches in the government and non-government
sectors;
(ii)  collation of information and experience nationwide on approaches to gender
sensitisation, materials available, etc., in the government and non-
government sectors;
(iii)  preparation of an inventory of training and communications materials
available nationwide in areas of relevance to the project, e.g., group



-180-
Annex 11
Attachment 2
formation, savings and credit, entrepreneurship and micro-enterprise
development, women's health, nutrition, etc.;
(iv)   preparation of an inventory of appropriate technologies available nationwide
in the areas of drudgery reduction;
(v)   analysis of project implenmentation experience and impact in the
participating states over time as part of the overall process of learning,
transfer of innovations and interchange of experiences;
(d)   Provide conceptual and strategic guidance for effective project implementation and
to ensure project design remains responsive to women's emerging needs and
aspirations.
(e)     Coordinate monitoring and evaluation of project performance to be undertaken by
independent M&E agencies and ensure the incorporation of lessons learnt into
project design and implementation processes
Women's Development Corporation (WDCs)
3.    The key role played by the WDCs would be in promoting the project concept, and guiding,
coordinating and monitoring the implementation of the project.
4.    The main line responsibilities of the WDCs at the state and district levels would be: (a)
providing overall direction and guidance to the project including promoting and safeguarding the
project concept and re-orienting project strategies in line with new thinking on women's issues and
in response to women's evolving needs and priorities; (b) coordinating the required project inputs
for mobilisation of women, nurturing of SHGs, and access to resources for development; (c)
contracting with NGOs and other project partners; (d) monitoring and supervising project
implementation; (e) liaising with the state M&E agency contracted to undertake concurrent
monitoring and evaluation; (f) liaising with the line departments, banks and other implementing
agencies; (g) releasing funds to the partner implementing agencies; (h) preparing annual and
quarterly action plans and budgets; (i) preparing quarterly progress reports; and () preparing and
submitting reimbursement claims to DWCD for onward transmission to the Department of
Economic Affairs, GOI.
NGOs
5.    The role of the NGOs would be to mobilise poor women in the community, assist them to
form SHGs and provide on-going support to nurture and sustain the SHGs. This would include
providing awareness raising for SHG members, training in capacity building for the SHGs,
facilitating linkage with lending institutions and ensuring access of SHG members to the necessary



-181-
Annex 11
Attachment 2
technical support services for their selected income generating activities. In addition, the more
experienced NGOs would play a key role in capacity building of less experienced NGOs and
orientation of project implementors to the SHG concept and participatory processes. Full details of
the responsibilities of the NGOs are given in Annex 5.
Technical Support Agencies
6.    Technical support agencies, comprising both line departments and private sector
agencies/individuals would be responsible for providing women with the necessary information to
assist them in the selection of appropriate income generating activities and for providing technical
support through training, technology transfer, assistance with the establishment of market linkages
to ensure the success of their business activities.
Lending institutions
7.    Banks and other lending institutions would be responsible for providing credit to the SHGs
based on their assessment of the credit management capability of the SHG.

TERMS OF REFERENCE
FOR UPGRADING WDC FINANCIAL SYSTEMS
1.    At present the level of financial transactions undertaken by the WDCs is minimal. As a
result, they do not have appropriate financial management procedures in place to cope with the
increasing workload under the project. In addition, with the incorporation of district level units and
activities there is a need to put in place more systematic and rigorous accounting and control
procedures. The WDCs will also need to establish separate accounts for the project which can be
independently audited in a timely manner.
2.    To upgrade the financial management systems, the WDCs would engage experienced
independent consultants to carry out a review of the existing accounting and financial control
procedures and assist in the establishment of upgraded systems at both the head quarters and district
levels for accounting, auditing and internal control which are acceptable to IDA. More specifically
the consultants would:
(a)   review and implement necessary changes to upgrade the existing accounting
systems and procedures;
(b)   review the existing internal and extemal audit procedures and recommend necessary
changes;
(c)   review the existing internal control procedures and upgrade them to internationally
acceptable standards; and
(d)   train WDC accounts and management staff in the modified procedures and oversee
the implementation of the improved procedures.
3.    Time frame and reporting
The work would commence immediately after completion of the audit work in progress of
pending accounts and would be completed by June 1997.



-196-
Annex 11
Attachment 6
STUDY ON LONG-TERM ROLE OF WDCs
TERMS OF REFERENCE
1.    Two recent appraisals of the WDCs7 supported the need to strengthen the WDCs to enable
them to be more effective promoters of the all round development of women. However, given the
limited role being currently played by WDCs, to date, no action has been taken to operationalize
these recommendations. The actions proposed under the project for the immediate strengthening of
the WDCs to manage the project will go some way to addressing the issues raised in these studies.
Once the WDCs have gained experience through implementation of their expanded role under the
project, it would be timely to further review the progress they have made and to re-assess the long-
term role of the WDCs against the background of changes in the overall environment within the
country and the specific changes made in widening the scope of the WDCs' activities as a result of
the project. Accordingly, prior to the Mid-Term Review, the CPSU would commission a study
from consultants/individuals with wide-ranging experience of institutional development and
women's issues to review the performance and to make recommendations on the long-term roles of
the WDCs. The findings of this review would be used at Mid-Term Review to guide introduction
of changes in the roles and responsibilities of WDCs. More specifically the study would:
(a)   undertake an assessment of the performance of the WDCs under the on-going
project;
(b)   critically analyse the future role of the WDCs;
(c)   suggest changes in the organizational structure required to meet the future needs
specifying relationships with public and private sector institutions including NGOs
and lending institutions;
(d)   specify improvements in management systems required to make the operation of
the WDCs more efficient in carrying out its responsibilities; and
(e)   recommend requirements for capacity building to enable the WDCs to perform their
role more effectively.
7     Appraisal of Women's Development Corporations; Women's Development Division, National Institute of
Public Cooperation and Child Development, September 1989
Role of Women's Development Corporations; Sarojini Kumtakar, Council for Social Development,
January 1994
