Annex 12
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
TRAINING
Introduction
1.   In order to support project activities in each state, a comprehensive training program
would be implemented to upgrade the skills of WDCs, NGOs, SHGs, lending institutions and
line departments in a variety of areas. It is expected that training needs would evolve during the
project in a manner corresponding to the evolution of SHGs and their activities. The major
phases currently envisaged in the project are: (a) pre-project, (b) project start-up, (c) project
build-up, and (d) stabilization (see Annex 16).
I In the pre-project phase, the emphasis would be on appointment of key staff in the
WDCs at state and district levels, and on working out common approaches for several
sub-components of the project including training, communications, and monitoring
and evaluation.
* In the start-up phase, the focus of activity would be on the establishment of SHGs
capable of managing savings and credit operations. During this phase the capacities of
WVDCs and NGOs to form and support SHGs would be developed, and institutional
arrangements for the project would be worked out in detail. Communications
activities focussed on whole village communities would help to build a supportive
environment for the project.
* In the build-up phase, the institutional capacity to manage the project would be
greatly strengthened, including that of support agencies such as banks, line
departments and technical resource agencies. During this phase, linkages between
SHGs and lending institutions would be developed in order to ensure women's
continued access to credit.
* The stabilization phase is characterised by increasing involvement of SHGs in
income generating activities, through provision of skills training, design assistance,
technology and market linkages. In addition, women's access to better health and
drudgery reducing technologies would be enhanced, and community assets created,
which would call for additional training of SHGs and project implementors in the
social aspects related to the project.
2.    Training activities in each phase would build on those in the previous phase with the help
of training needs assessments (TNAs), overseen by the Training Coordinator of the WDC, and
on feedback from the monitoring and evaluation system. The TNAs would be conducted



-202-
Annex 12
periodically to: (a) identify the training needs of staff at all levels by functional groups; (b) match
available training resources with identified needs, and specify gaps in training resources in each
state. These steps would lead to the preparation of a training plan and appropriate training
programs for the next phase of the project. Both the TNAs and the M&E system would provide
information to evaluate previous training efforts, which would be used to improve subsequent
training.
Contents of Training Component
3.    The following areas of training are envisaged as key for the members of SHGs and staff
of WDCs and NGOs. The first three areas listed below would also be applied to the staff of
lending institutions, line departments and other technical support agencies, while the latter two
would be provided to this group if needed and/or in appropriately modified form..
*  The SHG concept and approach: principles of cooperation; group dynamics and
group management, including the formation and organization of groups; roles and
responsibilities of group members; characteristics of good groups; group activities,
meetings and minute-keeping; and collective action;
- Participatory processes, and the importance of the participatory approach in
stimulating and supporting group activities through all phases of the project;
3  Communications skills, including information gathering, negotiation and conflict
resolution, and monitoring and evaluation skills;
-  Savings and credit management, including formal and informal banking systems,
dealing with financial institutions, processing of loans and their repayment, utilization
of loans and the maintenance of assets created out of loans, establishment of interest
rates and repayment periods, loan defaults and related issues, understanding
passbooks; and
*  Accounting, maintenance of basic books of accounts, record keeping and report
writing.
4.    Further, specific modules would be developed to meet the training needs of particular
groups, including:
*  Economic organization for SHGs: economic rules, the benefits of collective savings
and investment, collective bargaining, importance of occupation specific skills, and
potential for economic activity;
*  Planning and management for WDC staff: management skills, planning, financial
management, critical path scheduling, management systems, monitoring and
evaluation; and



-203-
Annex 12
Entrepreneurship and business development for NGOs, to improve their ability to
guide women in selection of appropriate income generating activities and coordinate
the provision of necessary support systems.
5.    Gender sensitization would be an important aspect of the training of all project
participants, and would include topics such as the need for collective action among women to
combat social, economic and political problems, empowerment of women at the grassroots,
women heads of households, and legal safeguards.
6.    At the start of the project, the focus would be on training SHGs, NGOs and WDC staff in
the five areas listed in para 3 above; and all project implementors, i.e., NGOs, WDCs, lending
institutions and line departments, would be trained in the topics noted, and would be gender
sensitized. From the second year of the project, the need for technical/production skills and
business development training for SHG members would develop and would be provided as the
women begin to take up income generating activities. The scope of the skills development
program would be determined by the activities adopted by the women from the menu of options
prepared for income generating activities (see Annexes 8 and 9). Such training would be
coordinated with the relevant line department where applicable. Training would also be
organized in nutrition, health, family welfare and other issues, depending on the needs and
demand expressed by group members.
Training Activities at the State Level
7.    Based on the broad areas of training identified above for SHGs and project implementors,
the following training-related activities would be undertaken in the project:
(a)   Preparation of an Inventory of Training Resources. To facilitate the WDCs in
formulating the overall training plan, each WDC would prepare an inventory of
existing training programs, resources and materials available in, or accessible to,
the state. Terms of reference are given in Attachment 1. This inventory would
also identify the gaps in training resources within the state level which could
specifically be redressed with the assistance of resources from outside the state.
The WDCs would need to access this database regularly to identify appropriate
training resources for the different training requirements under the project, and
therefore the inventory would be "user friendly". The inventory would focus on
the following:
(i)   Identification of NGOs and other agencies in or available to the state
which have the capacity to conduct training appropriate to project needs;
(ii)   Development of a detailed catalogue of the training programs run by these
NGOs and agencies and their availability for (i) WDC staff, (ii) NGOs,
(iii) SHGs, (iv) banks, (v) line departments, (vi) technical support



-204-
Annex 12
agencies; the catalogue should specify type of training, duration,
availability during the year and costs;
(iii)  Identification of key training materials available with the training
agencies, or from other accessible sources, which can be utilized in the
training programs, for refresher training, or as reference materials for the
trainees in the above categories;
(iv)   Assessment of the capacity of these training agencies to provide training to
the above groups during the project and, where applicable, identification
of the training needs of the organizations themselves, to equip them to
conduct training which is relevant to the project;
(v)    Assessment of the costs of different training modules offered by these
agencies.
(b)    Development of Training Strategy. Given the varied and evolving nature of the
training requirements under the project, it would be useful for the states to inter-
actively develop a framework for training with the assistance of an institution
experienced in the planning and implementation of training programs. The CPSU
would identify a lead training institute to organise a workshop to help the
participating states to develop the training strategy in the pre-project phase. The
strategy would be supported by identified modules for each type of training, and
information on the duration, location and agency which would be responsible for
providing the training. The lead institute would work closely with the state
WDCs and implementing NGOs to:
(i)    clarify with each state, through a participatory process, the training
priorities of the project, and develop the training strategy;
(ii)   assess state-specific training needs in conjunction with the various project
affiliates: WDCs, NGOs, SHGs, banks, line departments, and technical
support agencies;
(iii)  assist WDCs, as necessary, in identifying appropriate training modules and
materials and institutions/individuals who could develop additional
modules/materials to fill gaps in these training resources;
(iv)   undertake training of trainers, as necessary, to further develop the
capacities of identified institutions to meet the training needs of the
project.
This work would build on the inventory of training resources prepared in each state, and
would in turn help to refine the inventory.



-205-
Annex 12
(c)    Training Plans. Based on this strategy, each WDC would prepare an annual
training plan, which would be updated quarterly, and also take into account
feedback received from the concurrent M&E, to regularly reassess training needs.
The training plan would separately identify: (i) the target group to receive
training, including trainers; (ii) the issues/subjects to be addressed; (iii) the
number of staff to be trained in each category, and number of courses, batches
and trainees each year; (iv) the availability of training programs and materials;
(v) the need to develop new training curricula, modules and materials; and (vi)
the unit cost of training. This training plan would need to be flexible, and capable
of addressing the evolving needs of the project. Utilizing the training strategy,
training plan and the inventory of training resources, the Training Coordinator and
District Project Managers would make the necessary arrangements for the training
programs and participation of relevant persons. Ensuring the timeliness of
training would be one of the most important tasks of the Training Coordinator and
Project Director. The initial phases of the project provide the opportunity to
develop the training resources that would be necessary to carry out training in the
later, more complex stages of project implementation.
(d)    Training Needs Assessments. Training needs assessments would be conducted
periodically to determine the training requirements for the next phase of project
implementation. The training strategy would provide guidance regarding these,
and the lead training institute would help the WDCs to identify appropriate
institutions to carry out these assessments.
(e)    Appointment of a Training Coordinator. The WDCs would take early action
to identify a Training Coordinator at the state level to plan and coordinate the
overall training program for the project in the state and oversee its phased
implementation. The hiring of the Training Coordinator is a Condition of
Negotiations.
(f)    Criteria for Selection of Training Institutions. The criteria to be used in
choosing appropriate training agencies would include:
(i)   Institutional data, including the history of the organization, its appropriate
training experience, its past and present capacity to conduct relevant
training programs, the qualifications of its trainers, its strategies for
upgrading and refreshing the skills of trainers, availability of its training
capacity at short notice, and the costs per program;
(ii)   The types of training conducted: Who are the target groups? Are the
modules congruent with the training needs of the project? Is the duration
of the training programs suitable for the persons to be trained in this
project? Are the training methods appropriate for the trainees and in



-206-
Annex 12
keeping with the project's participatory principles? What training
materials are available and/or used?
(iii)  The development of training modules: Was it needs-based? Was there an
adequate system of evaluation and feedback? Was the feedback
incorporated while refining the training modules? Does the institution
have the ability and flexibility to adapt the contents and methods of their
training to project and local needs, issues, problems, demands?
(iv)   A cross-check with target groups on the usefulness of the training as well
as the quality of the trainers; and
(g)   Follow-up of Training Implementation. The lead training institution would
continue to provide guidance and advice to the state WDCs throughout project
implementation. Possible areas of assistance include: (i) reviewing and revising
the training strategy; (ii) conducting training needs assessments in conjunction
with the various project affiliates; (iii) developing appropriate training modules;
(iv) undertaking training of trainers; (v) putting WDCs in touch with out-of-state
training resources at their request; (vi) following up the effectiveness of the
training strategy; and (vii) developing a system for inter-state sharing of
experiences. In instances where there are very few persons to be trained in a state
in some specific aspect of the project, the lead institute would assist the WDCs in
the different states to coordinate their efforts to organise a joint training program
and share their resources.
(h)   Training for Income Generating Activities. Techno-economic surveys would be
undertaken to generate a menu of options for income generating activities that
could be taken up by SHGs. Depending on the activities adopted from this menu
by SHG members, a skills training program would be developed at the appropriate
time by the WDCs. The Training Coordinator would therefore need to work
closely with the technical agencies supporting development of the income
generating activities.
(i)    Monitoring and Evaluation. A system would be developed for monitoring the
effectiveness of training programs on an on-going basis and feedback from this
monitoring would be utilized to improve the focus and quality of these training
programs.
(j)    Coordination. The Training Coordinator in each state WDC would work
especially closely with the Communications Officer, to ensure that the training
and communications strategies and activities are well coordinated throughout the
project, to avoid duplication, and to maximize the mutual reinforcement that
would take place between these efforts.



-207-
Annex 12
Attachment
INVENTORY OF TRAINING RESOURCES
TERMS OF REFERENCE
Background
1.   In order to support project activities in each state, a comprehensive training program
would be implemented for upgrading the skills of WDCs, NGOs, SHG members, banks, line
departments and technical resource agencies in a variety of areas. During the pilot phase of the
project, the WDC in each state will appoint a Training Coordinator, responsible for matters
relating to training and skill development. The Training Coordinator would work with a team to
develop the comprehensive training program. The team would consider several factors while
formulating the program: (a) the target group to receive training; (b) the issues/subjects that need
to be addressed; (c) the number of staff in each category to be trained; (d) the phasing of training,
and consequently the type and number of training courses, batches and trainees each year; (e) the
need for training of trainers; (f) the availability of appropriate training curricula, modules and
materials, and the need to develop new ones; and (g) the unit cost of training.
Outline of Tasks to be Carried Out
2.    To assist the training teams to formulate the training program and to access available
training resources, the WDC in each state is preparing an inventory of existing training materials
and resources available in each state. The inventory would focus on the following:
(a)   Identification of NGOs and other agencies in the state level or available to the
state to conduct training in the following areas:
(i)   The SHG concept and approach, including principles of cooperation,
group dynamics and group management, formation and organisation of
groups, roles and responsibilities of group members, characteristics of
good groups, and group activities, meetings and minute keeping;
(ii)   Participatory processes, including the importance of participatory
approaches in stimulating and supporting group activities;
(iii)  Economic organization for SHGs, including economic rules, the benefits
of collective savings and investment, collective bargaining, importance of
occupation specific skills, and potential for economic activity;
(iv)   Savings and credit management, including formal and informal banking
systems, dealing with financial institutions, processing of loans and their
repayment, utilization of loans and the maintenance of assets created out



-208-
Annex 12
Attachment
of loans, establishment of interest rates and repayment periods, loan
defaults and related issues, understanding pass books;
(v)   Communications skills, including information gathering, negotiation and
conflict resolution, and monitoring and evaluation skills;
(vi)   Accounting, maintenance of basic books of accounts, record keeping and
report writing;
(vii)  Gender sensitization, including the role of collective action among women
in addressing their social, economic and political problems, empowerment
of women at the grassroots, special concerns of women heads of
households, and important legal safeguards and provisions for women;
(b)   Development of a detailed catalogue of the training programs run by these NGOs
and agencies and their availability for (i) WDC staff, (ii) NGOs, (iii) SHGs, (iv)
banks, (v) line departments, (vi) technical support agencies; the catalogue should
specify type of training, duration, availability during the year and costs;
(c)   Identification of key training materials available with the training agencies, or
from other accessible sources, which can be utilized in the training programs, for
refresher training, or as reference materials for the trainees in the above
categories;
(d)   Assessment of the capacity of these training agencies to provide training to the
above groups during the project and, where applicable, identification of the
training needs of the organizations themselves, to equip them to conduct training
which is relevant to the project;
(e)   Assessment of the costs of different training modules offered by these agencies.
Schedule for Completion of Tasks
3.    The activities outlined above should be completed before negotiations. The material
collected as a result of this activity will be an important input for a training workshop that will be
held towards the end of the pilot phase of the project.

Annex 13
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
INFORMATION AND COMMUNICATIONS
Introduction
1.    Communications support under the project would underpin other project activities, and
fulfil the following objectives:
(a)   to promote access to information and enhance collective knowledge related to
project objectives, approaches and methods, in order to give meaning to the
participatory principles of the project;
(b)   to assist in the process of attitudinal change at all levels, particularly to sensitize
people to gender and participation issues;
(c)   to promote the sharing of experiences among Self Help Groups (SHGs) to
strenigthen their abilities to carry out the project effectively;
(d)   to ensure res ponsiveness of the project to the changing needs of SHG members
facilitate communication between SHGs and intervenors;
(e)   to enhance SHGs' interactions with their communities to ensure harmonious
relations, resolve conflicts and facilitate collective action to access social programs
and create community assets;
(f)   to provide support material and follow-up to training activities;
(g)   to promote exposure fo SHG members to alternative income generating activities,
an appreciation of markets, and ways of forming linkages with markets; and
(h)   to disseminate information about project implementation across participating states,
as well as more widely to interested national and international agencies.
Communications Strategy
2.    Four broad approaches are proposed for the communications strategy:
(a)   increasing awareness among the target communities, including tribal groups,, of the
project's concepts and approaches, and services to be provided by the project;



-210-
Annex 13
(b)   sensitizing the staff of the WDCs, NGOs, lending institutions, line departments and
technical resource agencies to gender and participation issues, to enhance the
relevance and quality of services provided;
(c)   improving the target group's knowledge of financial, income generating and social
support services, and of their rights, and promoting the services in a manner that
increases the target group's willingness to utilize them and, where possible, to share
in the costs of service delivery; and
(d)   propagating information about the project to the wide range of project entities and
other interested agencies.
The communications strategy would take into account the different phases of project
implementation, and the specific situations of each project state (or area), particularly those related
to gender, tribal, poverty, and infrastructure development issues.
3.    The development of the communications strategy would be based on:
identification of specific target groups ("segments");
selection of messages; and
selection of media.
(a).   Target Groups. The broad target groups for communications activities would
include:
members of the SHGs, the priority target group, who would need information to
facilitate initiation of and participation in project activities, and to help them
improve access to and utilization of financial, technical and social services;
target communities, both women and men, who would need to be made aware of the
project, sensitized to gender and poverty issues, and motivated to participate in or
support the project;
WDC staff who would need to be sensitized to gender and social issues, and kept
closely in touch with the experiences of SHGs and communities while the project is
in progress; they are key intervenors who would also be instrumental in spreading
information about the project more widely;
NGO staff, who would need to learn about the functioning of support agencies such
as lending institutions and line departments, in order to work closely with htem in
project implementation; and who would be key providers of information both to and
from SHGs and communities;



-211-
Annex 13
staff of the lending institutions, line departments and other technical resource
agencies, who would need to be sensitized to the concerns and priorities of SHG
members and communities, and to learn about project implementation while in
process; and
project entities in other participating states and the Centre, and other national and
international agencies who would benefit from learning of project experiences and
would share other relevant information with project managers, thereby encouraging
and supporting the project.
(b).   Messages. The contents of the messages communicated would reflect the specific
phase of the project and local/target group considerations. Special attention would
be paid to tribal communities, given the socio-cultural, linguistic, livelihood and
infrastructural circumstances of these groups. The messages would focus on the
major project approaches and components, namely:
* the SHG concept, group functioning, participatory processes and democratic
decision making, community interaction and collective action for
development;
* gender sensitization and women's legal rights;
* savings, credit rotation and accessing credit from lending institutions;
* income generating activities, including promoting awareness of economic
activities, entrepreneurship development, and providing technical information
on agriculture, animal husbandry, appropriate technology, resource
management and environmental issues; and
* availability and use of social services, including health, education/literacy,
family welfare, nutrition, water supply and sanitation.
(c).   Media. A variety of media would be used to accomplish the objectives of the
communications strategy, with a stress on participatory and interactive approaches
such as melas, demonstrations, plays, and puppet shows. Films, video- and audio-
tapes, booklets, pamphlets and newsletters would also be used. Existing
communications resources which could be utilised in the project would be
identified and compiled into a communications inventory described in para 7(a)
below. These would be evaluated for their suitability and new materials
commissioned to supplement them as needed to ensure that the project's
objectives, target groups and messages would be covered.
Communications Activities at the State Level
4.    To facilitate the implementation of communications activities, the WDCs would take the
following steps:



-212-
Annex 13
(a)   Preparation of an inventory of communications resources: In each state, the
WDCs would identify locally available communications materials and
communications agencies, including NGOs, involved in the development and
dissemination of relevant materials. The inventory would include: information on
the previous experience of the resource agencies in working with target groups
similar to those involved in the project; a profile of materials developed by the
agencies in the areas identified in para. 5 above; and the costs, by agency, for
available materials and for developing and pre-testing new materials. It would
also provide an assessment of the capacity of the agency to undertake/participate
in the communications activities for the project. The terms of reference for the
inventory of communications resources is attached.
(b)   Appointment of a Communications Officer: WDCs would identify and appoint a
Communications Officer at the state level to assist in planning and coordinating
the implementation of the communications strategy.
(c)   Preparation of a Communications Plan: Following an initial assessment of the
communications needs of each community, the WDCs and implementing NGOs
would draw up a communications plan which would best address the identified
needs. This plan would be updated periodically in response to the changing needs
of the project. With the help of the communications inventory, the WDCs and
implementing NGOs would identify the appropriate agencies to undertake
communications activities, deciding on approaches, messages, media, scheduling
and duration in a consultative manner.
(d)   Newsletter: From the second year onwards, a monthly newsletter would be
produced in each state by the WDC and distributed to all SHGs, NGOs, line
departments, lending institutions, technical reource agencies, and the WDCs and
members of these groups in the other project states. The newsletter would serve to
disseminate factual information, to provide news of project activities and to
facilitate exchange of views and experiences. The WDC Communications Officer
would be responsible for compiling the newsletter based on information received
from the districts and other relevant sources. The newsletters would form an
important input for project implementors to understand women's needs and
priorities and the progress of the project. SHGs would also be encouraged to
produce simple newsheets to share experiences and knowledge with the
community - factual information can be extracted from the newsletters. The
newsheet would be displayed at places in the village where people gather, e.g.,
school, market, Panchayat building, etc.
(e)   Monitoring and Evaluation. A system for monitoring the effectiveness of the
communications activities on an on-going basis would be developed, and the
feedback from it utilized to better target and improve subsequent communications
efforts. Conversely, the communications activities would also address, where



-213-
Annex 13
useful, the issues and problems brought to light by the monitoring and evaluation
of other project activities.
(f)   Training and the Communications Strategy. The communications strategy would
supplement and not duplicate training activities. As some areas, such as gender
sensitization and group formation, are common areas of focus the WDCs would
identify areas of overlap and develop systems to ensure that the activities under
each component reinforce each other. The Training and Communications Officers
in the WDCs would work together for this purpose.
Training for Communications
5.    To ensure proper use of the communication materials, project intervenors such as WDC
staff and NGOs, would be trained in the proper use of audio-visual and other material. They
would also be trained in how to use various media to advance participation and interactive
communication, rather than for passive viewing through video presentations, etc. Workshops
would be held in each district for the field workers of partner NGOs to update their
communication skills and use of media. The NGO workers would provide training to SHG
members in the production of materials such as posters and newsheets.
6.    Communications Equipment. Communications equipment for production and
dissemination is widely available within India and, hence, to avoid the problems associated with
owning and maintaining equipment, the project would contract production services and hire
equipment such as TVs and video recorders, which are available for hire in most large villages.



-214-
Annex 13
Attachment
Inventory of Communications Resources
Terms of Reference
Background
1.    An effective communications component would be an integral part of project
implementation. It would fulfill the following objectives:
(a)   to promote access to information and enhance collective knowledge related to
project objectives, approaches and methods, in order to give meaning to the
participatory principles of the project;
(b)   to assist in the process of attitudinal change at all levels, particularly to sensitize
people to gender and participation issues;
(c)   to promote the sharing of experiences among self help groups (SHGs) to strengthen
abilities to carry out the project effectively;
(d)   to facilitate communication between SHGs and intervenors on the changing pattern
of needs, concerns, priorities and perspectives, in order to ensure continuous
responsiveness of project planning to these;
(e)   to enhance SHGs' interactions with their communities to ensure harmonious
relations, resolve conflicts and facilitate collective action to access social programs
and create community assets;
(f)   to provide support material and follow-up to training activities;
(g)   to promote exposure fo SHG members to alternative income generating activities,
an appreciation of markets, and ways of forming linkages with markets; and
(h)   to disseminate information about project implementation across participating states,
as well as more widely to interested national and international agencies.
2.    The development of the communications strategy entails: (a) identification of specific target
groups ("segments"); (b) selection of messages; and (c) selection of media. The broad target
groups identified for communications activities include: (a) members of the SHGs, (b) target
communities, both women and men; (c) WDC staff, (d) NGO staff, (e) staff of lending
institutions, line departments and other technical resource agencies, (f) project entities in other
participating states and the Centre, and (g) other national and international agencies. The messages
would focus on the major project approaches and components, and would reflect the specific phase
of the project and local/target group considerations. Special attention would need to be paid to



-215-
Annex 13
Attachment
tribal communities. A variety of media would be used to accomplish the objectives of the
communications strategy, with a stress on participatory and interactive approaches such as melas,
demonstrations, plays, and puppet shows. Films, video- and audio-tapes, booklets, pamphlets
and newsletters would also be used. These Terms of Reference concern the development of an
inventory of existing communications resources which could be utilised in the project.
Outline of the Tasks to be Carried Out
3.   In order to facilitate the development of an implementation plan for the communications
activities, an inventory of communications resources available in the state is to be prepared. This
inventory should focus on the following:
(a)   Identification of NGOs and other agencies involved in communications activities
and in developing and disseminating communications materials.
(b)   Details of their previous experience of working with target groups similar to those
involved in the project, for example:
(i)    members of groups at the village level, especially women's groups and
SHGs;
(ii)   village communities in general, both women and men;
(iii)  NGOs and organizations such as the WDCs, who are the main project
implementors;
(iv)   government functionaries working in the field as well as officials of banks
and line departments at block, district and state levels, who will play an
important role in facilitating project implementation.
(c)   Development of a profile of materials available with the resource agencies in the
following areas:
(i)   gender sensitization, women's issues and legal rights;
(ii)   group development, including the SHG concept, group functioning and
dynamics, participatory processes and decision-making, collective
community action;
(iii)  savings, credit rotation and accessing credit from lending institutions;
accounts and record keeping;



-216-
Annex 13
Attachment
(iv)  income generating activities, including promotion of economic activities,
entrepreneurship development, and technical information for agriculture,
animal husbandry, appropriate technology, resource management and
environmental issues; and
(iv)  social services, such as health, education, family planning, nutrition, water
supply and sanitation.
(d)   Assessment of the costs, by agency, of available materials and for developing and
pre-testing a sample of relevant new materials.
(e)   Assessment of the capacity of the communications agency to undertake or provide
support to communications activities in the project.
Schedule for Completion of Tasks
4.    The activities outlined above should be completed before project effectiveness. The
material collected in the inventory would be important input for the development of the
communications plan by the end of the pilot phase of the project.



-217-
Annex 14
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
MONITORING AND EVALUATION
Background
1.    To coordinate the diverse activities of the project, the monitoring and evaluation system
would need a computerized project management system, as well as a management information
system (CPM/MIS). Given the innovative nature of the project, and the need to benefit from
implementation experience, the CPM/MIS system is essential for effective concurrent monitoring
and evaluation. In addition, an impact evaluation of the overall project on poverty reduction, as
well as other socio-economic indicators, must be carried out to assess whether such programs
have desirable impacts both at the participant level and the society level. The main objective is
to assess the project's self sustainability and cost effectiveness as well as evaluate it's effects on
poverty reduction and empowerment. In particular the M&E will:
* Assess the group sustainability, financial viability, outreach, and institutional viability
of the program.
*  Provide feed-back to relevant project affiliates that is based on process monitoring
and evaluation.
*  Assess via impact evaluations the poverty reduction effects as well as effects on other
socio-economic indicators of the project.
2.    Participatory methods would be promoted so that SHG members are assisted to assess
their own progress in tracking the impacts of their own actions in the family and community.
Sponsoring NGOs would facilitate the SHGs in these processes. In addition, a household survey
will be administered on a sample of households to evaluate the above-mentioned impacts of the
project.
Conceptual Outline
3.    Five modules have been proposed to provide guidance on the selection of indicators for
different components of the project. They provide a guideline for developing the detailed
framework for M&E of the project.
4.    Module 1: Group Formation, Activities and Outreach. The objective of this module
is to assess formation of stable, viable and self-reliant SHGs. This includes measures of group
dynamics such as internal cohesion and homogeneity, equity and savings and loans behavior.
Other qualitative indicators such as relationship among members, group functioning, rotation of
leadership, etc., would also be measured.



-218-
Annex 14
5.    Module 2: Financial Sustainability. Achieving financial sustainability is important to
demonstrate the program's independence from donor funds for its activities. The CPM/MIS
would track the progress made towards financial sustainability. Essentially, the system would
continuously track the income and expenses of the program at the group, cluster/NGO level, and
check whether they are moving towards balancing each other. There are several indicators that
provide useful information on the bottleneck(s) towards achieving financial sustainability. All
indicators must be evaluated at the group level. They may also be evaluated at the
cluster/federation level should financial links evolve for clusters/federations.
6.    Indicators for this module would include measures of savings and loan process, income
and expenses and other variables. Estimates would also be generated of portfolio at risk rate,
loan loss rate, operating costs as a percent of portfolio, total costs as a percent of portfolio,
average loan size/cost per loan, percent of loans with past due amounts, amount of liquid funds
as a percent of loan portfolio, and trends in distribution of loan use. In addition, growth
indicators for tracking progress would be measured. Most of the information pertaining to
savings, loans, repayment, and interest rates in this module would be gathered from the account
books of individual members in the group which would be aggregated for the group in a ledger
book. This aggregation would be facilitated by NGOs initially, and as the groups mature, the
leaders of the groups would take over the task. The expenses and costs figures would be kept by
the NGOs and frequently updated to reflect accurate information. Processing of information at
the group level would also be facilitated, so that groups would be able to assess their progress
and understand and act on the components that are not performing as desired.
7.    Module 3: Institutional Viability. The objective of this module is to assess the
institutional viability of the program at all levels, i.e., groups, clusters, local NGOs, district level
organizations, state WDCs, credit and financial institutions. Indicators would include measures
of group level effectiveness, NGO staff productivity, institutional efficiency (district and state
level) and institutional durability.
8.    Module 4: Empowerment. Choice of variables for this module would be participatory
in order that the measures and indicators reflect the client/group members' perceptions of
empowerment. The baseline survey would include these measures to facilitate assessment of
changes that may have occurred due to project participation. A few key variables for assessment
of empowerment are suggested for evaluation vis-a-vis self, family and community. These
include self-related variables and social family/community variables. The information on the
above variables would be collected via in-depth interviews. Participatory methods of self-
assessment are very important for the groups since this will enable their process of
empowerment. Since self-help groups inculcate positive development values like self-respect
and dignity, these types of impacts are also best captured in detailed case analyses.
9.    Module 5: Impact Assessment. Client, household and intra-household impact of project
participation would be evaluated based on household surveys of targeted samples. A household
survey questionnaire would be developed to capture baseline information, as well as follow-up



-219-
Annex 14
surveys on all key variables to assess household and intra-household impacts in consumption and
production and the poverty reduction effects of the program. A few key variables would be,
among others, patterns of income spending on food, clothing, housing, health, etc., by the client
on herself and her family; asset accumulation; income growth; access to factors of production;
and time use profiles. Since impact is assessed by looking at changes before and after project
experience, care would be taken to control for factors outside the purview of this project that
could influence these measures. Participatory impact assessments, utilizing suitable
methodologies, would also measure these impacts. The results of the qualitative and quantitative
impact assessments would be combined to provide a holistic picture of project impacts on the
target population.
Organizational Structure for M&E
10.   The development of a comprehensive framework for M&E (concurrent and impact) as
well as the design of the CPM/MIS will be undertaken by a lead team composed of individuals
with expertise in participatory methods, computer and information technology, statistical
methods and rural development economics. In addition, the lead agency is strongly encouraged
to hire expert consultants for specific components of the monitoring and evaluation tasks for
which in-house capabilities may be insufficient, i.e., for the design and development of the
CPA/MIS system as well as assessing alternative participatory methods of data collection and
analysis. The main objective of the lead team (TOR attached) would be to develop a detailed
framework for project M&E, using as a guideline the conceptual outline and preliminary set of
indicators that were finalized at the workshop on July 5 1996. The task is two-fold:
*  To establish a framework for concurrent monitoring and evaluation to assess program
implementation, performance and sustainability. This component is critical to
facilitate timely identification and feedback of program constraints and bottlenecks to
ensure timely and effective responses. To this end, a framework for computerized
MIS would be developed.
*  A framework for studying the impact evaluation would be developed to assess the
poverty reduction and other socio-economic impacts of the project. A sound
methodological framework would be necessary since program participation is self-
selective and program placement is context specific. Special household survey design
may be required to estimate both program placement and participation impacts.
11.   The design of the M&E framework, including the identification of appropriate indicators
by the lead team, will be subject to review by World Bank/IFAD at critical points in its
development giving the opportunity to ensure that the system design and list of indicators does
not become too complex and unwieldy. The M&E framework developed by the lead team will
be followed by six independent state agencies who will conduct the monitoring and evaluation
for the project in each state.



-220-
Annex 14
* It will be the lead team's responsibility to ensure that there is consistency of the
programs indicators across states. This will be facilitated by the lead agency in an
inter-state workshop with agencies from all six states before project launch.
* The lead agency would also install the CPM/MIS system in all six state agencies and
train the staff in implementation of the system for smooth operation. This also
includes fine-tuning of the system for the specific needs in each state.
* The lead agency would continue to review on a six-monthly basis, the evaluation
reports of the six agencies to enable inter-state comparisons of project performance as
well as ensure consistency of measurement of the various indicators.
* The lead agency would also be responsible for the impact evaluation of the poverty
reduction effects of the program as well as assessment of other socio-economic
indicators of development. This will be based on household survey.
12.   State Agencies for M&E. Each state would hire a state agency to implement the
monitoring and evaluation for the state (TOR attached). The CPM/MIS developed by the lead
team would be installed in each state agency and the relevant staff in each agency would be
trained and assisted by the lead team until such time as they are able to manage the CPM/MIS
independently. Each state agency would carry out the concurrent monitoring ad evaluation, track
progress, and provide timely feedback to all project affiliates (group members/leader, NGOs, and
WDCs).

Attachment 1
Terms of Reference for the Lead Team for Monitoring and Evaluation
1.    Background: The World Bank and IFAD have entered into an agreement to jointly
finance the proposed Rural Women's Development and Empowerment Project which will cover
six states viz. Bihar, Gujerat, Haryana, Karnataka, Madhya Pradesh, and Uttar Pradesh. The
overall objective of the project would be to launch a program which strengthens processes to
promote economic development of women and to create an environment for social change to
improve their quality of life. To coordinate the diverse activities of the project, the monitoring
and evaluation system would need a computerized project management system, as well as a
management information system (CPM-MIS). Given the innovative nature of the project, and
the need to benefit from implementation experience, the CPM/ MIS system is essential for
effective concurrent monitoring and evaluation. In addition, an impact evaluation of the overall
project on poverty reduction as well as other socio-economic indicators must be carried out to
assess whether such programs have desirable impacts both at the participant level and the society
level.
2.    The main objective of the lead team should be to develop a detailed framework for the
Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) of the project, using as a guideline, the conceptual outline
and preliminary set of indicators that were discussed at the workshop on July 5 1996. The task is
two-fold:
A) To establish a framework for concurrent monitoring and evaluation to assess program
implementation, performance and sustainability. This component is critical to facilitate timely
identification and feedback of program constraints and bottlenecks to ensure timely and effective
responses. To this end, a framework for computerized MIS must be developed.
B) A framework for studying the impact evaluation must be developed to assess the poverty
reduction and other socio-economic impacts of the project. A sound methodological framework
must be provided since program placement is context specific and program participation is self
selective. Special household survey design may be required to estimate both program placement
and participation impacts.
Specific needs for concurrent Monitoring and Evaluation (Task A)
* Define a list of indicators and variables for process monitoring which are consistent with the
program objectives and reflect the different stages of project implementation. These are typically
information on operating costs, loan disbursements, savings, training and other costs as well as
information pertaining to group formation and dynamics including different dimensions of group
functioning, i.e., practice of SHGs, participation in meetings, problem solving abilities,
leadership etc.
* Define the methodology as well as tools to measure each indicator which include a combination
of participatory methods as well as household surveys.



-222-
Annex 14
Attachment 1
* Participatory methods must evolve so that the participants themselves become not just providers
of data. They would also be the analysts who assess their progress in group functioning, track
the impacts of their own actions in the group and community and revise their strategies
accordingly. Ideally, the data should be analyzed at each level (individual members, group,
cluster/area NGO) and the aggregated indicators passed on to the next level and so on. The
information would finally be synthesized to reflect overall progress and performance of the
program.
* Define the methodology to track progress and provide feedback to facilitate project
implementation and performance.
3.    These three components will be useful for developing a framework for the design of a
computerized project management (CPM) and Management Information Systems (MIS) for
concurrent monitoring and evaluation.
Specific Needs for Impact Evaluation (Task B)
* Specify survey methodology
Specific needs:
-     Selection of survey villages within the influence of the program
-     Selection of control villages where program does not exist
-     Census of villages selected for some aggregate indicators and household
information to facilitate household selection for in-depth interviews
-     Benchmark and follow up surveys will use same household and same villages
* Define possible impacts to be evaluated and indicators to be used to evaluate these impacts
* Define clearly the methodology and tools for each indicator.
* Define methods of data collection, and the analytical procedures that will be used to derive
findings on the impacts of all program interventions for poverty reduction and program
sustainability.
* Define i) recommended sample framework and size of stratified samples to ensure that program
impacts on different segments of the population and community as a whole are statistically
meaningful; (Pay attention to the fact that there may be some decay of original sample between
successful surveys) ii) procedures for use of "control" households and villages to isolate the
impacts of alternate poverty reduction programs from other interventions that have taken place in
these villages over the time frame of the project.
Task B may be achieved both via participatory methods of evaluation and based on a carefully
designed household survey.



-223-
Annex 14
Attachment 1
4.    In order to accomplish tasks A and B, the lead agency is strongly encouraged to hire
expert consultants for specific components of the monitoring and evaluation tasks for which in-
house capabilities may be insufficient, i.e., for the design and development of the CPA/MIS
system as well as assessing alternative participatory methods of data collection and analysis.
5.    The M&E framework developed by the lead team will be followed by six independent
state agencies who will conduct the monitoring and evaluation for the project in each state.
*  It will be the lead team's responsibility to ensure that there is consistency of the programs
indicators across states. This will be facilitated by the lead agency in an inter-state workshop
with agencies from all six states before project launch.
*  The lead agency must also install the CPM/MIS system in all six state agencies and train the staff
in implementation of the system for smooth operation. This also includes fine-tuning of the
system for the specific needs in each state.
*  The lead agency will continue to review on a six-monthly basis, the evaluation reports of the six
agencies to enable inter-state comparisons of project performance as well as ensure consistency
of measurement of the various indicators. The report should be sent to the CPSU, who will in
turn, share the report with the World Bank.
*  The lead agency will also be responsible for the impact evaluation of the poverty reduction
effects of the program as well as assessment of other socio-economic indicators of development.
This will be based on household survey. The lead agency will indicate the role of the state
agencies, if any, for this task.
Participatory impact assessments will be mainly conducted by the groups, facilitated by field
officers.
Stages in the Review Process
The review process will occur in five stages:
*  Stage 1: Development of indicators for concurrent evaluation and impact assessment.
*  Stage 2: Development of the design of CPM/MIS for concuuent M&E.
*  Stage 3: Organization of inter-state workshops for implementation of M&E in the six states.
*  Stage 4: Installation and training of CPM/MIS in all state agencies
*  Stage 5: Implementation of the impact evaluation of the poverty reduction effects of the program
as well as assessment of other socio-economic indicators of development.
In addition, the quarterly and six-monthly reviews of the M&E reports by the state
agencies would be reviewed by the lead agency through inter-state workshops and the findings
would be reviewed by the funding organizations.

six states viz. Bihar, Gujarat, Haryana, Karnataka, Madhya Pradesh and Uttar Pradesh. The

* Participate in an inter-state workshop, to be organized by the lead team, to facilitate the
finalization of the activities described above.
5.    During project launch, Participate in the launch workshops and develop a common
understanding of the role of the state agencies in the monitoring and e-aluation component of the
project.
6.    After project launch, The main source of data for the CPM/MIS would be the group
members. Initially the group members would be assisted by the NGO to prepare income
statements, and minutes of the meetings which provide information on all activities of the group.

*  Train the WDCs in the operation of CPM/MIS, so that the monitoring role will gradually be
handed over to them.
*  The state agencies will also facilitate participatory impact evaluations and assist project
affiliates, especially group leaders/members to analyze and process the information. This will
enable participants to track their progress in terms of their own well-being as well as other
socio-economic indicators of change.

RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
STRATEGY FOR INTERVENTIONS IN TRIBAL AREAS
I. Tribal Populations in the Project
1.     Tribal Demography of the Project Districts. Of the six states in the project, four have
sizable tribal populations (Bihar, Gujarat, Kamataka and Madhya Pradesh), while two do not have
any tribal people (Haryana and Uttar Pradesh). Further, some of the project districts have
significant tribal populations, while others have a very low percentage of tribal people in their total
populations. Table ...I gives the percentage of each district's rural population which is tribal, and
highlights those districts in which the total rural population is greater than 25 percent tribal.

-230-
Annex 15
2.    Status of Women. The status of women in tribal societies is generally more equitable than
that of women in non-tribal communities, as demonstrated by the more favorable sex ratio among
tribal people: 977 females per 1000 males for tribal India as a whole, compared to 927 females per
1000 males in the non-tribal population. However, the poverty, isolation of tribal communities, and
low level of literacy among tribal women contributes to their overall disadvantage. High female
work participation rates among tribal groups is a reflection of greater equity - but also of the
greater burden faced by women who are often solely responsible for the well-being of their
households on account of the out-migration of male members, and may have large households to
manage including numerous children. Poor access to social services such as child care facilities,
health care and family planning services, and schooling for children, contribute to this situation.
3.    Socio-Economic Characteristics. The tribal districts in the project are characterized by
widely differing socio-economic, cultural and agro-climatic features. Table ...1 shows the
percentage of females among the tribal populations of each district who were literate at the time of
the 1981 Census; the percentage of tribal women participating in the labor force; and the percentage
of all tribal workers (male and female) who were in the primary sector. Although these data are
based on the 1981 census, they give an indication of the poor status of tribal women in the project
districts, and permit some inter-district and inter-state comparisons. While the percentage of
literate females is likely to have increased since 1981 (by about 10 percentage points at most in any
district), changes in the other two parameters have probably been marginal.
4.    In general, work participation among tribal communities, and especially among tribal
women, is higher than among non-tribal counterparts. Among tribal groups, work was traditionally
related to community ownership of the means of production and so was usually undertaken
collectively. In most areas, tribal people traditionally did not depend entirely on agriculture but
engaged also in collection of forest produce, hunting, craft-making, and so on. This also meant that
tribal workers had a range of skills, such as those for cultivation, animal-skinning, woodwork,
domestic arts, etc. However, many tribes in the central part of the country (Bihar and Madhya
Pradesh) have increasingly adopted the culture of non-tribal peasant communities. Dependence on
agriculture has increased as tribal people have been alienated from forests, and gradual assimilation
into non-tribal ways of life has eroded the traditional skill base. The project's approach of forming
women's Self-Help Groups has special significance in these contexts, as working collectively and
engaging in multiple occupations are integral aspects of tribal culture. The provision of literacy to
tribal women and improving market linkages for tribal produce are also important to improving the
quality of tribal people's lives.
5.    The agro-climatic features of tribal areas in the project districts also vary remarkably. The
Chotanagpur plateau of south Bihar is characterized by red clay soils upland and fertile lower lands
and valleys, with an overall low percentage of irrigated land. About 90 percent of people in these
areas are engaged in agriculture, and about 60 percent of land holdings are under one hectare. Rice
or maize is the staple crop, vegetable gardening is common, and small animals are an important
source of cash income, especially for women. Small farmers supplement their income through
wage labor or selling forest produce, but there are also groups which are largely dependent for their



-231-
Annex 15
livelihoods on the collection and sale of minor forest produce. The situation in the tribal district of
Panchmahals in Gujarat is somewhat the reverse -- greater dependence on forest collection and
wage labor among the tribals. In some other areas of Gujarat there is an emphasis on craft. In
Karnataka, tribal people in the project districts are largely engaged as agricultural labor, with some
involved in sericulture on their own small holdings, and other groups being mainly pastoralists.
6.    Spatial Distribution. Tribal populations usually live clustered in specific blocks and
villages. The higher the proportion of tribal people in a district's rural population, the wider spread
are tribal habitations. For example, in the most highly tribal of the project districts (Ranchi in
Bihar, Bharuch and Panchmahals in Gujarat, and Betul in Madhya Pradesh), almost all blocks of
the district have significant proportions of tribal people, though the actual percentages may vary
widely, as seen in Table .1. A large number of villages in these districts and blocks would have
tribal residents. On the other hand, districts with lower concentrations (e.g., below 20 percent of
their total rural populations) have only a few blocks containing significant tribal populations; and
these people would be living clustered in a few villages. In such situations, the tribal population is
often quite isolated, living in small villages or hamlets with poor access and with limited and
eroding resource bases.
7.    Infrastructure. Areas inhabited by tribal people are less well developed in terms of
infrastructure such as roads, markets, schools, health centres, and so on. Due to their physical
isolation and lack of resources, public systems and facilities are usually understaffed and may offer
virtually no services. As a result, tribal people have less choice of economic activities, lower access
to technology, lower incomes, and poorer social indicators than the general population. These
factors have an important bearing on the project, and linkages between project activities will be
stronger and greater effort will be spent on improving women's access to services in both the
economic and social spheres. The project proposes to harness the Tribal Sub-Plans (TSP) in each
state and the resources provided therein to strengthen the necessary linkages. For this purpose, the
social assessments (see Section II below) have included a review of the local tribal area sub-plans,
and have been followed by the development of specific action plans for each tribal district.
8.    Tribal Institutions. Tribal areas are also characterized by the existence of special
institutions - both traditional and "modern" ones created by the government. In the former
category are moneylenders who exercise a strong hold over poor tribal families. Legislation has not
succeeded in curbing some exploitative practices of moneylenders, such as taking over lands or
bonding labor. Access to public lending institutions is constrained by tribal people's lack of
awareness of facilities, low educational levels constraining abilities to "negotiate" fornal systems,
and physical distance from facilities. In addition, tribal people may be doubly exploited by
unscrupulous traders who charge high prices for goods brought from outside the area, while
offering very low prices for forest and other produce offered in exchange by the tribal people. The
project's approaches of encouraging thrift, credit rotation from among women's own savings, and
improved marketing could help to reduce some of these dependencies and the resultant problems.
Amongst the "modem" tribal institutions are Tribal (or SC/ST) Cooperative Marketing Federations
and Large Area Multipurpose Societies (LAMPS) which encompass primary cooperatives and were



-232-
Annex 15
specifically established for tribal areas. The main functions of the LAMPS are to provide credit to
members for agricultural inputs, including fertilizers, and to purchase produce from farmers at fair
prices on behalf of various apex organizations. In some cases the LAMPS operate PDS centers to
provide basic commodities to tribal people.
9.    Tribal Sub Plans. This project goes beyond the approach of the Tribal Sub Plans in some
significant ways, and builds on institutions which benefit tribal people. A working group set up in
connection with the preparation of the Eighth Five Year Plan found a lack of impact of TSP
investments and ascribed this to a lack of attention to the "felt needs" of tribal people. In contrast,
this project will base its interventions on extensive consultations with tribal people, especially
women. Through such consultations, and the development of women's capacities to participate
further in implementation, the project will focus on the expressed needs and preferences of tribal
women and implement activities which are culturally acceptable to them. In addition, the project
aims to create sustainable livelihoods through the formation of thrift and credit groups and the
introduction and/or enhancement of viable income-generating activities, supported by the necessary
"forward and backward" linkages. Thus, the project is also in tune with several principles espoused
by the Eighth Plan, which are likely to continue in the Ninth Plan, for the development of tribal
peoples: decentralized planning, involving people and non-governmental agencies; local
employment creation; poverty reduction; improving education, health and other social amenities;
and investment for asset creation.
It. The Project's Approach to to Planning for Tribal Women
10.   A Tribal Status Paper and Tribal Development Plans are being prepared for each district in
the project which has a significant tribal population, and would be ready by negotiations. The
Tribal Status Paper collates key information on tribal people in the project areas in order to provide
an analytical base for planning meaningful interventions to improve the economic and social status
of tribal women. The Tribal Development Plans (TDPs) are aimed at ensuring that tribal women in
the project areas participate in project activities, that their capacities to do so are developed, and
that they receive economic and social benefits under the project. The Status Papers and TDPs are
based on social assessments which have been undertaken to understand first hand the socio-
economic situation of tribal women and to participatively plan interventions which can be
implemented within the project framework and are culturally acceptable to them. The assessments
would:
a) collate demographic information on the tribal populations of the proposed project
districts identifying block/village concentrations of tribal people;
b) compile relevant social and economic data on the main tribal groups from secondary
sources; focusing attention on the livelihoods, economic roles and work patterns of
women, their access to major resources, income levels, literacy, health status,
community organizations and other relevant issues, and analyzing and reporting the
information in a manner that is useful for project implementation;



-233-
Annex 15
c) collect information on available infrastructure and government programs which are
being implemented for tribal people in the project districts, including their coverage and
effectiveness and resources available under the local Tribal Sub Plans;
d) collect information from the major NGOs in the project districts on their program
efforts with tribal people, and on their views of the success or failure of these, the
priority needs of the tribal population, and the constraints in addressing them;
e) carry out participatory assessments, including focus group discussions and in-depth
interviews, with tribal people, especially women, to understand their situations, identify
their priority needs and preferences for economic and social interventions which can be
made within the scope of the project, and determine how the project might address
these; in some instances village case studies will be prepared; and
f) refine and organize these suggestions into strategies and specific interventions to be
undertaken to address the key needs of tribal women in the project areas, preparing clear
action plans for the project.
11.   The TDPs would identify the specific objectives to be achieved and strategies to be
followed, the proposed activities, implementation arrangements, and actions to monitor the project
in tribal areas. They would include a set of matrices on the following:
* ProjectActivities: listing Project Objectives, Development Actions to be taken under
the project, and Implementing Agency/agencies; this matrix identifies actions under
sub-sections on, for example, Group Formation, Thrift and Credit, Production/Income-
Generation, Social Development, Training, and Coordination;
*  Organization and Management: giving the Implementing Agency, its Members and
their Functions; the agencies include SHGs, NGOs, local (tribal) community
organizations, line departments, and the Women's Development Corporation;
*  Monitoring and Evaluation: specifying the monitoring Action to be taken, the Agency,
and how the Action is to be carried out.
III. Strategies and Interventions for Tribal Women
12.   From the information gathered on the situations of tribal areas in the project districts, and
through the project's approach to planning for tribal women, an overall strategy and a number of
specific interventions have been identified for tribal populations.
13.   Project Strategy. The overall strategy of the project would be as successful among tribal
communities as among non-tribal groups. The project's approach of forming women's Self-Help



-234-
Annex 15
Groups, motivating members to save and rotate their savings amongst themselves, obtain first cash
credit and then term loans from banks, and avail of project provided skills training, inputs and
linkages, is appropriate to tribal women on account of the poverty and poor resource base of tribal
areas, and it is supported by favorable cultural norms prevailing among tribal people.
14.   In the context of the approach of this project, that of forming village-level Self-Help Groups
through the intermediation of NGOs, the spatial distribution of tribal people has particular
significance. First, to overcome the physical isolation of tribal people and penetrate prevailing
cultural barriers, it will be necessary to involve NGOs that have experience of working with tribal
people, preferably in the same areas or at least with the same or similar tribal groups, and/or NGOs
who are willing to work amongst these groups. NGOs working in districts such as Ranchi (Bihar)
or Panchmahals (Gujarat) which are highly tribal are likely to have the capability to work with
tribal people. In other districts, such as Hoshangabad or Dewas (Madhya Pradesh) or the four
project districts in Kamataka, there may be some NGOs with relevant experience, and others
without. In these cases, judicious selection of NGOs will be called for in order to ensure that the
project reaches some tribal women.
15.   Second, an understanding of the livelihood patterns (including occupations, resource base,
income, and migratory habits) of the tribal people in each district (or sub-district area) is critical to
planning project activities - thrift and credit, income generation, as well as social development - in
ways which are economically and culturally relevant to the tribal group. However, the poorer
infrastructure and difficult conditions of tribal areas suggest that more intensive efforts will be
required to achieve effective results. In this regard, the selection of strong, hard-working NGOs is
critical. Through their intermediation between the tribal communities and mainstream institutions,
such as banks and line departments, to ensure the provision of improved services, some major
constraints faced by tribal people could be removed, or at least reduced. Furthermore, the approach
of the project to provide group members functional literacy and communities with assets, basic
health care and child care could fill major gaps that persist in social services in tribal areas.
16.   Specific Interventions. Among specific interventions, the following have been identified
as most likely to be implemented among tribal communities in the project:
*  Functional literacy for all group members, on account of the low levels of literacy
prevailing among tribal women
*  Consolidation of water resources through group and individual action to improve
drinking water supplies as well as soil moisture for land-based activities; creation of
drinking water facilities for communities and education on maintaining clean water
sources, because tribal areas usually have either extreme water scarcity or shallow
sources of water
*  Enhancing productivity of homestead land for own consumption and market production,
through soil improvement, diversification of crops (vegetables, legumes, fruit trees) and
improved cropping practices, building on land availability and ownership in tribal areas



-235-
Annex 15
*  Improving small animal rearing and management as a primary or secondary income-
generating activity
*  Enhancing home-based processing of agricultural and forest products
*  Improving designs, product quality, and productive efficiency among craftspersons and
artisans, along with better marketing of craft products
*  Basic health care training, because the gap in health services in tribal areas is large;
elements of traditional health care would be introduced by involving specialized NGOs
who have developed capability in this field
*  Community organized 'mini' child care centres, particularly where tribal women work
at some distance from homes or live in secluded hamlets; these may be needed even in
areas where ICDS is operational because of the low coverage afforded by ICDS.
17.   Monitoring and Evaluation. Baseline surveys conducted before project launch will
capture relevant information on the characteristics of tribal women involved in the project. The
M&E component of the project would be appropriately designed to reflect any specific project
inputs in tribal areas which result from the assessments of tribal women's status and Tribal
Development Plans. In districts where SHGs consist mainly of tribal women, the M&E component
would compare the progress of tribal groups to that of other groups, and suitably modify project
inputs, if necessary.

RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
IMPLEMENTATION AND SUPERVISION PLAN
1.     Overall Strategy. Given the innovative nature and decentralized management structure
of the project, strong emphasis would be placed on learning from implementation experience on
an on-going basis to assist problem solving and adjust project design/implementation
approaches as necessary. The main source of information for this purpose would be the
concurrent M&E system, and implementation reviews, progress reports and studies on economic,
social, financial and institutional aspects of the project. This annex consists of: Part A, a set of
detailed implementation charts, covering each phase of project/SHG development; and Part B: a
supervision plan, and Part C: reporting and reviews modalities.

different phases of the project development and SHG maturation.
