B. PROJECT SUPERVISION
3.      Supervision Strategy. The project is by nature complex and will require intensive
management, monitoring and supervision. The creation of monitoring and evaluation capability
within the normal functions of project management in the center as well in the states would be an
important management tool to facilitate successful implementation. To coordinate the diverse
activities of the project, the monitoring and evaluation system would include a computerized
project management system, as well as a management information system (CPM-MIS) to fully
benefit from implementation experience, and to enable effective planning, monitoring and
supervision. (See Annex 14 for more information on the proposed M&E system for the project).



-242-
Annex 16
Other important inputs towards supervision of the project would include: the use of development
indicators; a system of quarterly and annual reports; planning/review workshops at the national,
state and district levels; annual action plans and budgets; special reports /studies on specific
activities; and a mid-term review. Complimentary use of these inputs with mandatory
supervision wok would be the key element of the proposed supervision strategy.
4.    Supervision Plans and Responsibilities. Given the decentralized nature and multi-state
structure of the project, responsibility for supervising the project would be shared between a
number of agencies. Each agency with supervision responsibility would first prepare its own
plan highlighting areas of responsibilities/focus. These plans would then be used to develop an
overall annual plan to maximize coordination and synergy. The main agencies involved in
supervision work would include:
(a)   Central Project Support Unit (CPSU). The CPSU would monitor project's
progress and participate in the supervision work with a special emphasis on
overseeing proposed actions for strengthening of implementation capacity of
WDCs. More specifically CPSU would supervise:: (a) activities to build the
capacities of project implementors, (b) information exchange amongst the state
WDCs and other implementing agencies, and between them and other national
and international agencies with relevant expertise and experience; and (c)
coordinate monitoring and evaluation activities. To facilitate information
exchange across states, the CPSU would propose and organize fora for
information sharing. The CPSU would also commission special
studies/consultancies to analyze the progress of specific aspects of the project and
share this information with the participating agencies as well as use the findings
as inputs to its own (and IDA's) supervision work;
(b)   State Women's Development Corporations (WDC). The state WDCs would be
responsible for promoting the project concept, for engaging NGOs to implement
the project at the field level, and for guiding and monitoring them, and for overall
coordination and supervision of project implementation in the state. The WDCs
would be responsible for preparing annual and quarterly action plans and budgets,
quarterly and annual progress reports, and reimbursement claims for submission
to the CPSU/DWCD. For this work they would seek assistance from consultants,
especially to assess NGOs' training activities, and activities related to credit and
finance. The WDCs would interact with the CPSU to indicate their specific needs
by way of special studies to be initiated by the center. Feedback from the M&E
system would guide the focus of the supervision work on an on-going basis;
(c)   Other Agencies. Project supervision would benefit from the input provided by
participating NGOs. While operating as the key agencies forming SHGs and
interacting with communities at the grassroots levels, NGOs would assist in
supervision by providing timely feedback to WDCs on the performance of SHGs



-243-
Annex 16
in project activities, and on the views of SHG members. In addition, the project's
supervision work would benefit from close interaction between DWCD, IDA,
IFAD and the Inter-Agency Working Group (IAWG) on Gender and
Development, which meets in Delhi regularly and includes representatives of UN,
multilateral and bilateral agencies. Some of the activities considered by the
IAWG in support of project supervision include: (a) establishment of an advisory
or resource group for periodic sharing of experiences; (b) involvement in
monitoring and evaluating the project; and (c) advocacy and policy dialogue with
GOI on the basis of "lessons learned" from the project. DWCD and WDCs would
be encouraged to liaise closely with inmplementors of bilateral women's projects
to ensure exchange of information and experiences. In addition, wherever
appropriate, representatives from bilateral agencies with relevant experience
would be invited to participate in IDA supervision missions or undertake
review/studies on specific subjects of generic interest to all concerned.
(d)   IDA S3peiiqon. The core of IDA's supervision work would be the six-monthly
supervision missions. IDA would also have the responsibility to supervise the
project on behalf of IFAD but representative would join mission at regular
intervals. Important inputs for IDA missions would be the quarterly and annual
progress reports to be produced by WDCs and annual synthesis report on the
overall progress of the project to be produced by CPSU. Annex 16, Attachment I
provides a provisional plan for IDA's supervision work. The key elements of this
plan would be:
(i)    Mission Frequency. Bank supervision missions will visit the project
approximately every six months. If the project is launched in March 1997,
the first supervision mission following project launch workshop would be
around September 1997. However, due to the innovative nature of the
project, during the initial two years of the project it is anticipated that
during the initial years additional short missions would be required to
facilitate implementation and adoption of IDA's guidelines for
procurement, disbursement and reporting.
(ii)   Composition of the Mission. The missions will be lead by the Bank Task
Manager, and will include, as appropriate at the time, specialists in
institutional development, M&E, training, communications, social,
economic, financial, and procurement issues. Annex 16 Attachment 2
provides an estimated IDA supervision input over the duration of the
project. This attachment also lists the requirements taken n to account in
developing this list.
(iii)  Project Activities linked with Missions. During one of the supervision
missions each year, a workshop will be held with all key project
implementation agencies to review project progress in a participatory
manner. The supervision mission will be informed by discussions of
experience on the ground, constraints encountered, and needs expressed by



-244-
Annex 16
the project stakeholders for project redesign. These annual workshops
may also be linked with the annual work plans and budgets prepared by
the WDCs and CPSU.
Part C: Reporting and Reviews
3.    Reporting. The main building blocks for the reporting system of the project would be the
quarterly and annual progress reports to be produced by WDCs and CPSU with the help of their
respective M&E agencies. The annual report on the overall progress of the project to be
produced by CPSU would synthesize the findings of the reports submitted by the states and draw
lessons as appropriate for sharing with the main implementing agencies. The proposed Table of
Contents of the progress reports is given in Annex 16, Attachment I, which would finalized in
discussion with the implementing agencies. The proposed structure of the reports allows the
implementing agencies to provide: (i) an overview of progress and status of performance
indicators; (ii) information on the progress by category of expenditures; (iii) information on
project expenditure in relation to budget for each quarter and fiscal year; (iv) information on the
financial management; and (v) information on compliance with project's legal covenants.
4.    The Mid-Term Review. Approximately halfway through the project period a mid-term
review of the project progress would be carried out jointly by GOI and IDA. The CPSU and all
implementing agencies in the participating states, would submit progress reports to IDA for this
review. Thus, if the project is launched in early 1997, this would be approximately in September
1999. The principal aim of the mid-term review would be to determine whether there are any
major issues or problems in the project which necessitate rethinking the original project design
and making mid-course corrections. Feedback from the CPM/MIS will be utilized in this
process. It may also be used for a preliminary impact assessment of the project. A mid-term
review report will be submitted to IDA and discussed with the subsequent supervision mission.
It would highlight any major problems, issues, bottlenecks or delays in project execution, and
discuss the prospects for resolving them and completing the project on time.

Annex 17
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
PROCUREMENT AND DISBURSEMENT
National Competitive Bidding
1.    National Competitive Bidding (NCB) would be used for the procurement of computers
and major items of office equipment where they can be bulked together. This only applies to the
start-up phase of the project in each state. National Competitive Bidding requires: (i) publication
of Invitation for Bid (IFB) in newspapers with wide circulation within the country; (ii) use of the
Bank's standard bidding documents; (iii) sale of bidding documents to start only after publication
of the IFB; (iv) bidding period 30-60 days from the date of the start of sale of bidding
documents; and (v) single envelop system for submission of bids.
2.    The following steps are to be followed: (i) notification/advertising; (ii) issue of bidding
documents; (iii) submission of bids; (iv) public opening of bids; (v) evaluation of bids; (vi)
award in favor of lowest evaluated bidder who is determined to be qualified to perform the
contract satisfactorily; and (vii) no negotiations. The notification/advertisement and evaluation
of bids report would be reviewed by IDA.
3.    Prior review would be required for all contracts of US$100, 000 and above and would take
place at the following stages: (i) invitation to bid; (ii) bidding documents; (iii) bid evaluation
report; and (iv) final contracts.
4.    The schedule of the critical steps for national competitive bidding suggests about 34
weeks would normally be required from preparation of the specifications and bidding documents
to the delivery date. Accordingly, to ensure that required equipment and goods are on hand to
start the work program for a fiscal year that begins on April 1, procurement activities should
normally begin by the preceding August 15.
National Shopping
5.    National shopping procedures would be used to procure vehicles, office and other
equipment, furniture and materials in individual contracts not to exceed US$20, 000. Rate
contracts of the Directorate General of Supplies and Disposals (DGS&D), New Delhi, would
only be acceptable as a substitute for procurement under national shopping. State rate contracts
would not be acceptable but may feature as one of the quotations under the standard national
shopping procedures.
6.    The essential elements of national shopping are: (i) clear description and quantity of the
item(s), as well as a specified delivery date; (ii) price quotations obtained from several suppliers,



-250-
Annex 17
usually at least three to ensure competitive prices; (iii) evaluation of quotations to follow sound
public/private sector practices of the purchaser; and (iv) the purchase order placed with the
lowest evaluated supplier by suitably incorporating the terms of the accepted offer. Further
negotiations are not permitted after the lowest qualifying quotation has been evaluated.
7.    All documents associated with the purchase of goods under national shopping procedures
would be subject to selective post-review by visiting missions. Such reviews would concentrate
on the procedures used and the competitiveness of prices paid and physical inspections of the
relevant items.
8.    The schedule of the critical steps for national shopping suggests about 10 weeks would
normally be required from the preparation of the initial specifications to the delivery date.
Accordingly, to ensure that required equipment and goods are on hand to start the work program
for a fiscal year that begins on April 1, procurement activities should normally begin by the
preceding January 15.
Contractual Services Arrangements for Non-Government Organizations (NGOs)
and Consulting Services
9.    The project would seek NGO participation in the formation and nurturing of the Self-
Help Groups and provision of training for many of the project components. The WDCs would
invite detailed proposals from interested NGOs against a well-defined terms of reference
outlining the services to be provided. The WDCs would be responsible for reviewing the
proposals and awarding contracts. As far as possible, competition would be encouraged with a
number of NGOs invited to submit proposals. The selection of NGOs would be guided by the
criteria given in Annex 5.
10.   Consultancy services from private firms/organisations, large NGOs, autonomous
government agencies would be required for design and implementation of the M&E procedures,
development of strategy and support structure for the development of non-farm activities, WDC
management review, and special studies (techno-economic, product specific feasibility studies,
market studies).
11.   Both NGOs and consulting services would be procured in accordance with the Guidelines
on the Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers. Prior review is required by IDA for: (i)
Terms of Reference regardless of value; (ii) all single source selection of consulting firm; (iii) all
consultant contracts for assignment of a critical nature, regardless of value; and (iv) contracts for
consultancy services valued at equivalent of US$100,000 and above for consulting firms and
US$50,000 and above for individuals. For contracts falling within the thresholds, prior review
would be at the following stages: (i) Terms of Reference; (ii) shortlist: (iii) documents
containing the Letter of Invitation, Supplementary Information to Consultants and Conditions of
Contract; (iv) Evaluation Report of the technical proposals; (v) negotiated draft contract (with a
copy of the winning proposal) before signing of the contract; and (vi) conformed signed copy of
the final contract required in order to authorise disbursement against the contract.



-251-
Annex 17
12.   The schedule of critical steps for employment of small NGOs suggests about 14 weeks
would be required from the preparation of the initial terms of reference (TORs) until the key staff
are in post. Accordingly, in order to ensure that required services are on hand to start the work
prograrn for a fiscal year that begins on April 1, recruitment activities should begin by the
preceding December 15.
Contractual Services Arrangements for Individuals
13.   The project will require the services of a large number of individuals to provide a wide
range of support functions to project activities including design, technology and market linkage
assistance to women producers. Procedures for the employment of individuals are usually less
formal. IDA only requires prior approval of terms of reference and the qualifications and
conditions of employment before an agreement (or contract) is negotiated and signed. In such
cases, the major concern will be that the individual is suitably experienced and capable.
Community Construction
14.  In some instances the community itself may wish to undertake the construction of
community assets, such as gravity drinking water supply schemes, community halls, or sanitation
with appropriate technical guidance and supervision. Only simple constructions would be
suitable for this approach. The community would enter into a contract with the project authorities
to implement the works. The community would be required to constitute a Management
Committee with designated representatives (authorised to operate the bank account) and open an
account in the name of the Community for the sole purpose of the construction scheme. The
community would contract the design and preparation of construction drawings, specifications
and bill of quantities (to be approved by the relevant district technical officers) or these would be
made available by the district departments. The community would submit an estimated cost of
the works with a breakdown into labor and materials; name of the engineer who would be
responsible for technical supervision of construction; documentation of clear title to use the land
in the proposed manner and details of the recording and accounting system to be used to ensure
accountability for the funds. The contract agreement would stipulate details of stage payments.
An advance amount would be paid into the community account to enable materials to be
procured. Work would be inspected by the relevant district department prior to release of stage
payments.

Annex 18
INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
FINANCIAL AND ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. Financial Analysis
1.    The resource base in the project areas would enable a wide range of income generating
activities to be undertaken by the women. Since the women will be responsible for selecting
activities which are appropriate to their circumstances, only an indication of the activities likely
to feature in the lending program can be given. Financial models for a range of on-farm and non-
farmn activities have been prepared and are presented in Tables I to 23 for on-farm activities
covering crop production (paddy, wheat, sorghum, pulses, sunflower and chillies); horticulture
(vegetable production, banana, annual flowers, vegetable seedling production and fruit tree
nursery); livestock (goat rearing, kid and lamb fattening) and a range of production, trade and
services non-farm activities. The physical and financial parameters used in the models will vary
across the participating states and hence the financial models can only give a broad indication of
the expected financial returns. Many of the crop production activities represent up-grading of
existing technology through the use of improved varieties and higher amounts of fertilizer. The
horticulture activities represent ways of increasing the returns to small areas of land through
focusing on high value crops which women are ideally suited to manage. Small livestock
activities would be undertaken in small units (e.g. 3 lambs or 5 kids) as supplementary income
earning activities. Non-farm activities selected mainly represent low investment, high turnover
trading and service activities.
2.    The results of the financial analysis of these activities are summarized in Table 24 in
terms of incremental net income, return per manday and incremental benefit: cost ratio.
Incremental net incomes are variable as they cover both supplementary and full-time activities
but generally exceed Rs. 4,000 per annum. Return per manday is in excess of Rs. 30 with many
activities yielding returns of between Rs. 50 and 80/day. This compares with the average wage
for women in agricultural labor of around Rs. 15-20 per day with the exception of Haryana where
higher rates prevail. The incremental benefit:cost ratios are in excess of 1.5.
B. Economic Analysis
Approach
3.    Given the multi-faceted nature of the project, many of the benefits, particularly those
relating to social change, cannot readily be quantified. The quantifiable benefits relate to the
project's objective to increase women's economic independence through access to credit,
training and other technical support services to increase their involvement in income generating
activities. Even here, given the flexibility in the credit mechanism and the freedom of choice to
be exercised by the women in the selection of activities, the economic analysis of the project
requires more than the usual number of assumptions and therefore a high degree of
approximation. The major uncertainties concern loan uptake, the proportion of loans used for



-258-
Annex 18
social purposes, the mix of activities financed, and the extent to which project loans are replacing
existing more expensive sources of credit from the informal sector and hence are not generating
incremental economic benefits.
4.    Economic prices and costs. Economic import parity prices have been calculated based
on the World Bank Quarterly Review of Commodity Markets dated August 1996. World market
prices for internationally traded commodities are based on price projections for the year 2000 in
constant 1996 prices. Farmgate equivalent import parity prices have been calculated for rice,
wheat, sorghum, urea, phosphate and potash (Table 25). India is approaching self-sufficiency in
rice and wheat. However, India's high population growth (2.2% per year), as well as the high
income elasticity of demand for rice and other cerealsl justifies considering these commodities as
import substitutes. All other commodities have been considered as non-traded on the
international market. Their financial prices (at constant June 1996 prices) have been adjusted to
economic prices using a SCF of 0.9.
5.    Women in the rural areas are not able to find employment throughout the year and face
considerable underemployment or unemployment for at least 4 months of the year. This has
been reflected in the derivation of shadow wage rates for female labour. For seasonal
agricultural activities, it has been assumed that the requirement for labour coincides with times
when women are able to find employment and the financial wage rate of INR 20 per day has
been adjusted by the SCF to derive a shadow wage of INR 18/day. For livestock and non-farm
activities which are undertaken on a year-round basis, a factor of 0.66 has been applied to the
financial wage rate, adjusted by the SCF, giving an average shadow rate of female labour of INR
12/day. Financial and economic prices are summarized in Table 26.
6.    Methodology To obtain an approximate estimate of the project benefits, a multi-stage
approach incorporating various assumptions has been used. Firstly, economic models have been
prepared for a range of 23 typical income generating activities taken up by women covering
crops, livestock, and non-farm activities. The experience of the lending pattern of some existing
self-help groups indicates that crop and livestock loans are expected to represent around 85% of
lending and non-farm activities around 15%. On this basis, a weighted average net incremental
benefit and net incremental cost over the range of activities was derived. A weighted average
unit investment requirement was similarly calculated for the representative range of activities
giving an average unit investment of Rs. 4,100. This was used to derive the number of activities
which could be financed each year with the funds available for investment. For crop activities, a
hectare has been used as the unit of investment but women are likely to borrow for smaller areas
and hence the average loan size would be less.
7.    A number of assumptions have been used to indicate the volume of lending from the
groups each year which is likely to generate incremental economic benefits. The assumptions
used in the base case are as follows: (a) the quantum of bank credit accessed by an SHG
increases in relation to the equity base of the group and an increasing loan:savings ratio to reach
around Rs. 100,000 by the fifth year of a group's existence (see Annex 7, Attachment 2); (b) the
percentage of funds available to the group which are lent out each year increases from 90% to



-259-
Annex 18
95% by the fourth year of the group's operations; (c) the average rotation of investment funds is
initially twice per year when very short term consumption loans dominate the lending program
(some groups achieve up to four times), but reduces to 1.5 times as more loans are given for
productive purposes; (d) the percentage of funds lent out which generate incremental economic
benefits increases from 15% to 40% by the fourth year of operation - analysis of existing SHGs
indicates that initially the percentage of lending for consumption/social purposes ranges from
20%-70% but reduces over time whilst in the early stages a high proportion of loans for
productive purposes replace other more expensive sources of credit; (e) most of the lending
initially is for short term activities and many activities will initially require repeat loans to
sustain the activity - it is assumed that on average it will take three years for activities to become
self-financing;
8.    All the project costs for support for income generating activities have been included in
the analysis whilst 50% of the other support costs (capacity building of the SHGs and intervenors
and project management costs) are assumed to have a direct impact on the involvement of
women in income generating activities. The costs of the social programs have been excluded.
Economic costs have been estimated by deducting taxes and multiplying the financial base costs
plus physical contingencies by the SCF of 0.9.
Economic Results
9.    Overall project returns Analysed over ten years, the economic rate of return is
estimated to be 25% and the NPV at the opportunity cost of capital of 12% is estimated to be Rs.
319 million (US$ 7.4 million) as shown in Table 27.
10.   Sensitivity analysis A number of scenarios were tested to establish the economic
viability of the project. The results are presented below:
EIRR (%)           NPV
Rs. million
Base case                                    25                  335
Benefits decrease by 10%                      12                 (10)
Activity costs increase by 10%                15                 70
Support costs increase by 10%                23                  288
Max. 50% of funds generate increm econ benefits   26             367
Max 30% of funds generate increm econ benefits   21              196
1 year lag in investment activities          19                  100
11.   Switching values indicate that the project benefits could decrease by 10% for the NPV to
fall to zero whilst project activity costs could increase by 13%. The percentage of funds
generating incremental economic benefits could fall to 20%. This indicates that the project is
more sensitive to the performance of the investment activities financed as these impact
individually on the cost and benefit streams than to variations in the assumptions on the overall
pattern of lending which impact on both costs and benefits simultaneously.

Annex 18

13.   The costs incurred under the project to generate these benefits can be categorised into the
direct expenditure on capacity building of the SHGs and development of the SHG members and
the indirect costs of strengthening the participating agencies. The latter would be expected over
time to benefit a wider clientele than the members of the SHGs formed under the project. The
indicative breakdown of costs over the life of the project is given below on a per group basis:

-262-
Annex 18
Public Sector Role and Fiscal Impact
13.   Public Sector and Non-governmental Roles. The project designs allocates substantial
responsibilities and resources to non-governmental entities and supplies a small amount of
supplemental resources directly to government in order to build capacity. Of the total project
costs, about US$ 19 million (36 percent) would flow to the SHGs and US$ 25 million (47%) to
NGOs, academic institutions and other contracted specialists. Project expenditures flowing to
government would comprise about US$ 9.0 million (17% of total project costs) for goods, travel,
salaries of incremental staff and operation and maintenance.
14.   Cost recovery. Whilst in the initial phase of the program no specific cost recovery
measures are proposed, it is intended to examine opportunities for partial recovery of the costs of
some services during project implementation. Experience from on-going NGO programs
suggests that SHG members are increasingly willing to pay for services which deliver tangible
benefits. In this context, the project will promote, on a pilot basis, the formation of clusters and
associations of SHGs. This experiment will test the ability of these bodies to take over many of
the NGO support services to SHGs and possibly some of the technical support services provided
under the project, on a self-financing basis.
15.   Fiscal impact. Given the pilot nature of the project, it represents a very small financial
burden and the overall impact on the state and/or central government budgets will be negligible.
All incremental staff would not be additional to the establishment but would be hired on contract
or would come on deputation from government departments where their posts would be frozen.
All contracts can be discontinued at the end of the project. The project incorporates a defined
exit strategy for intervenors. Support to SHGs by NGOs would be limited to four years. By the
end of the project, the mature SHGs would have taken over the responsibility for managing their
affairs from the NGOs either on an individual group basis or through the formation of clusters
and associations of clusters. Phasing of the process of group formation, however, means that
around 60% of the SHGs may be less than four years old by the end of the project period. Whilst
some of these may be integrated into clusters and associations for receipt of on-going support,
others would still require support directly from the NGOs. The total cost of NGO support
outside of the project period would be around Rs. 28 million (US$ 0.8 million) over three years.
16.   Sustainability. A major thrust of the project is capacity building of the SHGs so that
they are capable of managing their own affairs and are able to further their own social and
economic development by virtue of the awareness and linkages established under the project
with lending institutions and technical and social support agencies. Most of the SHGs would be
linked to the banks and the expected high repayment rates and reduction in the banks' transaction
costs should ensure a continuing flow of credit to the groups. In addition, the formation of
clusters of SHGs and cluster associations is expected to take place and these, over time, will take
over many of the support functions hitherto provided by the NGOs whilst also playing a vital
role in the wider replication of the SHG concept. Building the capacity of mature SHGs to
promote the formation of additional SHGs not only strengthens the existing groups and furthers



-263-
Annex 18
their sustainability but contributes to a more cost-effective and sustainable approach to the
formation of SHGs, with an anticipated reduction in direct NGO support costs of almost 50%.

INDIA
RURAL WOMEN'S DEVELOPMENT AND EMPOWERMENT PROJECT
DOCUMENTS AVAILABLE IN PROJECT FILE
I.    Draft Project Proposals from the State Women's Development Corporations: Bihar, Gujarat,
Haryana, Karnataka, Madhya Pradesh, and Uttar Pradesh.
2.    IFAD's Project Formulation Report -  Volume I: Main Text and Annexes
Volume II: Working Papers
3.    IFAD's Assisted Tamil Nadu Women's Development Project (240-IN): Mid-Term
Evaluation Report No. 0585-IN, March 1955.
4.     The MYRADA Experience: Alternative Management Systems for Savings and Credit of the
Rural Poor by Aloysius P. Fernandez, 1992.
5.    Role of Women's Development Corporations. Study undertaken by Sarohini Kumtakar Council
for Social Development, New Delhi, January 1994. Work undertaken for DANIDA assisted
program of the Women's Division of the National Institute of Public Cooperation and Child
Development, New Delhi.
6.    Transition Costs of Lending to the Rural Poor: Non-Governmental Organizations and Self
Help Groups of the poor as intermediaries for banks in India. Work undertaken by V.
Puhazenshi, The Foundation for Development Cooperation ---, Brisbane, Australia, in
collaboration with the National Bank for Agriculture and Rural Development (NABARD),
1994.
7.    Organizing Savings and Credit Groups for Poor Women - A field manual produced by
Women's World Banking, Ahmedabad, India 1994.
8.    Linking Self Help Groups with Banks -An Indian Experience, NABARD, May 1995.
9.    Community Financial Management System of Community Development Society (CDS)
Developed by NABARD, Thiravananthapuram, Kerala in Cooperation with Local
Administration Department, Government of Kerala, 1995.
10.   Impact Study on IFAD Assisted Women's Development Project. Prepared by Indian Bank,
Madras, December 1995.
11.   Self Help Group Clusters and Associations of Clusters. Report on a study undertaken by
Sudha Kothari, Chaitnaya, Pune, Maharashtra, September 1996.
12.   Women's Participation in Land-based Activities. Study undertaken by Aasha Mehta, Indian
Administrative Institute, New Delhi, May 1996.
13.   Credit Financing. Study undertaken by S.C. Wadhwa, Agricultural Finance Corporation,
Bombay, Maharashtra, May 1996.
